--- Lesson 6.5:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: DNA and RNA
Key Concepts: DNA and RNA

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of dna and rna in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to dna and rna
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 6.5:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is DNA, and what are the three components of each nucleotide that make up this molecule?
    [ ] DNA is a carbohydrate polymer made of glucose monomers linked by glycosidic bonds in a single chain
    [*] DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) is a double-stranded helical polymer of nucleotides — each nucleotide consists of (1) a deoxyribose sugar, (2) a phosphate group, and (3) one of four nitrogenous bases: adenine (A), thymine (T), guanine (G), or cytosine (C) — which stores the genetic instructions for building and maintaining all living organisms
    [ ] DNA is a protein composed of amino acids folded into a double helix that functions as an enzyme
    [ ] DNA is a lipid bilayer that forms the cell membrane and is composed of fatty acids and cholesterol
    Explanation: DNA structure: (1) Discovered as 'nuclein' by Friedrich Miescher (1869) from white blood cell nuclei. (2) Structure determined by Watson & Crick (1953), using Rosalind Franklin's X-ray diffraction Photo 51 and Chargaff's base ratios. (3) Each nucleotide has: deoxyribose sugar (pentose missing –OH at 2′ carbon — more chemically stable than ribose), phosphate group (gives DNA its acidic character and negative charge), and a nitrogenous base (purines: A, G — double ring; pyrimidines: T, C — single ring). (4) Nucleotides link via phosphodiester bonds (3′-OH to 5′-phosphate) forming the sugar-phosphate backbone. (5) Two antiparallel strands (5′→3′ and 3′→5′) twist into a right-handed double helix with ~10 bp per turn, 3.4 nm pitch, 2 nm diameter. (6) Human genome: ~3.2 billion base pairs on 23 chromosome pairs, encoding ~20,000 protein-coding genes (~1.5% of total DNA).

  Q2. How does complementary base pairing work in DNA, and why is it essential for DNA's function?
    [ ] Base pairing is random — any base can pair with any other base, and there are no specific rules governing which bases bond together
    [*] Adenine (A) pairs with thymine (T) through 2 hydrogen bonds, and guanine (G) pairs with cytosine (C) through 3 hydrogen bonds — this complementarity means each strand contains all the information needed to reconstruct the other, enabling accurate DNA replication, transcription, and repair
    [ ] Adenine always pairs with guanine, and cytosine always pairs with thymine through ionic bonds
    [ ] Base pairing only occurs in RNA, not in DNA — DNA strands are held together by covalent bonds between all four bases
    Explanation: Complementary base pairing: (1) Chargaff's rules (1950): in any organism's DNA, %A = %T and %G = %C. This was a crucial clue for Watson & Crick. (2) Chemical basis: purines (2 rings: A, G) always pair with pyrimidines (1 ring: T, C), maintaining constant 2 nm helix width. A=T: 2 hydrogen bonds; G≡C: 3 hydrogen bonds (GC-rich DNA is more thermally stable — higher melting temperature Tm). (3) Functional importance: DNA replication — each strand serves as template; DNA polymerase reads 3′→5′, synthesizes 5′→3′, adding complementary nucleotides. Transcription — RNA polymerase uses the template strand to synthesize complementary mRNA. DNA repair — mismatch repair enzymes can identify errors by comparing to the complementary strand. (4) The base stacking interactions (van der Waals forces between flat aromatic rings) contribute more to helix stability than H-bonds. (5) Exception: RNA base pairing uses A-U instead of A-T (uracil replaces thymine).

  Q3. What are the key structural and functional differences between DNA and RNA?
    [ ] DNA and RNA are identical molecules with the same sugar, bases, and structure — they are completely interchangeable
    [*] DNA has deoxyribose sugar, is double-stranded, uses thymine (T), and serves as the permanent genetic storage molecule — while RNA has ribose sugar, is typically single-stranded, uses uracil (U) instead of thymine, and functions in protein synthesis (mRNA, tRNA, rRNA) and gene regulation
    [ ] DNA is single-stranded and found in the cytoplasm, while RNA is double-stranded and found only in the nucleus
    [ ] DNA contains amino acids while RNA contains fatty acids — they belong to different macromolecule classes
    Explanation: DNA vs RNA comparison: (1) Sugar: DNA = deoxyribose (missing –OH at 2′ → more chemically stable); RNA = ribose (has –OH at 2′ → more reactive, shorter-lived). (2) Strands: DNA = double-stranded (except some viruses); RNA = single-stranded (but can fold into complex 3D structures — tRNA cloverleaf, ribosomal RNA). (3) Bases: DNA = A, T, G, C; RNA = A, U, G, C (uracil lacks the methyl group present on thymine). (4) Function: DNA = long-term genetic storage and transmission; RNA = multiple roles. (5) RNA types: mRNA (messenger — carries gene code from nucleus to ribosome), tRNA (transfer — carries amino acids, has anticodon loop), rRNA (ribosomal — structural/catalytic component of ribosomes; 80% of cellular RNA), plus regulatory RNAs: miRNA, siRNA (gene silencing), lncRNA, snRNA (splicing), CRISPR guide RNA. (6) Location: DNA primarily in nucleus (also mitochondria, chloroplasts); RNA made in nucleus, functions mainly in cytoplasm.

  Q4. What are the three main types of RNA involved in protein synthesis, and what is each one's specific role?
    [ ] There is only one type of RNA, and it performs all functions related to protein synthesis by itself
    [*] mRNA (messenger RNA) carries the genetic code from DNA to the ribosome as a sequence of codons; tRNA (transfer RNA) delivers specific amino acids to the ribosome by matching its anticodon to the mRNA codon; rRNA (ribosomal RNA) forms the structural and catalytic core of the ribosome where protein synthesis occurs
    [ ] The three types are DNA-RNA, protein-RNA, and lipid-RNA — each binds to a different macromolecule class
    [ ] mRNA stores genetic information permanently, tRNA replicates DNA, and rRNA digests proteins — they have no role in building proteins
    Explanation: The three RNA types in translation: (1) mRNA (messenger RNA) — transcribed from DNA by RNA polymerase in the nucleus; carries genetic instructions as a series of 3-nucleotide codons (4³ = 64 possible codons); processed (5′ cap, poly-A tail, intron splicing) before export to cytoplasm; relatively short-lived (minutes to hours in bacteria, hours to days in eukaryotes). (2) tRNA (transfer RNA) — small (~76-90 nucleotides); cloverleaf 2D structure, L-shaped 3D; carries specific amino acid (attached by aminoacyl-tRNA synthetase — 20 specific enzymes, one per amino acid); anticodon (3 bases) pairs with mRNA codon via complementary base pairing (wobble at 3rd position allows some flexibility). (3) rRNA (ribosomal RNA) — most abundant RNA (~80% of cellular RNA); structural and catalytic component of ribosomes; human ribosome: 80S (60S large subunit with 28S, 5.8S, 5S rRNA + ~49 proteins; 40S small subunit with 18S rRNA + ~33 proteins); peptidyl transferase activity is catalyzed by 23S/28S rRNA (a ribozyme).

  Q5. What is the central dogma of molecular biology, and how does genetic information flow from DNA to protein?
    [ ] The central dogma states that proteins make RNA which then makes DNA — the flow is Protein → RNA → DNA
    [*] The central dogma (Francis Crick, 1958) describes the flow of genetic information: DNA → (transcription) → RNA → (translation) → Protein — DNA is transcribed into mRNA by RNA polymerase, then mRNA is translated into a specific amino acid sequence (protein) by ribosomes using the genetic code (triplet codons)
    [ ] The central dogma states that genetic information flows randomly in any direction between DNA, RNA, and protein
    [ ] The central dogma only applies to prokaryotes — eukaryotes use a completely different system for making proteins
    Explanation: The central dogma: (1) DNA → RNA (transcription): RNA polymerase binds to a promoter region, unwinds DNA, reads the template strand 3′→5′, synthesizes mRNA 5′→3′ using complementary base pairing (A→U, T→A, G→C, C→G). In eukaryotes: pre-mRNA is processed (5′ 7-methylguanosine cap, 3′ poly-A tail of ~200 adenines, intron removal by spliceosome). (2) RNA → Protein (translation): ribosome binds mRNA at start codon (AUG = methionine). tRNAs deliver amino acids matching each codon. Peptide bonds form between amino acids. Translation continues until a stop codon (UAA, UAG, UGA). (3) Exceptions to simple central dogma: reverse transcription (RNA → DNA, by retroviruses like HIV, using reverse transcriptase — discovered by Temin & Baltimore, Nobel 1975); RNA replication (RNA → RNA, by RNA viruses); prions (protein → protein misfolding, no nucleic acid). (4) The genetic code is nearly universal across all life — strong evidence for common ancestry.

  Q6. What is the genetic code, and how do codons specify amino acids during translation?
    [ ] The genetic code uses individual nucleotides — each single base specifies one amino acid, giving only 4 possible amino acids
    [*] The genetic code is a triplet code — each three-nucleotide sequence on mRNA (codon) specifies one of 20 amino acids or a stop signal — with 64 possible codons (4³), making it degenerate (redundant: most amino acids have 2-6 codons), nearly universal across all life forms, and non-overlapping
    [ ] The genetic code uses pairs of nucleotides — each two-base sequence codes for one amino acid, giving 16 possible amino acids
    [ ] The genetic code is different for every species — human codons specify completely different amino acids than bacterial codons
    Explanation: The genetic code: (1) Triplet nature — proven by Crick, Brenner, and colleagues (1961) using frameshift mutations in bacteriophage T4. (2) 64 codons total: 61 code for amino acids + 3 stop codons (UAA 'ochre', UAG 'amber', UGA 'opal'). AUG = start codon (methionine). (3) Degeneracy — most amino acids have multiple codons (e.g., leucine has 6: UUA, UUG, CUU, CUC, CUA, CUG). Wobble hypothesis (Crick, 1966): 3rd codon position is more flexible in base pairing. (4) Deciphered by Nirenberg & Matthaei (1961: poly-U → polyphenylalanine), Khorana (synthetic mRNA work) — shared Nobel 1968 with Holley (tRNA structure). (5) Nearly universal — same code from E. coli to humans (evidence for common ancestor). Minor variations in mitochondria and some ciliate protists. (6) Non-overlapping and comma-free — read in consecutive, non-overlapping triplets from a fixed start point. Frameshift mutations (insertion/deletion) scramble all downstream codons.

  Q7. What is DNA replication, and how does it ensure accurate copying of the genetic information?
    [ ] DNA replication creates a completely new DNA molecule from scratch — the old DNA is discarded and replaced
    [*] DNA replication is semiconservative — the double helix unwinds and each original strand serves as a template for a new complementary strand, producing two identical DNA molecules each containing one original and one new strand — with accuracy ensured by complementary base pairing, DNA polymerase proofreading (3′→5′ exonuclease), and post-replication mismatch repair
    [ ] DNA replication occurs by splitting the double helix lengthwise, and each half spontaneously regenerates into a full chromosome without any enzymes
    [ ] DNA replication only occurs in bacteria — eukaryotic cells use a different mechanism that does not involve template strands
    Explanation: DNA replication details: (1) Semiconservative — confirmed by Meselson-Stahl experiment (1958) using ¹⁵N-labeled DNA and CsCl density gradient centrifugation. Each daughter molecule has one parental strand + one new strand. (2) Key enzymes: helicase (unwinds double helix at replication fork), topoisomerase/gyrase (relieves tension ahead of fork), primase (makes short RNA primer), DNA polymerase III (main replicase, synthesizes 5′→3′, proofreads 3′→5′), DNA polymerase I (replaces RNA primers with DNA), ligase (seals Okazaki fragments on lagging strand), SSB proteins (stabilize single strands). (3) Leading strand: continuous synthesis toward fork. Lagging strand: discontinuous (Okazaki fragments ~100-200 nt in eukaryotes, ~1000-2000 in prokaryotes). (4) Accuracy: base selection (~10⁻⁵ error rate) + proofreading (~10⁻² improvement) + mismatch repair (~10⁻³ improvement) = final error rate ~10⁻¹⁰ per base per replication. (5) Speed: ~1,000 nt/sec in bacteria (1 origin), ~50 nt/sec in humans (multiple origins per chromosome — essential for replicating 3.2 billion bp in ~8 hours).

  Q8. What are mutations, and how can changes in DNA sequence affect an organism?
    [ ] Mutations are always lethal and are immediately eliminated from the population — no beneficial mutations exist
    [*] Mutations are permanent changes in DNA nucleotide sequence — they can be point mutations (substitutions: silent, missense, or nonsense), insertions, deletions, or chromosomal rearrangements — with effects ranging from harmless (silent) to beneficial (sickle cell carrier malaria resistance) to harmful (cancer-causing oncogene activation) depending on what protein is affected and how
    [ ] Mutations only occur in reproductive cells and never affect the organism that carries them
    [ ] Mutations cannot be caused by environmental factors — they only occur randomly during DNA replication
    Explanation: Mutation types and effects: (1) Point mutations (single nucleotide changes): Silent — new codon specifies same amino acid (no effect due to code degeneracy); Missense — new codon specifies different amino acid (e.g., sickle cell: GAG→GTG, Glu→Val); Nonsense — new codon = stop codon → truncated protein (often loss of function). (2) Frameshift mutations — insertion or deletion of nucleotides (not multiples of 3) shifts the reading frame → all downstream codons are different → usually nonfunctional protein. (3) Causes: spontaneous (replication errors: ~10⁻¹⁰/base/replication after repair); induced by mutagens — UV radiation (thymine dimers), chemicals (alkylating agents, base analogs), reactive oxygen species. (4) Somatic mutations — affect only the individual (not inherited; basis of cancer). Germline mutations — inherited by offspring. (5) Evolutionary significance — mutations are the ultimate source of all genetic variation; most are neutral, some harmful, rare ones beneficial (e.g., CCR5-Δ32 deletion → HIV resistance).

  Q9. What is Chargaff's rule, and how did it contribute to understanding DNA structure?
    [ ] Chargaff's rule states that all four DNA bases are present in equal amounts — 25% each of A, T, G, and C in every organism
    [*] Chargaff's rule (Erwin Chargaff, 1950) states that in any organism's DNA, the amount of adenine equals thymine (A = T) and the amount of guanine equals cytosine (G = C) — this 1:1 ratio was a crucial clue that bases pair specifically across the double helix (A with T, G with C), which Watson and Crick used to build their structural model
    [ ] Chargaff's rule states that DNA always contains more purines than pyrimidines, with A + G always exceeding T + C
    [ ] Chargaff's rule only applies to RNA and has no relevance to DNA structure or base pairing
    Explanation: Chargaff's rules and their impact: (1) First rule: %A = %T and %G = %C in double-stranded DNA from any species. This means total purines = total pyrimidines (%A + %G = %T + %C = 50%). (2) Second rule: %A + %T ≠ 50% necessarily (varies between species: humans ~30% GC, M. tuberculosis ~66% GC). The AT/GC ratio is species-specific and was used as a taxonomic tool. (3) Why it matters: Chargaff's data contradicted the earlier 'tetranucleotide hypothesis' (which proposed equal amounts of all 4 bases, making DNA too 'boring' to carry genetic information). The 1:1 A-T and G-C ratios strongly suggested specific base pairing. (4) Watson and Crick realized: if A=T and G=C, then A pairs with T and G pairs with C across the helix. Purine (2 rings) always pairs with pyrimidine (1 ring) → constant 2 nm width. (5) Note: Chargaff's first rule does NOT apply to single-stranded DNA or RNA (no complementary strand to force equal ratios).

  Q10. What role did Rosalind Franklin play in the discovery of DNA's structure, and what did her X-ray diffraction data reveal?
    [ ] Rosalind Franklin had no involvement in DNA research — she studied protein crystals exclusively
    [*] Rosalind Franklin produced 'Photo 51' — an X-ray diffraction image of crystalline B-form DNA that revealed the molecule was helical, had a 3.4 nm pitch (repeat distance), contained ~10 base pairs per turn, and had a 2 nm diameter — this data was critical for Watson and Crick to build their correct double helix model in 1953
    [ ] Rosalind Franklin discovered the four nitrogenous bases in DNA but did not contribute to understanding its overall structure
    [ ] Rosalind Franklin used electron microscopy to directly photograph individual DNA molecules, showing they were straight, unbranched rods
    Explanation: Rosalind Franklin's contributions: (1) Photo 51 (May 1952, taken by Raymond Gosling under Franklin's supervision at King's College London) — clearly showed: X-shaped diffraction pattern indicating helical structure; layer lines at 3.4 nm spacing (distance between base pairs × bases per turn); absence of 4th layer line suggesting 2-fold symmetry (compatible with antiparallel strands); 2 nm diameter. (2) Franklin also determined B-form DNA's water content and that the phosphate groups were on the outside (crucial constraint for modeling). (3) Controversy — Maurice Wilkins showed Photo 51 to Watson without Franklin's knowledge or consent in January 1953. Watson immediately recognized the helix parameters. (4) Watson and Crick's Nature paper (April 1953) acknowledged Franklin's work only minimally. (5) Nobel Prize (1962) — awarded to Watson, Crick, and Wilkins. Franklin died of ovarian cancer in 1958 (age 37), possibly from X-ray exposure. Nobel rules at the time did not allow posthumous awards. Her contribution is now widely recognized as essential.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid)?
    [ ] A single-stranded nucleic acid that carries genetic instructions from the nucleus to ribosomes for protein synthesis
    [*] A double-stranded nucleic acid composed of nucleotides containing deoxyribose sugar, phosphate groups, and four nitrogenous bases (A, T, G, C), arranged in an antiparallel double helix that stores and transmits hereditary genetic information in all cellular organisms
    [ ] A type of protein found in the nucleus that controls gene expression by binding to chromosomes during cell division
    [ ] A carbohydrate polymer that provides structural support for chromosomes within the nuclear envelope
    Explanation: DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) — the molecule of heredity: (1) Structure: double helix of two antiparallel polynucleotide strands — each nucleotide = deoxyribose sugar + phosphate group + one of four nitrogenous bases: adenine (A), thymine (T), guanine (G), cytosine (C). (2) Base pairing: A=T (2 hydrogen bonds), G≡C (3 hydrogen bonds) → Chargaff's rules. (3) Discovery timeline: Friedrich Miescher isolated 'nuclein' (1869) → Avery-MacLeod-McCarty showed DNA = transforming principle (1944) → Hershey-Chase confirmed DNA = genetic material using ³²P/³⁵S bacteriophage (1952) → Watson-Crick model (1953, using Franklin's Photo 51). (4) Dimensions: 3.4 nm per full turn, 0.34 nm between bases, ~10 bp/turn, 2 nm diameter. Major and minor grooves allow protein binding. (5) Human genome: 3.2 billion bp, ~20,000-25,000 protein-coding genes (only ~1.5% of total DNA; rest includes regulatory sequences, introns, repetitive elements, transposons). (6) Functions: stores genetic information, replicates for cell division, serves as template for RNA transcription, can be repaired by multiple enzyme systems.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes RNA (ribonucleic acid)?
    [ ] A double-stranded nucleic acid that permanently stores genetic information in the nucleus and never leaves the chromosome
    [*] A typically single-stranded nucleic acid composed of nucleotides containing ribose sugar, phosphate groups, and four nitrogenous bases (A, U, G, C), which functions in gene expression by carrying genetic messages (mRNA), delivering amino acids (tRNA), and forming ribosomal structure (rRNA)
    [ ] A lipid-based molecule that helps transport proteins across cell membranes during secretion
    [ ] A modified form of DNA that exists only in viruses and has no role in normal cellular function
    Explanation: RNA (ribonucleic acid) — the versatile nucleic acid: (1) Structure: typically single-stranded (can form secondary structures: hairpins, stem-loops, pseudoknots). Nucleotide = ribose sugar (has -OH at 2′ position, making RNA less stable than DNA) + phosphate + base (A, U, G, C — uracil replaces thymine). (2) Three classic types: mRNA (messenger — carries genetic code from DNA to ribosomes; 1-5% of total RNA), tRNA (transfer — adapter molecule, delivers amino acids; cloverleaf shape with anticodon loop; ~15% of total RNA), rRNA (ribosomal — structural/catalytic component of ribosomes; ~80% of total RNA; ribosomes = ribozymes). (3) Additional types discovered since 1990s: miRNA (microRNA — regulates gene expression by silencing mRNA, ~22 nt), siRNA (small interfering RNA — RNA interference/gene silencing), snRNA (small nuclear RNA — splicing), lncRNA (long noncoding RNA — diverse regulatory roles). (4) RNA world hypothesis (Gilbert, 1986): RNA may have been the first self-replicating molecule — it can store information AND catalyze reactions (ribozymes — Cech & Altman, Nobel 1989). (5) mRNA vaccines (COVID-19 Pfizer/Moderna) — synthetic mRNA delivered to cells to produce viral spike protein → immune response.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes a nucleotide?
    [ ] A complete gene sequence that codes for one specific protein in the cell
    [*] The monomer (building block) of nucleic acids, consisting of three components: a five-carbon sugar (deoxyribose in DNA, ribose in RNA), a phosphate group, and a nitrogenous base (purine: A or G; pyrimidine: C, T, or U) — linked together by phosphodiester bonds to form polynucleotide chains
    [ ] An amino acid that is incorporated into the structure of DNA during replication
    [ ] A type of enzyme that catalyzes the breaking of hydrogen bonds between DNA strands
    Explanation: Nucleotide — the monomer of DNA and RNA: (1) Three components: pentose sugar (5-carbon: deoxyribose in DNA lacks -OH at 2′ carbon; ribose in RNA has -OH at 2′) + phosphate group (negatively charged, gives DNA/RNA their acidic properties) + nitrogenous base. (2) Bases: Purines (2 rings) = adenine (A) and guanine (G); Pyrimidines (1 ring) = cytosine (C), thymine (T, DNA only), uracil (U, RNA only). Mnemonic: 'Pure As Gold' (purines A, G). (3) Numbering: sugar carbons numbered 1′-5′ (prime). Base attaches at 1′ carbon (glycosidic bond). Phosphate at 5′. Next nucleotide's 3′ -OH links to phosphate → phosphodiester bond. (4) Directionality: 5′→3′ (phosphate→hydroxyl). DNA polymerase adds nucleotides only at 3′ end. (5) Beyond DNA/RNA: nucleotides serve other vital cellular functions — ATP (adenosine triphosphate = energy currency), GTP (signaling, energy), cAMP (second messenger), NAD⁺/FAD (electron carriers in metabolism), coenzyme A (has adenine nucleotide component).

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes complementary base pairing?
    [ ] The random association of any nucleotide base with any other base, determined solely by physical proximity during DNA replication
    [*] The specific hydrogen bonding between nitrogenous bases in nucleic acids — adenine pairs with thymine (A=T, 2 H-bonds) in DNA or with uracil (A=U) in RNA, and guanine pairs with cytosine (G≡C, 3 H-bonds) — ensuring accurate DNA replication, transcription, and the structural stability of the double helix
    [ ] The covalent bonding between sugar and phosphate groups that forms the backbone of a DNA strand
    [ ] The ionic attraction between positively charged histone proteins and negatively charged DNA in chromosomes
    Explanation: Complementary base pairing — the foundation of molecular genetics: (1) Specific pairing rules: A=T (or A=U in RNA) connected by 2 hydrogen bonds; G≡C connected by 3 hydrogen bonds. Purine (2 rings) always pairs with pyrimidine (1 ring) → constant helix width of 2 nm. (2) Why these specific pairings? Hydrogen bond donor/acceptor patterns: A has H-bond donor at N6 + acceptor at N1 → matches T's acceptor at O4 + donor at N3. G has donor at N1 + N2 + acceptor at O6 → matches C's acceptor at N3 + O2 + donor at N4. (3) Consequences: G-C rich regions are more thermally stable (3 H-bonds > 2). DNA melting temperature (Tm) correlates with GC content. (4) Applications: DNA replication (each strand templates its complement), transcription (DNA→mRNA), tRNA anticodon-mRNA codon pairing during translation, DNA probe hybridization, PCR primer annealing, CRISPR guide RNA targeting. (5) Chargaff's rules are a direct consequence: in dsDNA, %A = %T and %G = %C. (6) Wobble base pairing (Crick, 1966) — flexibility at 3rd codon position allows inosine in tRNA to pair with U, C, or A.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes the double helix?
    [ ] A flat, ladder-like structure where two parallel DNA strands lie side by side without any twisting
    [*] The three-dimensional structure of DNA — two antiparallel polynucleotide strands wound around each other in a right-handed spiral, with sugar-phosphate backbones on the outside and complementary base pairs stacked on the inside, connected by hydrogen bonds — first described by Watson and Crick in 1953
    [ ] A single-stranded RNA molecule that folds back on itself to form a helical hairpin during translation
    [ ] The coiled structure of a protein's alpha-helix secondary structure stabilized by peptide bonds
    Explanation: The double helix — DNA's iconic structure: (1) Watson-Crick model (April 25, 1953, Nature): two antiparallel strands (one 5′→3′, other 3′→5′) wound into a right-handed helix (B-form DNA, the predominant form under physiological conditions). (2) Dimensions: 3.4 nm pitch (per complete turn), 0.34 nm rise per base pair, ~10 bp per turn, 2 nm diameter. (3) Sugar-phosphate backbones on outside (hydrophilic, negatively charged). Bases stacked inside (hydrophobic stacking interactions contribute significantly to stability, in addition to H-bonds). (4) Major groove (~22 Å wide) and minor groove (~12 Å wide) — proteins (transcription factors, restriction enzymes) read DNA sequence through groove contacts without unwinding the helix. (5) Other DNA forms: A-form (shorter, wider, 11 bp/turn — dehydrated conditions, RNA-DNA hybrids); Z-form (left-handed helix, discovered by Alexander Rich, 1979 — may play roles in gene regulation). (6) The model's key insight: the structure immediately suggested a mechanism for replication — 'It has not escaped our notice that the specific pairing we have postulated immediately suggests a possible copying mechanism for the genetic material' (Watson & Crick, 1953).

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes a codon?
    [ ] A single nucleotide within DNA that directly bonds to one amino acid during protein synthesis
    [*] A sequence of three consecutive nucleotides (triplet) on mRNA that specifies a particular amino acid or a stop signal during translation — with 64 possible codons encoding 20 amino acids plus 3 stop signals, making the genetic code degenerate (redundant) and nearly universal across all life
    [ ] A large segment of DNA containing thousands of base pairs that constitutes an entire gene
    [ ] The hydrogen bond between two complementary bases on opposite DNA strands
    Explanation: Codon — the language unit of the genetic code: (1) Three consecutive mRNA nucleotides = 1 codon → specifies 1 amino acid (or stop). 4³ = 64 possible codons. (2) Start codon: AUG (methionine in eukaryotes, formyl-methionine in prokaryotes). Stop codons: UAA ('ochre'), UAG ('amber'), UGA ('opal') — recognized by release factors, not tRNAs. (3) Degeneracy: most amino acids encoded by 2-6 codons (e.g., Ser has 6 codons: UCU, UCC, UCA, UCG, AGU, AGC; Met and Trp each have only 1 codon). Third position 'wobble' (Crick, 1966) accounts for most synonymous codons. (4) Codon usage bias: organisms preferentially use certain synonymous codons based on tRNA abundance → affects translation efficiency. (5) Reading frame: set by AUG start codon; no punctuation between codons → frameshift mutations (ins/del not ×3) change all downstream amino acids. (6) The genetic code was deciphered primarily by Nirenberg-Matthaei (1961: poly-U → poly-Phe), Nirenberg-Leder (1964: triplet binding assay), and Khorana (synthetic mRNAs) — shared Nobel 1968.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes transcription?
    [ ] The process by which ribosomes read mRNA codons and assemble amino acids into a polypeptide chain
    [*] The process by which the enzyme RNA polymerase reads a DNA template strand (3′→5′) and synthesizes a complementary mRNA molecule (5′→3′) — occurring in the nucleus of eukaryotes — as the first step of gene expression in the central dogma (DNA → RNA → Protein)
    [ ] The copying of an entire DNA molecule during S phase of the cell cycle to produce two identical chromosomes
    [ ] The transport of finished proteins from the rough ER through the Golgi apparatus to the cell membrane
    Explanation: Transcription — DNA to RNA: (1) Three stages: Initiation — RNA polymerase binds promoter (TATA box in eukaryotes, -10/-35 sequences in prokaryotes) with help of transcription factors (TFIID, TFIIB, etc.); Elongation — RNA pol reads template strand 3′→5′, synthesizes mRNA 5′→3′, adding complementary ribonucleotides (A→U, T→A, G→C, C→G); Termination — poly-A signal (AAUAAA) in eukaryotes, rho-dependent or rho-independent terminators in prokaryotes. (2) Eukaryotic mRNA processing (before export from nucleus): 5′ cap (7-methylguanosine — protects from degradation, aids ribosome recognition), 3′ poly-A tail (100-250 adenines — stability, export), splicing (introns removed by spliceosome — a large RNA-protein complex with snRNAs). (3) Alternative splicing — one gene → multiple proteins (human average: ~3 mRNA variants per gene; Drosophila DSCAM gene: 38,016 possible variants). (4) RNA polymerase types in eukaryotes: RNA Pol I (rRNA), RNA Pol II (mRNA, most snRNAs), RNA Pol III (tRNA, 5S rRNA). Prokaryotes have one RNA polymerase (core enzyme: α₂ββ′ω + σ factor).

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes translation?
    [ ] The unwinding of the DNA double helix by helicase enzyme to allow replication or transcription to begin
    [*] The process by which ribosomes decode the mRNA nucleotide sequence into a specific amino acid sequence (polypeptide) — tRNA molecules serve as adapters, matching their anticodons to mRNA codons and delivering the corresponding amino acids — occurring at ribosomes in the cytoplasm (or rough ER) as the second step of gene expression
    [ ] The conversion of one type of RNA into another type through enzymatic modification in the nucleolus
    [ ] The movement of DNA from one chromosome to another during crossing over in meiosis
    Explanation: Translation — mRNA to protein: (1) Three stages: Initiation — small ribosomal subunit binds mRNA (at 5′ cap in eukaryotes, Shine-Dalgarno sequence in prokaryotes), scans for AUG start codon, initiator tRNA (Met-tRNA) binds, large subunit joins → complete ribosome with A, P, E sites; Elongation — cycle of codon recognition (aminoacyl-tRNA enters A site), peptide bond formation (catalyzed by rRNA = peptidyl transferase — ribosome is a ribozyme!), translocation (ribosome moves 3 nt → tRNA shifts A→P→E); Termination — stop codon (UAA/UAG/UGA) in A site → release factor binds → polypeptide released → ribosome dissociates. (2) Speed: ~15-20 amino acids/second in bacteria; ~6 amino acids/second in eukaryotes. (3) Polysomes (polyribosomes): multiple ribosomes simultaneously translate one mRNA → efficient protein production. (4) Post-translational modifications: folding (chaperones), cleavage, phosphorylation, glycosylation, ubiquitination (targets for proteasomal degradation). (5) Energy cost: 4 ATP equivalents per amino acid added (2 GTP for elongation + 1 ATP for tRNA charging + costs of initiation/termination factors).

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes a mutation (in genetics)?
    [ ] The normal process of DNA replication that produces exact copies of the original molecule every time
    [*] A permanent, heritable change in the nucleotide sequence of DNA — ranging from single base substitutions (point mutations: silent, missense, or nonsense) to insertions, deletions, duplications, and large chromosomal rearrangements — that can be spontaneous (replication errors) or induced by mutagens (UV, chemicals, radiation)
    [ ] A temporary change in protein shape caused by environmental temperature fluctuations that reverts when conditions normalize
    [ ] The natural variation in physical traits between parents and offspring caused solely by meiotic recombination
    Explanation: Mutation — the source of all genetic variation: (1) Point mutations: Silent/synonymous — no amino acid change (code degeneracy); Missense — different amino acid (conservative: similar properties, or nonconservative: different properties); Nonsense — premature stop codon → truncated protein. (2) Frameshift: insertion or deletion of bases (not multiples of 3) → completely altered downstream reading frame. (3) Large-scale: chromosomal deletions, duplications, inversions, translocations; polyploidy (whole genome duplication). (4) Causes: spontaneous — replication errors (~10⁻¹⁰/base after repair), tautomeric shifts, depurination (~5,000/cell/day), deamination (C→U, ~100-500/cell/day). Induced by mutagens: UV (pyrimidine dimers — repaired by nucleotide excision repair; defects → xeroderma pigmentosum), chemical alkylators (EMS), intercalators (ethidium bromide), base analogs (5-BrU), ionizing radiation (double-strand breaks). (5) Somatic vs. germline: somatic affect individual only (cancer); germline can be inherited. (6) Evolutionary role: mutations are the ultimate source of new alleles — raw material for natural selection.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes the central dogma of molecular biology?
    [ ] The principle that all cells must divide by mitosis to maintain genetic consistency throughout an organism's life
    [*] The fundamental principle (Francis Crick, 1958) that genetic information flows from DNA → RNA → Protein — where DNA is transcribed into mRNA and mRNA is translated into protein — describing the unidirectional flow of sequence information in biological systems, with known exceptions including reverse transcription (RNA → DNA) and RNA replication
    [ ] The theory that proteins can directly modify the DNA sequence to adapt to environmental changes without involving RNA
    [ ] The hypothesis that RNA was the only molecule needed for all cellular functions before DNA and proteins evolved
    Explanation: The central dogma — Crick's framework for molecular biology: (1) Original statement (Crick, 1958; refined 1970): information flows DNA → RNA → Protein. Once information passes to protein, it cannot flow back to nucleic acids. (2) Three information transfers: Replication (DNA → DNA), Transcription (DNA → RNA), Translation (RNA → Protein). (3) Known exceptions that don't actually violate the dogma: Reverse transcription (RNA → DNA): retroviruses (HIV) use reverse transcriptase (discovered by Temin and Baltimore, Nobel 1975); also in retrotransposons (~45% of human genome) and telomerase. RNA replication (RNA → RNA): RNA-dependent RNA polymerase in some RNA viruses (e.g., influenza, SARS-CoV-2). (4) What would violate it: Protein → RNA or Protein → DNA (never observed). Prions (misfolded proteins that propagate shape change) don't transfer sequence information, so they don't technically violate the dogma. (5) Modern complexity: gene expression is regulated at every level — epigenetics (DNA methylation, histone modification), transcriptional regulation, post-transcriptional (miRNA, RNA interference), post-translational modification. The simple 'one gene → one protein' model has been replaced by a complex regulatory network.

  Q21. A forensic scientist arrives at a crime scene and collects a tiny blood sample from a doorknob. Using PCR (polymerase chain reaction), she amplifies the DNA and generates a STR (short tandem repeat) profile. The profile matches the suspect with a probability of random match of 1 in 10 billion. How does PCR work, and why is DNA evidence so powerful for identification?
    [ ] PCR uses restriction enzymes to cut DNA into visible fragments that can be compared by size alone — identification works because every person has a unique restriction enzyme pattern
    [*] PCR exponentially amplifies specific DNA segments through repeated cycles of denaturation (95°C — separates strands), annealing (50-65°C — primers bind flanking regions), and extension (72°C — Taq polymerase synthesizes new strands) — doubling target DNA each cycle (2ⁿ amplification after n cycles) — and STR profiling exploits the fact that humans vary in the number of tandem repeats at specific loci, creating a virtually unique genetic fingerprint when 13+ loci are analyzed
    [ ] PCR works by cloning the entire human genome into bacteria, which then produce enough DNA for analysis — this takes several weeks but yields complete genomic data
    [ ] PCR photographs the DNA double helix using X-ray crystallography, and identification relies on matching the physical shape of the helix between samples
    Explanation: PCR and DNA forensics: (1) PCR (invented by Kary Mullis, Nobel 1993): uses thermostable Taq DNA polymerase (from Thermus aquaticus, a hot springs bacterium) to survive repeated heating cycles. 30 cycles → 2³⁰ ≈ 1 billion copies from a single molecule. Each cycle: denaturation → annealing → extension. Requires: template DNA, two primers (short oligos flanking target), dNTPs, buffer, Mg²⁺. (2) STR (Short Tandem Repeat) profiling: human genome has thousands of STR loci — regions where 2-6 base pair sequences repeat (e.g., AATG repeated 7-15 times). Different individuals have different numbers of repeats. FBI's CODIS database uses 20 core STR loci (expanded from 13 in 2017). (3) With 20 loci (each person has 2 alleles per locus), probability of two unrelated individuals sharing the same profile: ~1 in 10¹⁸ to 10³⁰ (depending on population). (4) Applications beyond crime: paternity testing, identifying disaster victims, exonerating the wrongly convicted (Innocence Project — 375+ exonerations via DNA evidence as of 2023), evolutionary studies (ancient DNA — Svante Pääbo, Nobel 2022, for Neanderthal genome).

  Q22. A pharmaceutical company develops an mRNA vaccine against a novel respiratory virus. After injection into the deltoid muscle, the mRNA enters cells, and within hours, the patient begins producing viral spike proteins that trigger an immune response. How does this process illustrate the central dogma, and why was mRNA chosen over DNA or protein-based approaches?
    [ ] mRNA vaccines work by permanently inserting the viral gene into human DNA, which then continuously produces the viral protein — this was chosen because DNA integration ensures lifelong immunity from a single dose
    [*] mRNA vaccines exploit the translation step of the central dogma — the synthetic mRNA (encoding viral spike protein) is taken up by cells and directly translated by host ribosomes into spike protein, which the immune system recognizes as foreign — mRNA was chosen because it cannot integrate into the genome (no reverse transcriptase in human cells), is naturally degraded within days, and can be rapidly designed and manufactured once the viral sequence is known
    [ ] mRNA vaccines contain weakened live virus particles coated with synthetic mRNA — the virus infects cells at a low level while the mRNA boosts the immune response indirectly
    [ ] mRNA vaccines directly stimulate antibody production without involving any cellular protein synthesis — the mRNA itself acts as the antigen that the immune system attacks
    Explanation: mRNA vaccines and the central dogma: (1) Central dogma link: DNA → RNA → Protein. mRNA vaccines skip the first step — they provide the pre-made mRNA, and host cells perform translation (RNA → Protein). No DNA involved; no nuclear entry required. (2) Technical details: Pfizer-BioNTech and Moderna COVID-19 vaccines use modified nucleosides (N1-methylpseudouridine replacing uridine — Karikó & Weissman, Nobel 2023 — reduces innate immune response to the mRNA itself). Encapsulated in lipid nanoparticles (LNPs) for cellular uptake and stability. (3) Advantages over other platforms: Speed of design (Moderna designed their vaccine in 2 days after receiving SARS-CoV-2 sequence, January 2020); no risk of genomic integration (mRNA stays in cytoplasm, degraded by cellular RNases within days); scalable manufacturing (in vitro transcription — no need to grow virus or use cell cultures). (4) Limitations: requires cold chain storage (mRNA is fragile); current delivery mainly to injection site + local lymph nodes; immune response wanes → boosters needed. (5) Future: mRNA vaccines being developed for influenza, RSV, HIV, malaria, cancer (personalized neoantigen vaccines).

  Q23. A couple visits a genetic counselor because their first child was diagnosed with cystic fibrosis (CF), an autosomal recessive disorder caused by mutations in the CFTR gene on chromosome 7. Both parents are healthy carriers. The counselor explains the molecular basis: the most common mutation, ΔF508, is a three-nucleotide deletion. How does this deletion affect the CFTR protein, and what is the probability that their next child will have CF?
    [ ] The ΔF508 deletion removes an entire exon from the CFTR gene, preventing any mRNA from being produced — since both parents carry this deletion, all their children will inevitably have CF
    [*] The ΔF508 deletion removes three nucleotides coding for phenylalanine at position 508 of the CFTR protein — this is an in-frame deletion (not a frameshift, since 3 nt = 1 codon), causing the protein to misfold, be retained in the ER, and degraded before reaching the cell membrane — resulting in defective chloride ion transport — and since both parents are carriers (Cf × Cf), each child has a 25% chance of having CF (homozygous ff), 50% carrier, 25% unaffected
    [ ] The ΔF508 deletion adds a premature stop codon that halts translation midway through the CFTR protein — the truncated protein is toxic to epithelial cells — there is a 50% chance the next child will be affected
    [ ] The ΔF508 deletion converts the CFTR gene into an oncogene that causes uncontrolled cell growth in the lungs — this is why CF patients are at high risk for lung cancer from birth
    Explanation: Cystic fibrosis and mutation types: (1) CFTR (Cystic Fibrosis Transmembrane conductance Regulator) — a chloride/bicarbonate ion channel in epithelial cell membranes. Gene located on chromosome 7q31.2 (cloned 1989 by Tsui, Collins, Riordan). 250 kb, 27 exons. (2) ΔF508 (deletion of phenylalanine at position 508): accounts for ~70% of CF alleles worldwide. The 3-nt deletion (CTT) removes one codon → protein is 1 amino acid shorter but NOT frameshifted. However, loss of Phe-508 causes misfolding → protein fails ER quality control → targeted for proteasomal degradation (ERAD pathway) → never reaches plasma membrane → no Cl⁻ transport. (3) Result: thick, sticky mucus in lungs (recurrent infections — Pseudomonas aeruginosa), pancreas (maldigestion), sweat glands (salty sweat — basis of sweat chloride test). (4) Genetics: autosomal recessive. Carrier × Carrier: 25% affected (ff), 50% carrier (Ff), 25% unaffected (FF). Carrier frequency: ~1/25 in Caucasians (heterozygote advantage hypothesized — possible resistance to cholera or typhoid). (5) Treatment advances: Trikafta (2019) — triple combination CFTR modulator (elexacaftor/tezacaftor/ivacaftor) helps ΔF508 protein fold correctly and reach membrane. Life expectancy increased from ~30 years (2000) to ~50+ years projected.

  Q24. Scientists studying the tobacco mosaic virus (TMV) in the 1950s performed a crucial experiment: they separated the virus into its RNA and protein components, then mixed RNA from strain A with protein coat from strain B. The reassembled virus infected tobacco plants, and the progeny viruses had strain A's protein coat. What does this experiment demonstrate about which molecule carries genetic information?
    [ ] The experiment proves that the protein coat carries genetic information, since the reassembled virus had strain B's coat and produced offspring with strain B characteristics
    [*] The Fraenkel-Conrat and Singer experiment (1957) demonstrates that RNA, not protein, carries the genetic information — since the progeny viruses had strain A's protein coat (encoded by strain A's RNA), proving that the RNA directed the synthesis of new viral components regardless of which protein coat was used for initial infection — supporting the concept that nucleic acids, not proteins, are the hereditary material
    [ ] The experiment shows that both RNA and protein equally contribute genetic information, with RNA controlling coat shape and protein controlling infectivity
    [ ] The experiment is inconclusive because TMV is a virus, and viruses do not follow the same genetic principles as cellular organisms
    Explanation: TMV reconstitution experiment — proof that RNA = genetic material: (1) Heinz Fraenkel-Conrat and Robley Williams (1955) first showed TMV could be disassembled into RNA + protein coat, then reassembled into infectious particles. (2) Fraenkel-Conrat and Singer (1957): mixed RNA from strain A + coat protein from strain B → reconstituted virus infected plants → progeny had strain A coat protein. This means strain A's RNA directed the production of new proteins. (3) Historical context: parallel to Hershey-Chase (1952, ³²P/³⁵S experiment showing DNA = genetic material in bacteriophage T2) and Avery-MacLeod-McCarty (1944, DNA = transforming principle in Streptococcus pneumoniae). TMV experiment was critical because it showed RNA can also serve as genetic material (TMV has no DNA at all). (4) Broader significance: established that nucleic acids (DNA or RNA) are the universal hereditary material. Not all viruses use DNA — many important pathogens are RNA viruses (influenza, HIV, SARS-CoV-2, Ebola, rabies). (5) This experiment was foundational for understanding: retroviruses (RNA genome → reverse transcribed to DNA), RNA world hypothesis, and the later development of mRNA therapeutics.

  Q25. A researcher using CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing technology designs a guide RNA (gRNA) that is complementary to a 20-nucleotide sequence within the BRCA1 gene. The Cas9 enzyme, guided by this gRNA, makes a precise double-strand break at the target site. How does CRISPR-Cas9 exploit the principles of base pairing, and what are the implications for treating genetic diseases?
    [ ] CRISPR-Cas9 works by randomly cutting DNA at thousands of locations throughout the genome, and natural selection within the cell ensures only beneficial cuts are retained — this makes it useful but highly unpredictable
    [*] CRISPR-Cas9 exploits Watson-Crick base pairing — the 20-nt guide RNA (gRNA) forms complementary base pairs with the target DNA sequence, directing the Cas9 nuclease to cut precisely at that location — this programmable specificity allows scientists to knock out genes, correct mutations, or insert new sequences, with therapeutic applications including sickle cell disease treatment (Casgevy, approved 2023) and potential cures for genetic disorders
    [ ] CRISPR-Cas9 uses protein-protein interactions, not base pairing — the Cas9 protein recognizes the shape of specific DNA sequences, similar to how transcription factors bind promoters, making gRNA unnecessary for targeting
    [ ] CRISPR-Cas9 permanently removes all copies of the target gene from every cell in the body after a single injection, making it a one-time cure for any genetic disease without any risk of side effects
    Explanation: CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing: (1) Origin: bacterial adaptive immune system (bacteria store snippets of viral DNA in CRISPR arrays — Clustered Regularly Interspaced Short Palindromic Repeats — to recognize and destroy returning phages). Discovered by Ishino (1987), characterized by Mojica, Horvath, Barrangou. (2) Mechanism: guide RNA (gRNA = crRNA + tracrRNA, or single chimeric sgRNA) base-pairs with 20-nt target complementary DNA. Cas9 (from Streptococcus pyogenes) also requires a PAM sequence (NGG) adjacent to the target for binding. Cas9 makes a blunt double-strand break (DSB). (3) Repair pathways: NHEJ (non-homologous end joining — error-prone → insertions/deletions → gene knockout) or HDR (homology-directed repair — template-guided → precise correction/insertion, but lower efficiency). (4) Jennifer Doudna and Emmanuelle Charpentier — Nobel Prize in Chemistry, 2020, for developing CRISPR-Cas9 as a programmable gene-editing tool. (5) Clinical applications: Casgevy (exagamglogene autotemcel, approved December 2023 by FDA) — first CRISPR therapy — treats sickle cell disease and transfusion-dependent β-thalassemia by editing patient's bone marrow cells to reactivate fetal hemoglobin (HbF) gene. (6) Concerns: off-target effects, mosaicism, ethical issues of germline editing (He Jiankui controversy, 2018).

  Q26. A patient with chronic myeloid leukemia (CML) is found to have the Philadelphia chromosome — a translocation between chromosomes 9 and 22, creating the BCR-ABL fusion gene. This fusion gene produces an abnormal protein with constitutive tyrosine kinase activity. How does this chromosomal mutation lead to cancer, and how does the drug imatinib (Gleevec) target it?
    [ ] The Philadelphia chromosome causes CML by deleting tumor suppressor genes on both chromosomes 9 and 22 — imatinib works by replacing these missing genes through gene therapy
    [*] The t(9;22) translocation fuses the BCR gene (chr 22) with the ABL proto-oncogene (chr 9), creating a BCR-ABL fusion protein with unregulated (constitutive) tyrosine kinase activity — this continuously activates cell growth signaling pathways (RAS, JAK-STAT, PI3K), preventing apoptosis and driving uncontrolled white blood cell proliferation — imatinib (Gleevec) is a targeted therapy that specifically binds the ATP-binding pocket of BCR-ABL, blocking its kinase activity and effectively treating CML
    [ ] The Philadelphia chromosome causes an increase in chromosome number (aneuploidy) that overwhelms cellular DNA repair mechanisms — imatinib restores normal chromosome number by inducing selective apoptosis of aneuploid cells
    [ ] The translocation inserts a viral oncogene into human chromosome 22 from a latent retrovirus — imatinib is an antiviral drug that eliminates the integrated viral DNA
    Explanation: Philadelphia chromosome and targeted cancer therapy: (1) Discovery: Peter Nowell and David Hungerford (1960) — first consistent chromosomal abnormality associated with cancer, named after Philadelphia where it was discovered. (2) Molecular basis: t(9;22)(q34;q11) — ABL1 proto-oncogene (chr 9) fuses with BCR gene (chr 22). ABL1 normally encodes a tightly regulated tyrosine kinase. BCR-ABL fusion protein (p210 in CML) has constitutive (always-on) kinase activity → continuous phosphorylation of downstream signaling proteins → uncontrolled cell proliferation + resistance to apoptosis. (3) Present in ~95% of CML cases; also in some ALL (acute lymphoblastic leukemia). (4) Imatinib (Gleevec/Glivec, approved 2001): developed by Brian Druker, Nicholas Lydon, and Charles Sawyer. Small molecule that fits into the ATP-binding pocket of BCR-ABL → blocks kinase activity → cancer cells stop dividing and undergo apoptosis. Revolutionized CML treatment: 5-year survival went from ~30% to ~90%. (5) Resistance: some patients develop point mutations in BCR-ABL (e.g., T315I 'gatekeeper' mutation) → second/third-generation drugs developed: dasatinib, nilotinib, ponatinib (covers T315I). (6) Paradigm shift: demonstrated that understanding the molecular genetics of cancer can lead to highly targeted therapies with fewer side effects than traditional chemotherapy.

  Q27. Scientists extract DNA from a 40,000-year-old Neanderthal bone found in a Croatian cave. After sequencing, they discover that modern non-African humans share 1-4% of their DNA with Neanderthals. How is ancient DNA preserved and extracted, and what does this genomic comparison reveal about human evolution?
    [ ] Ancient DNA is perfectly preserved in bone because bone is completely impermeable to water and bacteria — extraction simply involves dissolving the bone and filtering out intact, full-length DNA chromosomes
    [*] Ancient DNA survives in bone as highly degraded short fragments (typically 30-80 bp), preserved by adsorption to hydroxyapatite mineral and cold/dry conditions that slow enzymatic degradation — extraction requires sterile conditions to prevent modern contamination, and sequencing uses specialized techniques for damaged and fragmentary DNA — the 1-4% Neanderthal ancestry in non-Africans indicates interbreeding (admixture) occurred when modern humans migrated out of Africa ~70,000-50,000 years ago and encountered Neanderthal populations in Europe/Western Asia
    [ ] Ancient DNA cannot actually be extracted from bones — the Neanderthal genome was reconstructed entirely by comparing modern human and chimpanzee genomes and computationally predicting what Neanderthal DNA must have looked like
    [ ] The 1-4% shared DNA is simply due to common ancestry with a shared primate ancestor millions of years ago and does not indicate any direct interaction between Neanderthals and modern humans
    Explanation: Ancient DNA and human evolution: (1) Svante Pääbo (Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine, 2022) — pioneer of paleogenomics. Published draft Neanderthal genome (2010) from Croatian Vindija Cave bones. (2) DNA preservation: in bone, DNA adsorbs to hydroxyapatite mineral → protected from nucleases. Cold, dry, stable environments best (permafrost ideal). DNA degrades exponentially: average fragment length decreases over time (typically 30-80 bp for ~40,000-year samples). Chemical damage: deamination of cytosine → uracil (C→T artifacts, corrected bioinformatically). (3) Extraction challenges: ancient DNA is vastly outnumbered by environmental/microbial DNA and modern human contamination. Strict protocols: UV-sterilized clean rooms, negative controls, authentication criteria. (4) Key findings: 1-4% Neanderthal DNA in non-African populations but near 0% in sub-Saharan Africans → interbreeding occurred during Out-of-Africa migration. Some Neanderthal alleles were adaptive: immune genes (HLA, TLR), skin/hair pigmentation genes. Others are linked to disease risk (type 2 diabetes, Crohn's disease, lupus). (5) Also discovered Denisovans — known mainly from DNA (single finger bone, Denisova Cave, Siberia) — contributed 3-6% of DNA to Melanesian populations. (6) Record oldest DNA: 1.2 million-year-old mammoth DNA from Siberian permafrost (2021).

  Q28. A molecular biology student performs gel electrophoresis on DNA fragments produced by restriction enzyme digestion. She notices that smaller fragments migrate farther through the agarose gel toward the positive electrode. Her professor asks her to explain why DNA moves toward the positive pole and how fragment size affects migration. What is the correct explanation?
    [ ] DNA moves toward the positive electrode because DNA molecules are positively charged due to their nitrogenous bases — smaller fragments move faster because they have fewer bases generating repulsive forces
    [*] DNA moves toward the positive electrode (anode) because the phosphate groups in its sugar-phosphate backbone carry negative charges at physiological pH — all DNA fragments have the same charge-to-mass ratio, so separation depends entirely on size: smaller fragments navigate through the agarose gel pores more easily, experiencing less friction, and thus migrate farther in a given time than larger fragments
    [ ] DNA moves toward the positive electrode because the nitrogenous bases are hydrophobic and are repelled by water in the buffer — fragment size doesn't actually matter; separation is based on base composition (GC content)
    [ ] DNA moves toward the positive electrode because restriction enzymes attach positively charged tags to the cut ends — larger fragments have more cut ends and therefore more positive charge, so they move faster
    Explanation: Gel electrophoresis — fundamental molecular biology technique: (1) DNA charge: each phosphodiester linkage has one negative charge at pH > 2 (pKa of phosphate ~1). At physiological pH, DNA is uniformly negatively charged. In an electric field, DNA migrates toward the anode (+). (2) Charge-to-mass ratio is constant for DNA (each nucleotide adds ~one charge and ~330 Da) → all linear DNA fragments migrate at the same rate in free solution. Size separation requires a molecular sieve. (3) Agarose gel acts as a sieve: pore size depends on agarose concentration (0.5% for large fragments 1-30 kb; 2% for small fragments 0.1-2 kb). Smaller fragments thread through pores more easily → migrate farther. (4) Visualization: ethidium bromide (intercalates between bases, fluoresces under UV) or safer alternatives (SYBR Safe, SYBR Green). (5) Size determination: compare migration distance to DNA ladder (known size standards) — log(size) vs. distance is approximately linear. (6) Types: standard agarose electrophoresis (100 bp-25 kb), pulsed-field gel electrophoresis (PFGE — for large DNA, up to several Mb), polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis (PAGE — single-nucleotide resolution, used in Sanger sequencing). (7) Applications: DNA fragment analysis, restriction mapping, PCR product verification, Southern blotting, forensic DNA profiling.

  Q29. Researchers discover that identical twins, despite having the same DNA sequence, can develop different diseases as they age — one twin develops type 2 diabetes at age 50 while the other remains healthy. Epigenetic analysis reveals differences in DNA methylation patterns in genes related to insulin signaling. How can identical DNA sequences produce different outcomes, and what role does epigenetics play?
    [ ] The twins must actually have different DNA sequences that were not detected by standard sequencing — identical twins always develop the same diseases if they truly have identical genomes
    [*] Epigenetic modifications — chemical changes to DNA or histone proteins that alter gene expression without changing the nucleotide sequence — explain how identical genomes can produce different outcomes: DNA methylation (adding -CH₃ to cytosine, typically at CpG sites) generally silences genes by blocking transcription factor binding, and different life experiences (diet, stress, exercise, environmental exposures) can cause divergent methylation patterns in identical twins over time, activating or silencing different genes
    [ ] The difference is caused by somatic mutations that accumulate randomly throughout life — epigenetics has no role, and the twins simply acquired different point mutations in their insulin signaling genes
    [ ] The healthy twin must have received a protective gene from a viral infection earlier in life that was incorporated into their genome through horizontal gene transfer
    Explanation: Epigenetics — beyond the DNA sequence: (1) Definition: heritable changes in gene expression that do not involve changes to the DNA nucleotide sequence. 'Epi' = above/on top of genetics. Term coined by Conrad Waddington (1942). (2) Major mechanisms: DNA methylation — addition of -CH₃ to 5′ position of cytosine (5-methylcytosine, 5mC), primarily at CpG dinucleotides. ~70-80% of CpGs in human genome are methylated. CpG islands (clusters of CpGs near promoters) are usually unmethylated in active genes. Methylation → gene silencing (recruits methyl-binding proteins → attract HDACs → chromatin condensation). Catalyzed by DNA methyltransferases (DNMT1, DNMT3A, DNMT3B). (3) Histone modifications: acetylation (generally activating — loosens chromatin), methylation (context-dependent: H3K4me3 = active, H3K27me3 = repressive), phosphorylation, ubiquitination. 'Histone code' hypothesis (Strahl & Allis, 2000). (4) Twin studies: MZ (monozygotic) twin epigenetic concordance decreases with age ('epigenetic drift'). Fraga et al. (2005): older twins showed more epigenetic differences than younger twins, correlated with different lifestyles and disease outcomes. (5) Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance: Dutch Hunger Winter (1944-45) — grandchildren of women who were pregnant during famine showed altered DNA methylation and increased cardiovascular disease risk. (6) Clinical relevance: cancer epigenetics (global hypomethylation + promoter hypermethylation of tumor suppressors); epigenetic drugs — DNMT inhibitors (azacitidine, decitabine) and HDAC inhibitors (vorinostat) for cancer treatment.

  Q30. During the COVID-19 pandemic, scientists tracked the evolution of SARS-CoV-2 by sequencing viral RNA genomes from patients worldwide. They observed that the virus accumulated about 2 mutations per month in its ~30,000-nucleotide RNA genome — much faster than typical DNA-based organisms. New variants (Alpha, Delta, Omicron) emerged with different transmissibility and immune evasion properties. Why do RNA viruses mutate faster than DNA-based organisms, and how does this relate to the structure of RNA versus DNA?
    [ ] RNA viruses mutate faster because RNA molecules are larger than DNA molecules, providing more targets for mutation — SARS-CoV-2 has a bigger genome than human DNA, which is why it evolves faster
    [*] RNA viruses mutate faster primarily because RNA-dependent RNA polymerase (RdRp) lacks the 3′→5′ proofreading exonuclease activity that DNA polymerases possess — resulting in error rates of ~10⁻⁴ to 10⁻⁶ per nucleotide per replication (vs. ~10⁻¹⁰ for DNA replication with proofreading and mismatch repair) — combined with RNA's inherent chemical instability (2′-OH on ribose makes it more susceptible to hydrolysis) and short generation times, this produces rapid viral evolution and the emergence of new variants
    [ ] RNA viruses don't actually mutate faster — they just appear to because scientists sequence them more frequently, creating the illusion of faster evolution compared to organisms that are sequenced less often
    [ ] RNA viruses mutate faster because uracil in RNA is chemically identical to thymine in DNA but is not recognized by any repair enzymes — since RNA uses uracil instead of thymine, all RNA bases eventually convert to uracil
    Explanation: RNA virus mutation rates and viral evolution: (1) RNA polymerase fidelity: RNA-dependent RNA polymerase (RdRp) error rate ~10⁻⁴ to 10⁻⁶ per nt per replication (varies by virus). DNA polymerase with proofreading: ~10⁻⁷. After mismatch repair: ~10⁻¹⁰. RNA viruses lack proofreading → 10,000-1,000,000× higher mutation rate. (2) Exception: coronaviruses (including SARS-CoV-2) have a proofreading exonuclease (nsp14-ExoN) — unique among RNA viruses — which reduces their error rate to ~10⁻⁶ per nt. This allows them to maintain larger genomes (~30 kb vs. typical RNA virus 5-15 kb). Still much higher mutation rate than DNA organisms. (3) RNA instability: 2′-OH on ribose → susceptible to alkaline hydrolysis and enzymatic degradation. DNA's deoxyribose (no 2′-OH) is more chemically stable → better for long-term information storage. Why DNA replaced RNA as the genetic material in most life. (4) SARS-CoV-2 evolution: ~2 substitutions/month in 30 kb genome. Variants of concern: mutations in spike protein (e.g., D614G increased transmissibility; Omicron BA.1 had 30+ spike mutations → immune evasion). (5) Viral quasispecies — RNA virus populations exist as 'clouds' of closely related but non-identical genomes (Manfred Eigen's quasispecies theory). This diversity allows rapid adaptation to new hosts, drugs, or immune responses. (6) Practical implications: why we need annual flu vaccines (antigenic drift), why HIV is hard to cure (high mutation rate → drug resistance), why combination antiretroviral therapy (cART) uses 3+ drugs simultaneously.

--- Lesson 6.6:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Biochemistry in Everyday Life
Key Concepts: Biochemistry in Everyday Life

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of biochemistry in everyday life in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to biochemistry in everyday life
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 6.6:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. How do vitamins function in the human body, and why are they essential despite being needed only in small amounts?
    [ ] Vitamins are the primary energy source for cellular metabolism — without them, cells cannot produce ATP or perform any biochemical reactions
    [*] Vitamins primarily function as coenzymes or coenzyme precursors that assist enzymes in catalyzing biochemical reactions — they are essential because the body cannot synthesize most of them (or makes insufficient amounts), yet they are required for metabolism, immune function, blood clotting, and many other processes — classified as fat-soluble (A, D, E, K — stored in adipose tissue) or water-soluble (B vitamins, C — excreted in urine, need regular intake)
    [ ] Vitamins serve as structural building blocks for cell membranes and organelles — they are incorporated directly into the phospholipid bilayer to maintain membrane integrity
    [ ] Vitamins are only needed during childhood growth — adults produce all necessary vitamins endogenously through metabolic pathways in the liver
    Explanation: Vitamins in biochemistry: (1) Coenzyme function: many B vitamins serve as coenzymes — NAD⁺ (from niacin/B3), FAD (from riboflavin/B2), coenzyme A (from pantothenic acid/B5), TPP (from thiamine/B1), PLP (from pyridoxine/B6). These coenzymes are essential participants in metabolic pathways (glycolysis, citric acid cycle, electron transport chain, amino acid metabolism). (2) Fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K): stored in liver and adipose tissue, can accumulate to toxic levels (hypervitaminosis). Vitamin A (retinol) — vision, gene expression; D (cholecalciferol) — calcium absorption, immune regulation (synthesized in skin via UV-B); E (tocopherol) — antioxidant protecting membrane lipids; K (phylloquinone) — blood clotting factors (carboxylation of glutamate residues), bone metabolism. (3) Water-soluble vitamins: B-complex (8 vitamins) + C (ascorbic acid). Excreted in urine → rarely toxic, but need regular intake. Vitamin C: collagen synthesis (hydroxyproline/hydroxylysine), antioxidant, iron absorption. Deficiency: scurvy (James Lind's citrus experiment, 1747 — one of the first clinical trials). (4) Deficiency diseases: beriberi (B1/thiamine), pellagra (B3/niacin — the 4 D's: dermatitis, diarrhea, dementia, death), pernicious anemia (B12 — intrinsic factor needed for absorption), rickets (vitamin D), night blindness (vitamin A). (5) Casimir Funk coined 'vitamine' (vital amine) in 1912.

  Q2. What is cholesterol's role in the body, and why is the distinction between LDL and HDL cholesterol important for health?
    [ ] Cholesterol is purely harmful — it serves no biological purpose and should be completely eliminated from the diet for optimal health
    [*] Cholesterol is an essential lipid (sterol) that serves as a structural component of cell membranes (modulating fluidity), and is a precursor for steroid hormones (estrogen, testosterone, cortisol), bile salts (fat digestion), and vitamin D — LDL ('bad' cholesterol) delivers cholesterol to tissues and can deposit in arterial walls causing atherosclerosis, while HDL ('good' cholesterol) transports cholesterol back to the liver for excretion (reverse cholesterol transport)
    [ ] Cholesterol is a type of protein that forms the structural framework of bones and cartilage — LDL transports it to bones while HDL removes it from joints
    [ ] Cholesterol is only obtained from dietary sources — the body cannot produce it, which is why high-cholesterol diets are the sole cause of heart disease
    Explanation: Cholesterol biochemistry: (1) Structure: a 4-ring steroid with a hydroxyl group — amphipathic molecule. (2) Cell membrane role: cholesterol inserts between phospholipids, reducing fluidity at high temperatures (restricts movement) and preventing crystallization at low temperatures — acts as a 'fluidity buffer.' About 25-30% of mammalian membrane lipids. (3) Biosynthesis: liver produces ~800 mg/day (endogenous) via the mevalonate pathway. Key enzyme: HMG-CoA reductase (target of statin drugs — lovastatin discovered by Akira Endo, 1976). Dietary cholesterol absorption varies widely (~30-70%). (4) Lipoprotein transport: Cholesterol is hydrophobic → transported in blood via lipoproteins. LDL (Low-Density Lipoprotein) — delivers cholesterol to peripheral tissues; excess LDL → oxidized LDL → taken up by macrophages → foam cells → atherosclerotic plaques → heart attack/stroke. HDL (High-Density Lipoprotein) — removes excess cholesterol from tissues via reverse cholesterol transport to liver → protective. (5) Health guidelines: optimal LDL < 100 mg/dL; HDL > 60 mg/dL (protective); total cholesterol < 200 mg/dL. (6) Precursor roles: sex hormones (estrogen, progesterone, testosterone), adrenal cortical hormones (cortisol, aldosterone), bile salts (emulsify dietary fats), vitamin D (7-dehydrocholesterol in skin → UV-B → cholecalciferol).

  Q3. What causes lactose intolerance, and how does it illustrate the relationship between enzyme function and everyday digestion?
    [ ] Lactose intolerance is caused by an allergic immune response to milk proteins (casein and whey) — it involves IgE antibodies and can cause anaphylaxis
    [*] Lactose intolerance results from insufficient production of lactase enzyme (β-galactosidase), which normally hydrolyzes lactose (a disaccharide) into glucose and galactose for absorption — without adequate lactase, undigested lactose passes to the colon where bacteria ferment it, producing gas (H₂, CO₂, CH₄), lactic acid, and short-chain fatty acids that cause bloating, cramping, and diarrhea — it demonstrates that enzyme specificity and availability directly determine what foods we can digest
    [ ] Lactose intolerance is caused by excess lactase production that breaks down too many sugars simultaneously, overwhelming the intestinal lining and causing inflammation
    [ ] Lactose intolerance occurs because the stomach's pH is too low to properly denature milk proteins — it can be cured by taking antacids before consuming dairy
    Explanation: Lactose intolerance biochemistry: (1) Lactose: β-1,4-glycosidic bond linking galactose to glucose. Requires lactase (β-galactosidase) in brush border of small intestinal epithelium for hydrolysis. (2) Lactase persistence vs. non-persistence: most mammals (and most humans globally) downregulate lactase after weaning (lactase non-persistence — the ancestral state). Lactase persistence (adult lactose tolerance) is a genetic trait: ~35% of global population. Most common in Northern Europeans (~95% lactase persistent) and some African pastoralist populations (Maasai, Fulani). (3) Genetics: caused by SNPs in regulatory region upstream of LCT gene (chr 2). In Europeans: -13910*T allele (C→T) — discovered 2002. Independent mutations arose in Africa (-13907*G, -13915*G, -14010*C). Classic example of convergent evolution driven by dairy farming culture (gene-culture coevolution). (4) Mechanism of symptoms: undigested lactose → osmotically draws water into intestinal lumen → colonic bacteria ferment lactose → H₂, CO₂, CH₄ (gas/bloating), lactic acid, short-chain fatty acids (acidic stool, cramping, diarrhea). (5) Diagnosis: hydrogen breath test (elevated H₂ after lactose ingestion). (6) Management: lactase enzyme supplements (Lactaid), lactose-free dairy products, or alternative calcium sources. (7) Important distinction: lactose intolerance ≠ milk allergy (which is an IgE-mediated immune response to casein or whey proteins — can cause anaphylaxis).

  Q4. How does fermentation work as a biochemical process, and why is it essential in food production?
    [ ] Fermentation is a form of aerobic respiration that requires oxygen to convert glucose into carbon dioxide and water — it's used in food production because oxygen improves flavor
    [*] Fermentation is anaerobic metabolism in which microorganisms (yeast, bacteria) convert sugars into useful byproducts without oxygen — alcohol fermentation by yeast (glucose → ethanol + CO₂) is used in bread (CO₂ makes dough rise), beer, and wine, while lactic acid fermentation by bacteria (glucose → lactic acid) is used to make yogurt, cheese, kimchi, and sauerkraut — preserving food by lowering pH and creating distinctive flavors
    [ ] Fermentation is a purely chemical (non-biological) oxidation process — no living organisms are involved; it occurs spontaneously when sugars are exposed to warm temperatures
    [ ] Fermentation requires extremely high temperatures (above 100°C) to break down sugar molecules, which is why all fermented foods must be cooked before consumption
    Explanation: Fermentation biochemistry: (1) Metabolic context: fermentation follows glycolysis (glucose → 2 pyruvate + 2 ATP + 2 NADH) but regenerates NAD⁺ without oxygen, allowing glycolysis to continue anaerobically. Far less efficient than aerobic respiration (2 ATP vs. 30-32 ATP per glucose). (2) Alcohol (ethanol) fermentation: pyruvate → acetaldehyde (via pyruvate decarboxylase, requires TPP/thiamine) + CO₂ → ethanol (via alcohol dehydrogenase, oxidizes NADH → NAD⁺). Organisms: Saccharomyces cerevisiae (baker's/brewer's yeast). Applications: bread (CO₂ = leavening agent, ethanol evaporates during baking), beer/wine (ethanol = desired product), biofuel production. (3) Lactic acid fermentation: pyruvate → lactate (via lactate dehydrogenase, oxidizes NADH → NAD⁺). Organisms: Lactobacillus, Streptococcus thermophilus. Applications: yogurt, cheese, kimchi, sauerkraut, kombucha. Also occurs in human muscle during intense exercise (oxygen debt → lactate accumulation → fatigue/soreness). (4) Other fermentations: acetic acid (vinegar — Acetobacter, actually aerobic), propionic acid (Swiss cheese holes — Propionibacterium). (5) Historical significance: Louis Pasteur (1857) demonstrated fermentation is caused by living organisms ('ferments'), disproving spontaneous generation. Buchner (1897) showed cell-free yeast extract could ferment — discovered enzymes (Nobel 1907). (6) Preservation: lactic acid lowers pH to ~3.5-4.5, inhibiting pathogenic bacteria — fermentation was one of humanity's earliest food preservation methods (~7000 BCE in China, ~6000 BCE in Mesopotamia).

  Q5. What are antioxidants, and how do they protect cells from damage caused by free radicals?
    [ ] Antioxidants are minerals that strengthen cell walls by forming cross-links between cellulose fibers — they prevent physical damage from environmental stress
    [*] Antioxidants are molecules (including vitamins C and E, glutathione, and carotenoids) that neutralize free radicals — unstable molecules with unpaired electrons that damage DNA, proteins, and cell membrane lipids through chain-reaction oxidation — by donating electrons to stabilize free radicals without becoming dangerously reactive themselves, thereby preventing oxidative stress linked to aging, cancer, cardiovascular disease, and neurodegenerative disorders
    [ ] Antioxidants work by removing all oxygen from cells, since oxygen itself is the sole cause of cellular damage — this is why anaerobic organisms never experience cell damage
    [ ] Antioxidants are synthetic pharmaceutical compounds not found in natural foods — they were invented in the 1990s and have no natural biological equivalents
    Explanation: Antioxidants and oxidative stress: (1) Free radicals: atoms or molecules with unpaired electrons — highly reactive. Reactive oxygen species (ROS): superoxide (O₂⁻•), hydroxyl radical (•OH), hydrogen peroxide (H₂O₂), singlet oxygen. Generated by: mitochondrial electron transport chain (1-3% of O₂ becomes superoxide normally), UV radiation, ionizing radiation, cigarette smoke, pollution, some drugs. (2) Damage mechanism: lipid peroxidation (chain reaction: free radical abstracts H from unsaturated fatty acid → lipid radical → reacts with O₂ → peroxyl radical → propagates to neighboring lipids → membrane damage). Also: DNA oxidation (8-oxoguanine → G→T transversions → mutations), protein oxidation (carbonylation → loss of function, aggregation). (3) Antioxidant defense: Enzymatic — superoxide dismutase (SOD: O₂⁻• → H₂O₂), catalase (H₂O₂ → H₂O + O₂), glutathione peroxidase (reduces H₂O₂ using GSH). Non-enzymatic — vitamin C (ascorbate, water-soluble, regenerates vitamin E), vitamin E (α-tocopherol, lipid-soluble, breaks lipid peroxidation chains), glutathione (GSH, most abundant intracellular antioxidant, tripeptide), carotenoids (β-carotene, lycopene), polyphenols (from fruits, vegetables, tea, coffee). (4) Oxidative stress: imbalance between ROS production and antioxidant defense → linked to aging (free radical theory of aging — Denham Harman, 1956), cancer, cardiovascular disease, Alzheimer's, Parkinson's.

  Q6. How do trans fats differ structurally from cis fats, and why are trans fats particularly harmful to cardiovascular health?
    [ ] Trans fats and cis fats are chemically identical — the only difference is that trans fats come from animal sources while cis fats come from plants
    [*] Trans fats have hydrogen atoms on opposite sides of the carbon-carbon double bond (creating a straight chain similar to saturated fats), while cis fats have hydrogens on the same side (creating a kinked chain) — trans fats are harmful because they raise LDL cholesterol AND lower HDL cholesterol simultaneously (worse than saturated fats), promote inflammation, and increase cardiovascular disease risk — most dietary trans fats come from industrial partial hydrogenation of vegetable oils
    [ ] Trans fats have more carbon atoms than cis fats, making them larger molecules that accumulate in blood vessels — they are harmful simply because of their molecular size, not their shape
    [ ] Trans fats are synthetic polymers that cannot be broken down by any human enzyme — they accumulate permanently in the body and can never be eliminated
