--- Lesson 7.3:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: The Plasma Membrane
Key Concepts: The Plasma Membrane

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of the plasma membrane in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to the plasma membrane
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 7.3:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is the fluid mosaic model, and who proposed it?
    [ ] The fluid mosaic model states that the cell membrane is a rigid, crystalline layer of proteins — proposed by Robert Hooke in 1665
    [*] Proposed by S.J. Singer and Garth Nicolson in 1972, the fluid mosaic model describes the plasma membrane as a dynamic, fluid phospholipid bilayer with a 'mosaic' of proteins (integral and peripheral) that can move laterally within the membrane — the phospholipids provide the fluid matrix while diverse proteins embedded in or attached to the bilayer carry out specific functions like transport, signaling, and cell recognition
    [ ] The fluid mosaic model proposes that cell membranes are made entirely of carbohydrates arranged in a grid pattern — proposed by Watson and Crick in 1953
    [ ] The fluid mosaic model states that all membranes are identical in composition regardless of cell type — proposed by Schleiden and Schwann as part of cell theory
    Explanation: The fluid mosaic model (Singer & Nicolson, 1972): (1) 'Fluid' = phospholipids and most proteins are not fixed in place — they move laterally within the plane of the membrane (lateral diffusion). Demonstrated by Frye & Edidin (1970) cell fusion experiment: fluorescently labeled human and mouse membrane proteins intermixed within 40 minutes. (2) 'Mosaic' = the membrane contains a diverse array of proteins (integral/transmembrane proteins that span the bilayer, peripheral proteins attached to surfaces) and other components (cholesterol, glycolipids, glycoproteins) creating a mosaic pattern. (3) Replaced earlier models: Davson-Danielli 'sandwich' model (1935) — proposed protein coated both surfaces of a lipid bilayer. Robertson's unit membrane model (1960s) — proposed all biological membranes were identical. (4) Key features: asymmetry (inner and outer leaflets differ in lipid and protein composition), fluidity (varies with temperature, fatty acid saturation, cholesterol content), and selectivity (controls what enters/exits the cell).

  Q2. What is the structure of a phospholipid, and why does its amphipathic nature drive membrane formation?
    [ ] Phospholipids are entirely hydrophobic molecules with four fatty acid chains — they form membranes because they are repelled by all water in the body
    [*] A phospholipid has a hydrophilic (water-loving) phosphate head group attached to a glycerol backbone and two hydrophobic (water-fearing) fatty acid tails — this amphipathic nature (both polar and nonpolar regions) drives spontaneous bilayer formation in aqueous environments: heads face water on both surfaces, tails face each other in the interior, creating a stable barrier that is the basis of all biological membranes
    [ ] Phospholipids are small, water-soluble sugars that dissolve in the cytoplasm — they do not actually form structural membranes
    [ ] Phospholipids have three hydrophilic tails and one hydrophobic head — they form membranes only in the presence of cholesterol
    Explanation: Phospholipid structure and membrane formation: (1) Structure: glycerol backbone with two fatty acid tails (hydrophobic, typically 16-18 carbons, one often unsaturated with a cis double bond creating a kink) and one phosphate head group (hydrophilic, often modified with choline, ethanolamine, serine, or inositol → phosphatidylcholine, phosphatidylethanolamine, phosphatidylserine, phosphatidylinositol). (2) Amphipathic = having both hydrophilic and hydrophobic regions. (3) Self-assembly: in aqueous solution, phospholipids spontaneously form bilayers to minimize hydrophobic exposure to water — thermodynamically favorable (increases entropy of surrounding water). Can also form micelles (single layer, tails inward) or liposomes (bilayer spheres). (4) Bilayer properties: ~5 nm thick. Hydrophobic core prevents passage of ions and polar molecules. Flexible — can bend, fuse, and bud. Self-sealing — if punctured, phospholipids rearrange to close the gap.

  Q3. What role does cholesterol play in animal cell membranes?
    [ ] Cholesterol makes membranes completely rigid and impermeable at all temperatures — it blocks all molecular transport across the membrane
    [*] Cholesterol acts as a 'fluidity buffer' in animal cell membranes — at high temperatures, it restrains phospholipid movement (reducing excessive fluidity) by interacting with fatty acid tails, and at low temperatures, it prevents tight packing and crystallization of phospholipids (maintaining fluidity) — cholesterol molecules are wedged between phospholipids with their hydroxyl group near the phospholipid heads and their steroid ring structure among the fatty acid tails
    [ ] Cholesterol's only function is to provide energy storage in the membrane — it serves no structural or regulatory role whatsoever
    [ ] Cholesterol is found only in plant cell membranes and is completely absent from all animal cells — plants use it for photosynthesis support
    Explanation: Cholesterol in membranes: (1) Positioning: cholesterol intercalates between phospholipids — hydroxyl group (polar) near phospholipid head, flat steroid ring system alongside upper portions of fatty acid tails, hydrocarbon tail extends toward bilayer center. (2) Fluidity buffer: at 37°C (body temp): cholesterol restricts phospholipid movement → reduces fluidity. At low temperatures: cholesterol disrupts regular packing of saturated fatty acid tails → prevents crystallization/gel phase → maintains membrane function. (3) Reduces permeability: decreases passage of small polar molecules (e.g., H₂O, small ions) by filling gaps between phospholipids. (4) Lipid rafts: cholesterol-enriched microdomains that organize signaling proteins and membrane receptors. Important for signal transduction, viral entry, and membrane trafficking. (5) Content: cholesterol comprises ~20-25% of membrane lipids in animal cells. Plant cells use phytosterols (e.g., stigmasterol, sitosterol) instead. Prokaryotes generally lack sterols (except mycoplasma, which acquire cholesterol from hosts).

  Q4. What are the different types of membrane proteins and their functions?
    [ ] There is only one type of membrane protein — structural proteins that form the rigid skeleton of the membrane and have no other functions
    [*] Membrane proteins include integral (transmembrane) proteins that span the entire bilayer with hydrophobic regions in the core and hydrophilic regions exposed on both sides, and peripheral proteins attached to membrane surfaces — functions include: transport (channels and carriers), enzymatic activity, signal transduction (receptors), cell-cell recognition (glycoproteins), intercellular joining (tight junctions, desmosomes), and attachment to the cytoskeleton/extracellular matrix
    [ ] Membrane proteins are all located on the outside of the cell and function only in immune defense — they never penetrate the lipid bilayer
    [ ] Proteins are not found in cell membranes — membranes consist entirely of phospholipids and cholesterol
    Explanation: Membrane protein types and functions: (1) Integral (transmembrane) proteins: span the entire bilayer. Have hydrophobic α-helical domains in the lipid core, hydrophilic domains on both surfaces. Can only be removed by detergents. Examples: ion channels, transporters, receptors. (2) Peripheral proteins: attached to membrane surfaces through electrostatic interactions or lipid anchors. Easily removed by changes in pH or ionic strength. Examples: spectrin (cytoskeleton attachment), signaling enzymes. (3) Six major functions: Transport — channels (aquaporins, ion channels) and carriers (GLUT transporters, Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase). Enzymatic activity — membrane-bound enzymes (adenylyl cyclase). Signal transduction — receptor proteins bind ligands (e.g., insulin receptor, G protein-coupled receptors). Cell-cell recognition — glycoproteins with carbohydrate chains (MHC molecules, blood group antigens). Intercellular joining — tight junctions, gap junctions, desmosomes. Cytoskeleton/ECM attachment — integrins link extracellular matrix to actin cytoskeleton.

  Q5. What does 'selective permeability' mean, and which molecules can cross the plasma membrane freely versus which require assistance?
    [ ] Selective permeability means the membrane allows everything to pass through equally — there is no distinction between molecules that can and cannot cross
    [*] Selective permeability means the membrane allows certain molecules to pass while blocking others based on size, charge, and polarity — small nonpolar molecules (O₂, CO₂, N₂) and small uncharged polar molecules (water, ethanol) can cross freely by diffusion through the lipid bilayer, while ions (Na⁺, K⁺, Cl⁻, Ca²⁺), large polar molecules (glucose, amino acids), and macromolecules (proteins) require transport proteins (channels or carriers) or vesicular transport (endocytosis/exocytosis)
    [ ] Selective permeability refers to the membrane's ability to select which organelles to contain — it has no effect on molecular passage
    [ ] All molecules require ATP to cross the membrane — no molecule can pass through the lipid bilayer without energy input
    Explanation: Selective permeability of the plasma membrane: (1) Based on the hydrophobic core of the phospholipid bilayer. (2) Can freely cross (by simple diffusion): small nonpolar molecules — O₂, CO₂, N₂ (dissolve in lipid bilayer). Small uncharged polar molecules — water (slowly; fast via aquaporins), ethanol, urea, glycerol. (3) Cannot freely cross (need assistance): ions — Na⁺, K⁺, Cl⁻, Ca²⁺, H⁺ (charged, repelled by hydrophobic core) → need ion channels or pumps. Large polar molecules — glucose, amino acids → need carriers (e.g., GLUT1-4 for glucose). Macromolecules — proteins, polysaccharides, DNA → need vesicular transport (endocytosis/exocytosis). (4) Importance: maintains different concentrations of ions and molecules inside vs. outside cell (e.g., high K⁺ inside, high Na⁺ outside). Enables electrochemical gradients essential for nerve impulses, muscle contraction, ATP synthesis. Prevents toxic substances from entering while allowing nutrients in.

  Q6. How do channel proteins and carrier proteins differ in facilitating membrane transport?
    [ ] Channel proteins and carrier proteins are identical — both form permanent open holes in the membrane through which any molecule can pass freely
    [*] Channel proteins form hydrophilic pores that allow specific ions or small molecules to pass rapidly through the membrane (often gated by voltage, ligands, or mechanical stress), while carrier proteins bind specific substrates and undergo conformational changes to shuttle them across — channels are generally faster (~10⁷-10⁸ ions/sec) but less selective, while carriers are slower (~10²-10³ molecules/sec) but more specific
    [ ] Channel proteins transport only lipids while carrier proteins transport only carbohydrates — neither can transport ions or amino acids
    [ ] Carrier proteins are found only in plant cells while channel proteins are exclusive to animal cells — no cell type has both
    Explanation: Channel vs. carrier proteins: (1) Channel proteins: form hydrophilic pores/tunnels through the membrane. Allow rapid passive transport of ions and small polar molecules. Types: ion channels (Na⁺, K⁺, Ca²⁺, Cl⁻ channels), aquaporins (water), porins (in outer membranes of bacteria and mitochondria). Gating: voltage-gated (respond to membrane potential changes — critical for nerve impulses), ligand-gated (open when signaling molecule binds — neurotransmitter receptors), mechanically gated (respond to physical stretch). Rate: ~10⁷-10⁸ ions/sec. (2) Carrier proteins: bind specific substrates on one side, undergo conformational change, release substrate on other side. Can mediate both passive (facilitated diffusion: GLUT1-4 glucose transporters) and active transport (Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase, Ca²⁺ ATPase). Rate: ~10²-10³ molecules/sec (much slower than channels). Higher specificity — structural complementarity with substrate.

  Q7. What are aquaporins, and why was their discovery significant?
    [ ] Aquaporins are enzymes that break down water molecules into hydrogen and oxygen inside cells — they were discovered by Watson and Crick
    [*] Aquaporins are specialized channel proteins that form pores allowing rapid, selective passage of water molecules across the plasma membrane — discovered by Peter Agre (Nobel Prize in Chemistry, 2003) — they transport up to ~3 billion water molecules per second per channel while excluding ions (even H⁺) and are critical in kidney collecting ducts (regulated by vasopressin/ADH), red blood cells, plant root cells, and the brain
    [ ] Aquaporins are phospholipids that are unique to aquatic organisms — they were discovered in the 1600s by Leeuwenhoek
    [ ] Aquaporins are a type of cholesterol that increases membrane permeability to all molecules, not just water
    Explanation: Aquaporins: (1) Discovery: Peter Agre isolated the first aquaporin (AQP1, originally called CHIP28) from red blood cell membranes in 1992. Nobel Prize in Chemistry 2003 (shared with Roderick MacKinnon for K⁺ channel structure). (2) Structure: tetrameric protein — 4 subunits, each forming an independent water pore. Each pore is ~2.8 Å at its narrowest, just wide enough for single-file water molecule passage. Selectivity: water molecules pass in single file; the pore's electrostatic environment (positively charged asparagine residues) prevents H⁺ (H₃O⁺) from passing (blocks proton 'hopping'). (3) Rate: ~3×10⁹ water molecules/sec/channel. (4) Types: 13 mammalian aquaporins (AQP0-AQP12). AQP1: red blood cells, kidney proximal tubule. AQP2: kidney collecting duct — regulated by vasopressin (ADH). Mutations → nephrogenic diabetes insipidus. AQP4: brain astrocytes (cerebral water balance). Aquaglyceroporins: also transport glycerol, urea. (5) Clinical: target for diuretic drug development and edema treatment.

  Q8. How does signal transduction work at the plasma membrane, and why is it important for cellular communication?
    [ ] Signal transduction requires direct physical contact between all communicating cells — no molecules are involved in the process
    [*] Signal transduction begins when a signaling molecule (ligand) binds to a specific receptor protein on the cell surface, triggering a conformational change that initiates an intracellular signaling cascade — this converts an extracellular signal into intracellular responses such as gene expression changes, enzyme activation, or cytoskeletal rearrangement — major pathways include G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), receptor tyrosine kinases (RTKs), and ion channel receptors
    [ ] Signal transduction occurs only in nerve cells — other cell types communicate exclusively through direct cytoplasmic connections
    [ ] Signal transduction uses DNA molecules as signals that pass freely through the membrane without any receptor involvement
    Explanation: Signal transduction at the plasma membrane: (1) Three stages: Reception (ligand binds receptor), Transduction (signal relayed through intracellular cascade, often amplified), Response (cellular change). (2) GPCRs (G protein-coupled receptors): largest family (~800 in humans). 7-transmembrane domain proteins. Ligand binding activates associated G protein → activates effector enzymes (adenylyl cyclase → cAMP, phospholipase C → IP₃ + DAG). Examples: adrenaline receptors, opioid receptors, most smell receptors. Nobel 2012 (Lefkowitz & Kobilka). (3) RTKs (receptor tyrosine kinases): ligand binding causes dimerization and autophosphorylation → activates Ras-MAPK pathway → gene expression changes (cell growth, differentiation). Examples: insulin receptor, EGF receptor. Many oncogenes encode mutant RTKs (constitutively active → cancer). (4) Ion channel receptors: ligand binding opens/closes ion channel → rapid changes in membrane potential. Example: acetylcholine receptor at neuromuscular junction. (5) Signal amplification: one ligand molecule → activation of many downstream molecules → large cellular response.

  Q9. How does the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase pump work, and why is it essential for cell function?
    [ ] The Na⁺/K⁺ pump is a passive transport process that requires no energy — sodium and potassium naturally flow to equal concentrations inside and outside cells
    [*] The Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase is an active transport pump (discovered by Jens Christian Skou, Nobel 1997) that uses one ATP molecule to pump 3 Na⁺ ions OUT of the cell and 2 K⁺ ions IN, against their concentration gradients — this creates an electrochemical gradient (membrane potential of ~-70 mV) essential for nerve impulse transmission, muscle contraction, maintaining cell volume, and driving secondary active transport (e.g., Na⁺-glucose cotransporter)
    [ ] The Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase pumps sodium and potassium in the same direction, concentrating both ions outside the cell
    [ ] The pump only functions in kidney cells and has no importance in neurons, muscles, or any other tissue type
    Explanation: Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase pump: (1) Discovered by Jens Christian Skou (1957, Nobel Prize 1997). Found in virtually all animal cells. (2) Mechanism: binds 3 Na⁺ on cytoplasmic side → ATP hydrolysis → phosphorylation causes conformational change → releases 3 Na⁺ outside → binds 2 K⁺ outside → dephosphorylation → conformational change → releases 2 K⁺ inside. Net: 3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in per ATP. (3) Creates electrochemical gradient: net export of positive charge → contributes to resting membrane potential (~-70 mV in neurons). Maintains [Na⁺] low inside (~12 mM) and [K⁺] high inside (~140 mM) vs. extracellular fluid (~145 mM Na⁺, ~4 mM K⁺). (4) Functions: enables nerve impulse transmission (action potentials rely on Na⁺ and K⁺ gradients), muscle contraction, maintains cell volume (prevents osmotic swelling), drives secondary active transport (Na⁺-glucose cotransporter SGLT1 in intestine/kidney uses Na⁺ gradient to import glucose). (5) Energy cost: ~25-40% of total cellular ATP consumption. In neurons, up to 70%.

  Q10. What are endocytosis and exocytosis, and how do they differ?
    [ ] Endocytosis and exocytosis are identical processes — both involve the cell expelling waste materials through the plasma membrane
    [*] Endocytosis is the process by which cells take in large molecules or particles by engulfing them with the plasma membrane to form intracellular vesicles (includes phagocytosis for large particles, pinocytosis for fluid, and receptor-mediated endocytosis for specific ligands via clathrin-coated pits), while exocytosis is the reverse — intracellular vesicles fuse with the plasma membrane to release their contents outside the cell (used for secretion of hormones, neurotransmitters, and waste removal)
    [ ] Endocytosis occurs only in plant cells while exocytosis occurs only in animal cells — neither process exists in prokaryotes or fungi
    [ ] Both processes are forms of passive transport that require no ATP and occur only when the cell is at rest
    Explanation: Endocytosis and exocytosis — vesicular transport: (1) Endocytosis (bringing material IN): Phagocytosis ('cell eating') — large particles engulfed by pseudopods → phagosome → fuses with lysosome. Used by macrophages, neutrophils. Pinocytosis ('cell drinking') — small droplets of extracellular fluid taken in via small vesicles. Nonspecific. Receptor-mediated endocytosis — specific ligands bind receptors → clathrin-coated pits → clathrin-coated vesicles → endosomes. Examples: LDL cholesterol uptake (familial hypercholesterolemia = defective LDL receptors), transferrin-iron uptake. (2) Exocytosis (releasing material OUT): vesicles from Golgi or other sources fuse with plasma membrane → contents released. Constitutive exocytosis: continuous (e.g., ECM components). Regulated exocytosis: triggered by signal (e.g., Ca²⁺-triggered neurotransmitter release at synapses, insulin secretion from pancreatic β cells). (3) Both require ATP and involve membrane fusion/budding. They balance each other: endocytosis removes membrane; exocytosis adds membrane. Crucial for membrane recycling.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes the plasma membrane?
    [ ] A rigid cellulose wall that surrounds plant cells and provides structural support against turgor pressure
    [*] A selectively permeable phospholipid bilayer that surrounds all cells, forming the boundary between the cell's internal environment and the external surroundings — it controls the passage of substances in and out of the cell and participates in cell signaling, adhesion, and recognition
    [ ] The network of protein filaments inside the cell that provides structural support and enables cell movement
    [ ] The fluid-filled space between the plasma membrane and the nucleus that contains all organelles
    Explanation: Plasma membrane (cell membrane): the thin (~7-8 nm) phospholipid bilayer boundary surrounding all living cells. Selectively permeable — controls what enters and exits the cell based on size, charge, and polarity. Contains embedded proteins for transport, signaling, recognition, and structural support. Present in all prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells. Distinct from cell wall (which some cells have outside the plasma membrane).

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes the phospholipid bilayer?
    [ ] A single layer of protein molecules that coats the outside of the cell and acts as a barrier to all substances
    [*] A double layer of phospholipid molecules arranged with their hydrophilic (water-loving) phosphate heads facing outward toward the aqueous environment on both sides, and their hydrophobic (water-fearing) fatty acid tails facing inward — this arrangement forms the structural core of the plasma membrane and all internal cellular membranes
    [ ] A layer of cholesterol molecules that makes the membrane completely impermeable to all substances including water and gases
    [ ] A carbohydrate coating on the cell surface that functions exclusively in immune recognition
    Explanation: Phospholipid bilayer: the fundamental structural arrangement of biological membranes. Two layers of phospholipid molecules: hydrophilic heads (phosphate group + variable head group) face the aqueous environments on both cytoplasmic and extracellular sides; hydrophobic tails (two fatty acid chains) face each other in the interior. ~5 nm thick. Self-assembles spontaneously in water due to the amphipathic nature of phospholipids. Creates a hydrophobic barrier that prevents free passage of ions and polar molecules.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes the fluid mosaic model?
    [ ] A model stating that the cell membrane is a rigid, crystalline structure with proteins permanently fixed in specific locations that never move
    [*] A model proposed by Singer and Nicolson (1972) describing the plasma membrane as a dynamic, fluid phospholipid bilayer with a diverse 'mosaic' of proteins (integral and peripheral) that can move laterally within the membrane — the phospholipids provide the fluid matrix while proteins carry out specific functions
    [ ] A model describing how cells are arranged in tissues like tiles in a mosaic floor, with no reference to membrane structure
    [ ] A model stating that membranes are made entirely of carbohydrates that flow freely like water
    Explanation: Fluid mosaic model (Singer & Nicolson, 1972): describes the plasma membrane as a two-dimensional fluid in which phospholipids and proteins can move laterally. 'Fluid' refers to the dynamic movement of lipids and proteins within the membrane plane. 'Mosaic' refers to the diverse array of embedded proteins (integral/transmembrane and peripheral) creating a mosaic pattern. Replaced earlier models (Davson-Danielli sandwich model). Key features: lateral mobility of components, membrane asymmetry, selective permeability.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes a channel protein?
    [ ] A lipid molecule that forms the structural backbone of the phospholipid bilayer
    [*] A membrane protein that forms a hydrophilic pore or tunnel through the lipid bilayer, allowing specific ions or small polar molecules to pass rapidly across the membrane — channel proteins may be gated (opening/closing in response to voltage changes, ligand binding, or mechanical stress) or constitutively open
    [ ] A protein that binds to DNA inside the nucleus and regulates gene expression during cell division
    [ ] A cytoplasmic enzyme that catalyzes the breakdown of glucose during glycolysis
    Explanation: Channel protein: a type of integral membrane protein that creates a hydrophilic passageway (pore) through the phospholipid bilayer. Allows rapid transport of specific ions or small molecules down their concentration or electrochemical gradient (passive transport). Types: ion channels (Na⁺, K⁺, Ca²⁺, Cl⁻), aquaporins (water), porins. Many are gated: voltage-gated (neurons), ligand-gated (synapses), mechanically gated (sensory cells). Transport rate: ~10⁷-10⁸ ions per second.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes cholesterol in the context of cell membranes?
    [ ] A type of carbohydrate that provides energy to membrane proteins for active transport processes
    [*] A steroid lipid molecule embedded in the plasma membrane of animal cells that acts as a 'fluidity buffer' — it restrains phospholipid movement at high temperatures (reducing excessive fluidity) and prevents tight packing at low temperatures (avoiding excessive rigidity and crystallization) — cholesterol is wedged between phospholipids with its hydroxyl group near the heads and its ring structure among the fatty acid tails
    [ ] A protein receptor on the cell surface that detects cholesterol levels in the blood and responds by adjusting lipid metabolism
    [ ] A signaling molecule secreted by the liver that has no structural role in cell membranes
    Explanation: Cholesterol in membranes: a steroid lipid (~20-25% of membrane lipids in animal cells). Structure: four fused hydrocarbon rings + short hydrocarbon tail + one hydroxyl group. Positioning: hydroxyl near phospholipid heads, rings alongside upper fatty acid tails. Functions: fluidity buffer (moderates fluidity across temperature range), reduces permeability to small polar molecules, forms lipid rafts (signaling microdomains). Plant cells use phytosterols instead. Prokaryotes generally lack sterols.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes selective permeability?
    [ ] The property of a membrane that allows all substances to pass through without any restriction or selectivity
    [*] The property of the plasma membrane that allows certain molecules to pass through while blocking others — based on factors including molecular size, charge, polarity, and the presence of specific transport proteins — small nonpolar molecules diffuse freely while ions and large polar molecules require protein-mediated transport
    [ ] A property unique to plant cell walls that blocks all water and dissolved substances from entering the cell
    [ ] The ability of the nucleus to select which genes are expressed, unrelated to membrane transport
    Explanation: Selective permeability: the ability of the plasma membrane to regulate passage of substances. Freely permeable to: small nonpolar molecules (O₂, CO₂, N₂), small uncharged polar molecules (water — slowly; fast via aquaporins). Requires transport proteins: ions (Na⁺, K⁺, Ca²⁺, Cl⁻), large polar molecules (glucose, amino acids). Requires vesicular transport: macromolecules (proteins, polysaccharides). Based on the hydrophobic interior of the phospholipid bilayer acting as a barrier to charged and polar substances.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes an integral (transmembrane) protein?
    [ ] A protein loosely attached to the outer surface of the membrane that can be easily removed by changing pH or ionic strength
    [*] A membrane protein that is permanently embedded in the phospholipid bilayer, typically spanning the entire membrane with hydrophobic regions in the lipid core and hydrophilic regions protruding on one or both sides — integral proteins function as channels, carriers, receptors, and enzymes and can only be removed by detergents that disrupt the lipid bilayer
    [ ] A soluble cytoplasmic protein that functions exclusively as an enzyme in glycolysis
    [ ] A structural protein found only in the extracellular matrix that connects cells to tissues
    Explanation: Integral (transmembrane) protein: a membrane protein permanently embedded in the lipid bilayer, typically spanning the entire membrane one or more times (single-pass or multi-pass). Hydrophobic α-helical domains interact with fatty acid tails in the membrane interior; hydrophilic domains protrude into aqueous environments on one or both sides. Cannot be removed without detergents. Examples: ion channels, aquaporins, GPCRs, receptor tyrosine kinases, Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes a glycoprotein?
    [ ] A lipid molecule with attached phosphate groups found exclusively in the interior of the membrane
    [*] A protein with short carbohydrate (sugar) chains covalently attached to its extracellular surface — glycoproteins on the plasma membrane function in cell-cell recognition, immune identification (distinguishing self from non-self, such as MHC molecules and blood group antigens), cell signaling, and protection of the cell surface
    [ ] A type of RNA molecule that coats the outside of the cell and prevents viral infection
    [ ] A storage form of glucose found inside the cytoplasm of liver and muscle cells
    Explanation: Glycoprotein: a protein with covalently attached oligosaccharide (sugar) chains. On the plasma membrane, carbohydrate chains always face the extracellular side (contributing to the glycocalyx). Functions: cell-cell recognition (immune cells identify self vs. non-self), signaling (many receptor proteins are glycoproteins), protection (glycocalyx shields cell surface), adhesion (cell-cell and cell-matrix interactions). Examples: MHC molecules, ABO blood group antigens, selectins, integrins.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes active transport?
    [ ] The movement of molecules from high concentration to low concentration without any energy input — also called diffusion
    [*] The movement of substances across the plasma membrane against their concentration gradient (from low to high concentration), requiring energy input — typically from ATP hydrolysis (primary active transport, e.g., Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase) or from an existing electrochemical gradient (secondary active transport, e.g., Na⁺-glucose cotransporter)
    [ ] The process of engulfing large particles by folding the cell membrane inward to form vesicles
    [ ] The spontaneous formation of the phospholipid bilayer in aqueous solution without energy input
    Explanation: Active transport: movement of substances against their concentration (or electrochemical) gradient — requires energy. Primary active transport: uses ATP directly. Examples: Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in), Ca²⁺ ATPase, H⁺/K⁺ ATPase (stomach acid). Secondary active transport (cotransport): uses the gradient established by primary active transport. Symport: substance moves in same direction as driving ion (Na⁺-glucose cotransporter SGLT1). Antiport: opposite directions (Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger). Consumes significant cellular ATP (~25-40% in many cells).

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes endocytosis?
    [ ] The process of intracellular vesicles fusing with the plasma membrane to release their contents outside the cell
    [*] The process by which cells take in large molecules, particles, or fluid by engulfing them with the plasma membrane, forming intracellular vesicles — types include phagocytosis (engulfing large particles like bacteria), pinocytosis (uptake of extracellular fluid and dissolved solutes), and receptor-mediated endocytosis (selective uptake of specific molecules via clathrin-coated pits)
    [ ] The process of DNA replication that occurs inside the nucleus before cell division
    [ ] The breakdown of glucose in the cytoplasm during the first stage of cellular respiration
    Explanation: Endocytosis: the process of bringing material into the cell via membrane-enclosed vesicles. Three types: (1) Phagocytosis ('cell eating') — large particles engulfed by pseudopods → phagosome → fuses with lysosome. Used by immune cells (macrophages, neutrophils). (2) Pinocytosis ('cell drinking') — small vesicles take in extracellular fluid and dissolved solutes. Nonspecific and continuous. (3) Receptor-mediated endocytosis — specific ligands bind receptors → clathrin-coated pits → clathrin-coated vesicles → endosomes. Highly selective. Example: LDL cholesterol uptake. All forms require ATP.

  Q21. A patient with familial hypercholesterolemia has dangerously high blood LDL cholesterol levels despite producing normal amounts of cholesterol in the liver. The defect is traced to mutations in the LDL receptor gene. Considering your knowledge of receptor-mediated endocytosis at the plasma membrane, why does this mutation cause high cholesterol?
    [ ] The mutation increases cholesterol production by the liver — LDL receptors have no role in cholesterol regulation or uptake
    [*] Normally, LDL receptors on cell surfaces bind LDL particles and internalize them via clathrin-coated pit receptor-mediated endocytosis — the LDL is then delivered to lysosomes where cholesterol is released for cellular use — with defective LDL receptors, cells cannot remove LDL from the blood efficiently, causing it to accumulate in the bloodstream → leads to atherosclerotic plaque formation and early heart disease — discovered by Goldstein and Brown (Nobel Prize 1985)
    [ ] The mutation causes LDL to become toxic inside cells, so the body produces more LDL to compensate for the damaged molecules
    [ ] LDL receptors normally destroy cholesterol in the blood using enzymatic activity — without them, cholesterol is simply never broken down
    Explanation: Familial hypercholesterolemia and receptor-mediated endocytosis: (1) Michael Brown and Joseph Goldstein (Nobel Prize 1985) discovered the LDL receptor pathway. (2) Normal process: LDL particles (carrying cholesterol) in blood bind to LDL receptors (integral membrane glycoproteins) on cell surfaces → receptors cluster in clathrin-coated pits → pit invaginates → clathrin-coated vesicle forms (endocytosis) → vesicle uncoats → fuses with endosome → acidic pH causes LDL to release from receptor → receptors recycled to surface (~150 trips per receptor) → LDL delivered to lysosomes → cholesterol esters hydrolyzed → free cholesterol used for membrane synthesis or stored. (3) In familial hypercholesterolemia: LDL receptor is absent, defective, or cannot cluster in coated pits → cells cannot internalize LDL → blood LDL rises dramatically → LDL oxidizes in blood vessel walls → foam cells → atherosclerotic plaques → heart attack/stroke. Heterozygotes: ~2× normal LDL, heart attack by 40s-50s. Homozygotes: ~5× normal LDL, heart attack in childhood/teens.

  Q22. A researcher studying arctic fish discovers that their cell membranes contain a much higher proportion of unsaturated fatty acids compared to tropical fish. Based on your knowledge of membrane fluidity and the fluid mosaic model, why is this adaptation necessary?
    [ ] Unsaturated fatty acids make membranes more rigid, which protects arctic fish from physical damage caused by ice crystals in cold water
    [*] Unsaturated fatty acids have cis double bonds that create kinks in the hydrocarbon tails, preventing tight packing of phospholipids and maintaining membrane fluidity at low temperatures — without this adaptation, the membranes of arctic fish would become too rigid and gel-like in cold water, impairing protein function, transport processes, and cellular signaling — this is an example of homeoviscous adaptation (cells adjust lipid composition to maintain optimal membrane fluidity across different temperatures)
    [ ] Unsaturated fatty acids are cheaper to produce metabolically, and arctic fish need to conserve energy in cold environments
    [ ] The higher unsaturated fatty acid content is coincidental and has no functional significance for membrane properties in cold environments
    Explanation: Homeoviscous adaptation and membrane fluidity: (1) Membrane fluidity depends on: temperature (↑temp = ↑fluidity), fatty acid saturation (unsaturated = more fluid due to kinks from cis double bonds preventing tight packing), fatty acid chain length (shorter = more fluid), and cholesterol content. (2) At low temperatures: saturated fatty acids pack tightly → gel phase (rigid, non-functional). Unsaturated fatty acids' kinks maintain spaces between phospholipids → liquid crystalline phase (functional). (3) Homeoviscous adaptation: organisms adjust membrane lipid composition to maintain optimal fluidity at their environmental temperature. Arctic organisms: high % unsaturated fatty acids. Tropical organisms: high % saturated fatty acids. Thermophilic bacteria: unique membrane lipids (ether-linked, branched). (4) Importance: membrane proteins require a fluid lipid environment to function. Rigid membranes impair: transport protein conformational changes, receptor signaling, membrane fusion/budding, lateral diffusion of signaling complexes.

  Q23. A neuroscientist is studying how a nerve impulse (action potential) travels along a neuron. She finds that the resting membrane potential is approximately -70 mV and that the action potential involves rapid changes in Na⁺ and K⁺ permeability. How do membrane proteins contribute to this process?
    [ ] Nerve impulses travel through the phospholipid bilayer directly — no membrane proteins are involved in electrical signaling in neurons
    [*] The Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase establishes the resting potential by maintaining ion gradients (high K⁺ inside, high Na⁺ outside) — during an action potential, voltage-gated Na⁺ channels open rapidly (depolarization to +30 mV), then inactivate while voltage-gated K⁺ channels open (repolarization back to -70 mV) — this coordinated opening/closing of ion channel membrane proteins propagates the electrical signal along the axon at speeds up to 120 m/s in myelinated neurons
    [ ] The action potential is caused by cholesterol molecules flipping between the inner and outer leaflets of the membrane, creating a temporary charge difference
    [ ] Peripheral proteins on the outside of the neuron physically carry sodium ions from one end of the axon to the other, creating an electrical current
    Explanation: Membrane proteins and action potentials: (1) Resting potential (-70 mV) established by: Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in → electrogenic); K⁺ leak channels (K⁺ diffuses out down gradient → negative interior); Nernst/Goldman equation determines membrane potential. (2) Action potential phases: Depolarization: stimulus opens voltage-gated Na⁺ channels → Na⁺ rushes in (down electrochemical gradient) → membrane depolarizes from -70 to ~+30 mV. Na⁺ channel inactivation: channels enter inactivated state within ~1 ms → Na⁺ influx stops. Repolarization: voltage-gated K⁺ channels open (delayed) → K⁺ rushes out → membrane repolarizes to -70 mV. Hyperpolarization: K⁺ channels slow to close → briefly overshoots to ~-80 mV. (3) Propagation: local current depolarizes adjacent membrane → opens more Na⁺ channels → wave of depolarization along axon. Myelinated neurons: saltatory conduction at nodes of Ranvier → up to 120 m/s. (4) All of this depends on membrane protein function — ion channels, pumps, and the phospholipid bilayer maintaining ion gradients.

  Q24. A pharmaceutical company is developing a new drug delivery system using liposomes — artificial phospholipid bilayer vesicles that can encapsulate medications. How does the amphipathic nature of phospholipids make this technology possible, and what advantages do liposomes offer?
    [ ] Liposomes are made of pure protein and deliver drugs by binding to DNA — the phospholipid bilayer has no role in drug delivery technology
    [*] Because phospholipids are amphipathic (hydrophilic heads, hydrophobic tails), they spontaneously self-assemble into bilayer vesicles in aqueous solution — liposomes can encapsulate hydrophilic drugs in their aqueous interior and hydrophobic drugs within the lipid bilayer — advantages include: biocompatibility (made of the same material as natural membranes), reduced side effects (targeted delivery), ability to cross cell membranes via fusion or endocytosis, and protection of fragile drug molecules from degradation
    [ ] Liposomes are solid metal nanoparticles coated with sugar molecules — they have no connection to phospholipid bilayer structure or membrane biology
    [ ] Liposomes work only for topical skin applications — they cannot be used for systemic drug delivery because they dissolve immediately in blood
    Explanation: Liposome drug delivery — applied membrane biology: (1) Self-assembly: amphipathic phospholipids spontaneously form bilayer vesicles (liposomes, 50 nm-5 μm diameter) when dispersed in aqueous solution. Thermodynamically driven to minimize hydrophobic exposure to water. (2) Drug loading: hydrophilic drugs (e.g., doxorubicin for cancer — Doxil®) in aqueous interior; hydrophobic drugs (e.g., amphotericin B for fungal infections — AmBisome®) within lipid bilayer; amphipathic drugs at the bilayer-water interface. (3) Advantages: biocompatible and biodegradable; reduce systemic toxicity (cancer drugs reach tumor preferentially via EPR effect — enhanced permeability and retention); protect drugs from degradation; can be modified with PEG (stealth liposomes — avoid immune clearance) or targeting ligands (antibodies, peptides) for cell-specific delivery. (4) mRNA vaccines (Pfizer/Moderna COVID-19): use lipid nanoparticles (related to liposomes) to deliver mRNA into cells — the lipid bilayer protects fragile mRNA and facilitates cellular uptake via endocytosis.

  Q25. A hospital patient receives an IV of distilled water by mistake instead of isotonic saline (0.9% NaCl). Within minutes, the patient's red blood cells begin to lyse. Explain why this happens in terms of osmosis and the properties of the plasma membrane.
    [ ] Distilled water contains toxins that directly dissolve the plasma membrane — this has nothing to do with osmosis or selective permeability
    [*] Distilled water is strongly hypotonic relative to the intracellular environment of red blood cells — because the plasma membrane is selectively permeable (freely permeable to water via aquaporins but not to dissolved solutes), water moves into the cells by osmosis (from low solute/high water concentration outside to high solute/low water concentration inside) — the influx causes cells to swell and eventually burst (hemolysis) because RBCs lack a rigid cell wall to counteract osmotic pressure
    [ ] The distilled water causes the sodium-potassium pump to work in reverse, pumping too much sodium into the cell and causing it to crystallize
    [ ] Red blood cells lyse because distilled water activates their apoptosis (programmed cell death) pathway — osmosis plays no role
    Explanation: Osmosis and hemolysis: (1) Osmosis: net movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane from a region of lower solute concentration (higher water potential) to higher solute concentration (lower water potential). (2) Tonicity: Hypotonic (outside < inside solute concentration) → water enters cell → swelling. Isotonic (equal) → no net movement → normal shape. Hypertonic (outside > inside) → water exits → shrinkage (crenation in animal cells, plasmolysis in plant cells). (3) This case: distilled water (0 solute) is extremely hypotonic vs. RBC cytoplasm (~300 mOsm). Water floods in through aquaporins (AQP1, abundant in RBCs). RBCs lack a cell wall → swell beyond limit → membrane ruptures → hemolysis (hemoglobin released into blood). (4) Clinical: isotonic saline (0.9% NaCl, ~308 mOsm) matches blood osmolarity → safe for IV. Wrong IV solution → hemolysis → renal failure (free hemoglobin damages kidneys), hyperkalemia (K⁺ released from lysed cells), shock. (5) Plant cell comparison: plant cells in hypotonic solution become turgid but don't burst because the rigid cell wall provides counter-pressure (turgor pressure = wall pressure opposing further water entry).

  Q26. Cystic fibrosis is caused by mutations in the CFTR gene, which encodes a chloride channel protein in the plasma membrane. Patients produce thick, sticky mucus in their lungs. How does a defective channel protein lead to this symptom?
    [ ] CFTR mutations cause the phospholipid bilayer to become impermeable to all gases — patients cannot absorb oxygen, which directly thickens the mucus
    [*] The CFTR protein is normally a chloride (Cl⁻) channel that also regulates sodium (Na⁺) channels in epithelial cell membranes — when CFTR is defective (most commonly the ΔF508 mutation — misfolded protein degraded before reaching membrane), Cl⁻ cannot be secreted, Na⁺ absorption increases, and water follows Na⁺ out of the airway lumen by osmosis — this dehydrates the mucus layer, making it thick and sticky, impairing mucociliary clearance and promoting chronic bacterial infections
    [ ] CFTR is a peripheral protein that produces mucus directly — mutations cause it to overproduce mucus regardless of ion transport
    [ ] The thick mucus is caused by excess cholesterol accumulating in lung cell membranes — CFTR has no connection to ion transport or water balance
    Explanation: Cystic fibrosis and membrane channel proteins: (1) CFTR (cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator): integral membrane protein that functions as a Cl⁻ channel and regulates epithelial Na⁺ channels (ENaC). Located in apical (lumen-facing) membrane of epithelial cells in lungs, pancreas, intestine, sweat glands. (2) Most common mutation: ΔF508 (deletion of phenylalanine at position 508) — causes protein misfolding → degraded by proteasome → never reaches plasma membrane. (3) Consequence in lungs: no Cl⁻ secretion into airway lumen + increased Na⁺ absorption (ENaC unregulated) → water follows Na⁺ out of lumen (osmosis) → dehydrated airway surface liquid → thick, sticky mucus → impaired mucociliary clearance → chronic Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Staphylococcus aureus infections → progressive lung damage. (4) Treatment advances: CFTR modulators (Trikafta® — elexacaftor/tezacaftor/ivacaftor) correct folding and enhance function of ΔF508 CFTR → dramatic clinical improvement. (5) Demonstrates how a single defective channel protein in the plasma membrane can cause systemic disease.

  Q27. In 1970, Frye and Edidin fused human and mouse cells and observed that within 40 minutes, the two types of fluorescently labeled membrane proteins had completely intermixed. What does this experiment demonstrate about the plasma membrane, and which model of membrane structure does it support?
    [ ] The experiment proved that membranes are rigid and that proteins cannot move — supporting the sandwich model where proteins coat a static lipid bilayer
    [*] This experiment demonstrated that membrane proteins can move laterally within the plane of the membrane (lateral diffusion) — supporting Singer and Nicolson's fluid mosaic model (1972) by showing the membrane is a dynamic, fluid structure rather than a static framework — the rapid intermixing of human and mouse proteins (~40 min at 37°C) proved that proteins are not fixed in place but float within the phospholipid bilayer
    [ ] The experiment showed that all membrane proteins are identical in humans and mice — there are no species-specific differences in membrane composition
    [ ] The experiment demonstrated that cell fusion creates a new species — the intermixing of proteins was a permanent genetic change, not a membrane property
    Explanation: Frye-Edidin experiment (1970) — evidence for membrane fluidity: (1) Method: fused human cells (labeled with green fluorescent antibodies against human membrane proteins) with mouse cells (labeled with red fluorescent antibodies against mouse membrane proteins). Used Sendai virus to induce cell fusion → heterokaryon (one cell with two nuclei and a continuous plasma membrane). (2) Results: immediately after fusion, one half of the cell was green, the other half red. After 40 minutes at 37°C, the colors were completely intermixed — even distribution of both human and mouse proteins. At 15°C (below phase transition), intermixing was greatly reduced. (3) Conclusion: membrane proteins undergo lateral diffusion within the lipid bilayer. The membrane is fluid, not static. Temperature dependence confirms that fluidity drives the movement. (4) Significance: provided key experimental support for the fluid mosaic model (Singer & Nicolson, published 1972). Disproved earlier models (e.g., Danielli-Davson) that treated the membrane as a static structure with immobile proteins.

  Q28. A diabetic patient takes insulin injections to control blood glucose. Insulin binds to the insulin receptor (a receptor tyrosine kinase) on the plasma membrane of muscle and fat cells, triggering GLUT4 glucose transporter vesicles to fuse with the membrane. Describe the sequence of membrane events involved.
    [ ] Insulin passes directly through the phospholipid bilayer into the cell and manually carries glucose molecules to the nucleus — no membrane proteins are involved
    [*] Insulin (the ligand) binds to the insulin receptor (an integral transmembrane protein/RTK) on the cell surface → triggers receptor autophosphorylation and intracellular signaling cascade (IRS → PI3K → Akt pathway) → stimulates GLUT4-containing vesicles stored in cytoplasm to translocate and fuse with the plasma membrane via exocytosis → GLUT4 carrier proteins are now exposed on the cell surface → facilitate glucose uptake by facilitated diffusion (passive transport down its concentration gradient) → blood glucose decreases
    [ ] Insulin destroys the plasma membrane temporarily, allowing glucose to flood into the cell through the gap — the membrane reforms after glucose enters
    [ ] Insulin causes active transport of glucose against its concentration gradient using the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase pump — GLUT4 proteins are unnecessary for glucose uptake
    Explanation: Insulin signaling and GLUT4 — membrane biology in diabetes: (1) Insulin: peptide hormone secreted by pancreatic β cells in response to elevated blood glucose. Cannot cross the plasma membrane (too large, polar). (2) Insulin receptor: a receptor tyrosine kinase (RTK) — integral transmembrane protein. Two α subunits (extracellular, bind insulin) + two β subunits (transmembrane, tyrosine kinase activity). (3) Signaling cascade: insulin binds → receptor autophosphorylation → phosphorylates IRS (insulin receptor substrate) → activates PI3K → activates Akt/PKB → signals GLUT4 vesicle translocation. (4) GLUT4: facilitated diffusion glucose carrier stored in intracellular vesicles. Upon insulin signaling, vesicles undergo exocytosis → fuse with plasma membrane → GLUT4 proteins inserted into membrane surface → glucose enters by facilitated diffusion (passive, down concentration gradient). (5) In Type 2 diabetes: insulin resistance — cells respond poorly to insulin → insufficient GLUT4 translocation → impaired glucose uptake → hyperglycemia. (6) Demonstrates: ligand-receptor signaling, signal transduction, exocytosis, and facilitated diffusion — multiple plasma membrane concepts in one physiological process.

  Q29. HIV enters human immune cells by first binding to the CD4 receptor and then to a co-receptor (CCR5 or CXCR4) on the host cell membrane, triggering membrane fusion. Some individuals with a mutation called CCR5-Δ32 are resistant to HIV infection. How does this relate to plasma membrane protein function?
    [ ] HIV enters cells by passive diffusion through the phospholipid bilayer — membrane proteins play no role in viral entry
    [*] HIV uses its envelope glycoprotein gp120 to bind CD4 (an integral membrane receptor protein on T-helper cells), which triggers a conformational change allowing gp120 to bind the co-receptor CCR5 (also an integral membrane protein) — this triggers gp41-mediated fusion of the viral envelope with the host plasma membrane — individuals homozygous for CCR5-Δ32 produce a truncated co-receptor that is not expressed on the cell surface, preventing HIV from completing the fusion step and conferring strong resistance to infection
    [ ] The CCR5-Δ32 mutation makes the phospholipid bilayer impermeable to all viruses by increasing cholesterol content — CD4 is a peripheral protein with no role in HIV entry
    [ ] HIV enters cells only by endocytosis — receptor binding is irrelevant, and CCR5-Δ32 resistance is due to enhanced lysosomal degradation of the virus
    Explanation: HIV entry and membrane proteins: (1) HIV entry steps: gp120 (viral surface glycoprotein) binds CD4 receptor (integral membrane protein on T-helper cells, macrophages, dendritic cells) → conformational change in gp120 exposes co-receptor binding site → gp120 binds CCR5 or CXCR4 (chemokine receptors, 7-transmembrane GPCRs) → conformational change in gp41 (viral transmembrane protein) → fusion peptide of gp41 inserts into host membrane → viral and host membranes fuse → viral capsid enters cell. (2) CCR5-Δ32 mutation: 32 base pair deletion in CCR5 gene → premature stop codon → truncated protein not expressed on cell surface. Homozygotes (Δ32/Δ32, ~1% of European descent): highly resistant to R5-tropic HIV strains. Heterozygotes: slower disease progression. (3) Clinical application: maraviroc (CCR5 antagonist drug) blocks the co-receptor. Timothy Brown ('Berlin patient') — received bone marrow transplant from CCR5-Δ32 homozygous donor → functionally cured of HIV. (4) Demonstrates: how specific integral membrane proteins serve as viral receptors, and how membrane protein mutations can confer disease resistance.

  Q30. Peter Agre received the 2003 Nobel Prize in Chemistry for discovering aquaporins, while Roderick MacKinnon shared the prize for determining the 3D structure of a potassium (K⁺) channel. How do these two types of channel proteins illustrate the concept of selective permeability in the plasma membrane?
    [ ] Aquaporins and K⁺ channels are identical proteins that transport all molecules equally — there is no selectivity in channel protein function
    [*] Aquaporins are exquisitely selective for water (~3×10⁹ molecules/sec) while excluding all ions including H⁺ (due to electrostatic repulsion by positively charged residues in the pore), while K⁺ channels are ~10,000× more selective for K⁺ over Na⁺ (despite Na⁺ being smaller) through a selectivity filter where K⁺ ions shed their hydration shell and interact with carbonyl oxygen atoms spaced to perfectly coordinate K⁺ but not Na⁺ — together, they demonstrate that channel proteins achieve remarkably specific selective permeability through precise structural features at the atomic level
    [ ] Aquaporins transport potassium while K⁺ channels transport water — Agre and MacKinnon discovered that these functions are always reversed from what the protein names suggest
    [ ] Both proteins function only in kidney cells — they have no role in any other cell type, and their selectivity has no broader significance for membrane biology
    Explanation: Aquaporins and K⁺ channels — Nobel Prize 2003: (1) Peter Agre — aquaporins: discovered AQP1 (1992). Water-selective channel: pore diameter ~2.8 Å at narrowest, just wide enough for single-file water. Selectivity mechanism: positively charged asparagine residues (NPA motif) create electrostatic barrier that reorients water molecules and prevents H⁺ (H₃O⁺) passage (blocks proton wire). Rate: ~3×10⁹ water molecules/sec/channel. (2) Roderick MacKinnon — K⁺ channel structure: determined crystal structure of KcsA K⁺ channel from Streptomyces lividans (1998). Selectivity filter: sequence TVGYG with carbonyl oxygens spaced to perfectly coordinate dehydrated K⁺ ions (ionic radius 1.33 Å). Na⁺ (ionic radius 0.95 Å) is too small to interact properly with these oxygens → energetically unfavorable for Na⁺ to shed hydration shell → excluded. K⁺ selectivity: ~10,000:1 over Na⁺. Rate: ~10⁸ K⁺ ions/sec. (3) Combined significance: demonstrate that selective permeability is achieved not just at the membrane level (lipid bilayer blocking ions) but at the individual protein level — each channel protein has evolved exquisite molecular selectivity through precise atomic-level structural features.
