--- Lesson 7.4:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Cellular Transport
Key Concepts: Cellular Transport

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of cellular transport in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to cellular transport
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 7.4:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. Which statement BEST describes diffusion and how it was first observed at the molecular level?
    [ ] Diffusion is active transport that requires ATP to move molecules from low to high concentration — it was first observed by Louis Pasteur in 1880
    [*] Diffusion is the net movement of molecules from an area of higher concentration to an area of lower concentration, driven by the random thermal (kinetic) energy of molecules — Robert Brown (1827) first observed the random motion of pollen particles in water (Brownian motion), and Adolf Fick (1855) formulated the mathematical laws of diffusion (Fick's Laws) that quantify the rate of diffusion based on concentration gradient, temperature, and molecular size
    [ ] Diffusion only occurs in gases and requires a vacuum — it was first described by Isaac Newton in his work on gravitational forces
    [ ] Diffusion is the movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane — it was first studied by Gregor Mendel in his pea plant experiments
    Explanation: Diffusion: the net movement of molecules (or ions) from a region of higher concentration to lower concentration. Driven by thermal kinetic energy (Brownian motion) — molecules move randomly and the statistical result is net movement down the gradient. History: Robert Brown (1827) observed random motion of pollen grains in water → Brownian motion. Adolf Fick (1855) → Fick's First Law: J = -D(dC/dx) — flux proportional to concentration gradient. Factors affecting diffusion rate: concentration gradient (steeper = faster), temperature (higher = faster — more kinetic energy), molecular size (smaller = faster), medium (faster in gas than liquid than solid). Diffusion is passive (no energy input), spontaneous, and continues until dynamic equilibrium is reached (molecules still move but no NET movement).

  Q2. Which statement BEST describes osmosis and its significance in biological systems?
    [ ] Osmosis is the active transport of solutes across a membrane requiring ATP — it works by pumping sodium and potassium ions against their gradients
    [*] Osmosis is the net diffusion of water (solvent) across a selectively permeable membrane from a region of lower solute concentration (higher water potential) to a region of higher solute concentration (lower water potential) — it requires no energy input and is driven by differences in water potential — osmotic pressure was quantified by Jacobus Henricus van 't Hoff (Nobel Prize in Chemistry, 1901) with the equation π = iMRT
    [ ] Osmosis is the movement of any molecule through any membrane regardless of concentration — it was discovered by Charles Darwin
    [ ] Osmosis only occurs in plant cells and has no relevance to animal cell physiology or medicine
    Explanation: Osmosis: the net movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane from a region of lower solute concentration to higher solute concentration. Key concepts: Water potential (Ψ) = pressure potential (Ψp) + solute potential (Ψs). Pure water has Ψ = 0. Adding solute decreases water potential (Ψs is negative). Water flows from high Ψ to low Ψ. Osmotic pressure (π): pressure required to stop osmotic flow. Van 't Hoff equation: π = iMRT (i = van 't Hoff factor/ionization, M = molarity, R = gas constant, T = temperature in Kelvin). Jacobus Henricus van 't Hoff — first Nobel Prize in Chemistry (1901) for this work. Biological significance: kidney function (countercurrent multiplier, collecting duct aquaporins regulated by ADH), red blood cell integrity (isotonic saline for IV fluids), plant turgor pressure (drives cell expansion and growth), guard cell function (stomatal opening/closing via K⁺-driven osmosis).

  Q3. What is the fundamental difference between passive transport and active transport across the plasma membrane?
    [ ] Passive transport uses ATP while active transport does not — both move molecules from high to low concentration
    [*] Passive transport moves substances DOWN their concentration gradient WITHOUT energy input (e.g., simple diffusion, facilitated diffusion, osmosis) — active transport moves substances AGAINST their concentration gradient and REQUIRES energy, typically from ATP hydrolysis (primary active transport) or from coupling to an existing electrochemical gradient (secondary active transport/cotransport)
    [ ] There is no difference — both passive and active transport require ATP and move molecules against their gradient
    [ ] Passive transport only occurs in prokaryotes, while active transport only occurs in eukaryotes
    Explanation: Passive vs. active transport: (1) Passive transport — no energy required, molecules move down their concentration (or electrochemical) gradient. Types: Simple diffusion (small nonpolar molecules cross lipid bilayer directly — O₂, CO₂, N₂, steroid hormones). Facilitated diffusion (polar/charged molecules move through channel or carrier proteins — glucose via GLUT1, ions via ion channels). Osmosis (water via aquaporins or through bilayer). (2) Active transport — requires energy, moves molecules against their gradient. Primary active transport: uses ATP directly (Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase, Ca²⁺ ATPase, H⁺/K⁺ ATPase). Secondary active transport (cotransport): uses energy stored in an electrochemical gradient established by primary active transport. Symport: both molecules move same direction (Na⁺-glucose SGLT1). Antiport: molecules move opposite directions (Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger). (3) Vesicular transport: endocytosis and exocytosis — also require ATP.

  Q4. How does facilitated diffusion differ from simple diffusion, and what types of membrane proteins are involved?
    [ ] Facilitated diffusion requires ATP and moves molecules against their concentration gradient — simple diffusion uses membrane proteins
    [*] Facilitated diffusion uses specific membrane transport proteins (channel proteins like aquaporins and ion channels, or carrier proteins like GLUT1 glucose transporter) to help polar molecules and ions cross the hydrophobic lipid bilayer — it is still passive (no ATP needed, moves down the gradient) but is faster and more specific than simple diffusion — it also shows saturation kinetics because the number of transport proteins is limited
    [ ] There is no difference between facilitated and simple diffusion — both involve molecules passing directly through the phospholipid bilayer
    [ ] Facilitated diffusion only occurs in plant cells and uses the cell wall rather than membrane proteins
    Explanation: Facilitated diffusion: passive transport that requires specific membrane proteins. Two types of transport proteins: (1) Channel proteins — form hydrophilic pores through the membrane. Ion channels: voltage-gated (respond to membrane potential changes), ligand-gated (respond to chemical signals), mechanically-gated (respond to physical forces). Aquaporins: water channels (~3×10⁹ H₂O/sec). Fast transport, not highly selective beyond size/charge of the pore. (2) Carrier proteins — bind specific solute → conformational change → release on other side. Slower than channels. Examples: GLUT1-4 glucose transporters, amino acid transporters. Show saturation kinetics (Vmax when all carriers occupied — similar to enzyme kinetics). Key differences from simple diffusion: requires proteins (specificity), shows saturation, can be regulated (channels open/close, carriers can be inserted/removed from membrane). Still passive: no ATP, moves down gradient.

  Q5. What is a concentration gradient, and why is it considered the driving force for passive transport?
    [ ] A concentration gradient is when all molecules are evenly distributed — it requires ATP to maintain and drives active transport exclusively
    [*] A concentration gradient is the difference in the concentration of a substance between two areas (such as inside and outside a cell) — it represents stored potential energy because molecules at higher concentration have a thermodynamic tendency to move toward lower concentration — this gradient provides the driving force for all forms of passive transport (simple diffusion, facilitated diffusion, osmosis) without requiring cellular energy input
    [ ] A concentration gradient only exists in gases and has no relevance to transport across biological membranes
    [ ] A concentration gradient is a type of membrane protein that transports ions — it was discovered by Watson and Crick in 1953
    Explanation: Concentration gradient: a difference in the concentration of a substance across a space (e.g., across a membrane). Higher concentration → lower concentration = 'down the gradient.' Lower concentration → higher concentration = 'against the gradient.' Thermodynamic basis: the second law of thermodynamics — systems tend toward maximum entropy (disorder). Molecules at high concentration are more ordered → spontaneously move toward lower concentration (increasing entropy) → releases free energy (ΔG < 0 for passive transport). For ions, the electrochemical gradient combines both the concentration gradient and the electrical gradient (membrane potential) — the Nernst equation calculates the equilibrium potential for each ion. The gradient is a form of potential energy: cells invest ATP (via Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase and other pumps) to create and maintain gradients → these gradients then drive secondary active transport, nerve impulses, and other vital processes without additional ATP expenditure.

  Q6. What happens to a cell placed in a hypertonic, hypotonic, or isotonic solution, and how does tonicity relate to osmosis?
    [ ] Tonicity has no effect on cells — cells maintain the same shape regardless of the surrounding solution's concentration
    [*] Hypertonic solution (more solute outside): water leaves cell by osmosis → cell shrinks (crenation in animal cells, plasmolysis in plant cells). Hypotonic solution (less solute outside): water enters cell → cell swells (lysis/hemolysis in animal cells; turgid in plant cells because the rigid cell wall resists over-expansion). Isotonic solution (equal solute): no net water movement → cell maintains normal shape and volume
    [ ] In a hypertonic solution, water rushes into the cell because the cell has less solute — in a hypotonic solution, water leaves the cell
    [ ] Tonicity only affects plant cells — animal cells are immune to changes in solution concentration because they lack a cell wall
    Explanation: Tonicity: the ability of a solution to cause a cell to gain or lose water. Depends on the concentration of non-penetrating solutes (solutes that cannot cross the membrane). Hypertonic (more solute outside, less water outside): water leaves cell → animal cell: crenation (shriveling). Plant cell: plasmolysis (membrane pulls away from cell wall). Example: wilting lettuce in salted water. Hypotonic (less solute outside, more water outside): water enters cell → animal cell: swells → may lyse (burst). Plant cell: becomes turgid (cell wall provides counter-pressure, turgor pressure). Optimal for plant cells. Example: hemolysis of RBCs in distilled water. Isotonic (equal solute): no net water movement → normal cell volume. Example: 0.9% NaCl (normal saline) for IV fluids. Key distinction: tonicity considers only non-penetrating solutes; osmolarity includes all solutes. A solution can be hyperosmotic but isotonic if the excess solute is penetrating (e.g., urea crosses membrane freely).

  Q7. How does the sodium-potassium pump (Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase) work, and why is it critical for cell function?
    [ ] The Na⁺/K⁺ pump moves sodium and potassium in the same direction using passive diffusion — it does not require ATP
    [*] The Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase is a primary active transport pump that uses 1 ATP per cycle to transport 3 Na⁺ out and 2 K⁺ into the cell against their concentration gradients — this creates an electrochemical gradient (inside negative, ~-70 mV) essential for nerve impulse transmission, muscle contraction, secondary active transport (Na⁺-glucose cotransporter), and maintaining cell volume — it was discovered by Jens Christian Skou (Nobel Prize in Chemistry, 1997) and consumes ~25% of a cell's total ATP
    [ ] The Na⁺/K⁺ pump is a channel protein that allows both sodium and potassium to flow freely down their gradients — no energy is required
    [ ] The pump only functions in kidney cells and has no role in nerve, muscle, or other cell types
    Explanation: Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (sodium-potassium pump): (1) Mechanism: E1 state — 3 Na⁺ bind intracellular sites → ATP hydrolyzed → phosphorylation triggers conformational change → E2 state — 3 Na⁺ released extracellularly → 2 K⁺ bind extracellular sites → dephosphorylation → return to E1 state → 2 K⁺ released intracellularly. Net: 3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in per ATP. (2) Electrogenic: moves net +1 charge out per cycle → contributes to membrane potential (~-70 mV resting potential in neurons). (3) Functions: maintains ion gradients for nerve impulses (action potentials require Na⁺ and K⁺ gradients); powers secondary active transport (Na⁺ gradient drives glucose, amino acid uptake via symporters); regulates cell volume (controls intracellular osmolarity); maintains low intracellular Ca²⁺ (indirectly via Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger). (4) Energy cost: ~25% of cellular ATP in most cells; ~70% in neurons during active signaling. (5) Discovery: Jens Christian Skou (1957), Nobel Prize in Chemistry (1997). (6) Medical relevance: cardiac glycosides (digoxin, ouabain) inhibit the pump → used to treat heart failure.

  Q8. What is the difference between endocytosis and exocytosis, and what are the main types of endocytosis?
    [ ] Endocytosis and exocytosis are identical processes — both involve releasing materials from the cell by fusing vesicles with the membrane
    [*] Endocytosis brings material INTO the cell by infolding the plasma membrane to form vesicles — three types: phagocytosis ('cell eating,' engulfs large particles via pseudopods), pinocytosis ('cell drinking,' nonspecific uptake of extracellular fluid/dissolved solutes), and receptor-mediated endocytosis (selective, ligand-receptor binding → clathrin-coated pits → highly efficient uptake of specific molecules like LDL cholesterol) — exocytosis releases material FROM the cell by fusing intracellular vesicles with the plasma membrane (secretion of hormones, neurotransmitters, enzymes)
    [ ] Endocytosis is passive transport that requires no energy, while exocytosis is the only form of active transport in cells
    [ ] These processes only occur in plant cells and are not found in animal cells or single-celled organisms
    Explanation: Vesicular transport (bulk transport): Endocytosis (bringing material in): (1) Phagocytosis ('cell eating'): cell extends pseudopods → engulfs large particles (bacteria, dead cells, debris) → forms phagosome → fuses with lysosome → contents digested. Performed by specialized cells: macrophages, neutrophils, dendritic cells, amoebae. (2) Pinocytosis ('cell drinking'): small invaginations of membrane capture extracellular fluid and dissolved solutes → small vesicles. Nonspecific and continuous in most cells. (3) Receptor-mediated endocytosis: most selective and efficient. Specific receptors bind ligands → receptors cluster in clathrin-coated pits → pit invaginates → clathrin-coated vesicle → uncoating → early endosome → sorting (receptor recycled to surface; ligand sent to lysosome). Example: LDL receptor pathway (Brown & Goldstein, Nobel 1985). Exocytosis (releasing material): vesicles from Golgi apparatus or secretory granules → transported to plasma membrane → SNARE proteins mediate vesicle docking and membrane fusion → contents released extracellularly + vesicle membrane incorporated into plasma membrane. Examples: neurotransmitter release at synapses, insulin secretion from β cells, mucus secretion. Both endocytosis and exocytosis require ATP.

  Q9. How do plant cells and animal cells respond differently when placed in a hypotonic solution, and what role does the cell wall play?
    [ ] Both plant and animal cells burst immediately in hypotonic solutions — the cell wall provides no protection whatsoever
    [*] In a hypotonic solution, water enters both cell types by osmosis — animal cells swell and may lyse (burst) because they lack a rigid cell wall — plant cells swell and become turgid but do NOT burst because the rigid cellulose cell wall provides counter-pressure (turgor pressure) that opposes further water entry — turgor pressure is essential for plant structural support, cell growth, and the opening/closing of stomatal guard cells
    [ ] Plant cells shrink in hypotonic solutions while animal cells swell — the cell wall has no effect on osmotic behavior
    [ ] Animal cells are never exposed to hypotonic solutions in nature, so this comparison is purely theoretical
    Explanation: Plant vs. animal cells in hypotonic solutions: (1) Animal cells: no cell wall → only the flexible plasma membrane → water enters by osmosis → cell swells → if pressure exceeds membrane strength → cell lyses (bursts). Example: hemolysis of RBCs in distilled water. Animals maintain isotonic body fluids to prevent this (kidneys regulate blood osmolarity, ~300 mOsm). (2) Plant cells: rigid cellulose cell wall outside the plasma membrane → water enters by osmosis → cell swells → plasma membrane pushes against cell wall → wall exerts equal and opposite pressure (wall pressure = turgor pressure) → equilibrium reached → cell becomes turgid but does not burst. Turgor pressure functions: structural support (wilting = loss of turgor pressure in drought), cell growth (turgor pressure drives cell expansion — acid growth hypothesis: auxin → H⁺ ATPase → wall loosening → turgor-driven expansion), stomatal regulation (guard cells: K⁺ uptake → water follows osmotically → cells swell → stomata open). (3) Plasmolysis: in hypertonic solution, water leaves plant cell → membrane pulls away from cell wall → plasmolysis → cell death if prolonged.

  Q10. What is secondary active transport (cotransport), and how does it use the Na⁺ gradient established by the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase?
    [ ] Secondary active transport uses ATP directly to move two molecules at the same time — it does not depend on ion gradients
    [*] Secondary active transport (cotransport) uses the electrochemical gradient of one substance (usually Na⁺, established by the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase) to drive the transport of another substance against its own gradient — without directly using ATP — symport moves both substances in the same direction (e.g., Na⁺-glucose cotransporter SGLT1 in intestinal cells), while antiport moves them in opposite directions (e.g., Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger in cardiac cells)
    [ ] Secondary active transport only involves potassium channels and has nothing to do with sodium gradients or cotransporters
    [ ] Secondary active transport is another name for simple diffusion — no proteins or gradients are involved
    Explanation: Secondary active transport (cotransport): (1) Principle: the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (primary active transport) creates a steep Na⁺ gradient (high Na⁺ outside, low inside). This gradient stores potential energy. Cotransporters use the energy released by Na⁺ flowing DOWN its gradient to move another molecule AGAINST its gradient — no direct ATP use, but ultimately depends on ATP (via the pump maintaining the Na⁺ gradient). (2) Symport (same direction): Na⁺-glucose cotransporter (SGLT1): Na⁺ moving in drives glucose absorption against its gradient in small intestine and kidney tubules. Na⁺-amino acid cotransporters: similar mechanism for amino acid absorption. (3) Antiport (opposite directions): Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger (NCX): 3 Na⁺ in drives 1 Ca²⁺ out — critical in cardiac muscle (regulates intracellular Ca²⁺ for contraction). Na⁺-H⁺ exchanger (NHE): Na⁺ in, H⁺ out — regulates intracellular pH. (4) Clinical: SGLT2 inhibitors (empagliflozin, dapagliflozin) — block glucose reabsorption in kidney → glucose excreted in urine → used to treat Type 2 diabetes.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes diffusion?
    [ ] The active transport of solute molecules across a membrane using ATP to move them from low concentration to high concentration
    [*] The net movement of molecules (or ions) from an area of higher concentration to an area of lower concentration due to random thermal motion — diffusion is passive (requires no energy input), spontaneous, and continues until dynamic equilibrium is reached — the rate depends on the concentration gradient, temperature, molecular size, and the medium
    [ ] The process by which cells engulf large particles by folding the membrane inward to form vesicles
    [ ] The specific movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane from low solute concentration to high solute concentration
    Explanation: Diffusion: the net passive movement of molecules from high to low concentration. Driven by random thermal (kinetic) energy — Brownian motion (Robert Brown, 1827). Quantified by Fick's First Law: J = −D(dC/dx), where J = flux, D = diffusion coefficient, dC/dx = concentration gradient. Factors: steeper gradient = faster diffusion; higher temperature = faster (more kinetic energy); smaller molecules = faster; shorter distance = faster. Reaches dynamic equilibrium: molecules continue to move randomly, but no NET change in concentration. Types of diffusion across membranes: simple diffusion (small nonpolar molecules through lipid bilayer — O₂, CO₂, N₂); facilitated diffusion (polar/charged molecules through protein channels or carriers — glucose, ions).

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes osmosis?
    [ ] The active pumping of sodium and potassium ions across the membrane using ATP hydrolysis
    [*] The net diffusion of water (solvent) across a selectively permeable membrane from a region of lower solute concentration (higher water potential) to a region of higher solute concentration (lower water potential) — osmosis is passive, requires no energy, and is driven by differences in water potential — it determines cell behavior in hypertonic, hypotonic, and isotonic solutions
    [ ] The movement of all dissolved molecules from high to low concentration through any type of barrier, regardless of permeability
    [ ] The process of vesicle fusion with the plasma membrane to release stored contents outside the cell
    Explanation: Osmosis: the net movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane from low solute concentration to high solute concentration. The membrane is permeable to water (via aquaporins and slowly through the bilayer) but impermeable to most solutes. Driven by water potential (Ψ): Ψ = Ψs (solute potential, always ≤ 0) + Ψp (pressure potential). Water flows from high Ψ to low Ψ. Osmotic pressure (π = iMRT, van 't Hoff equation). Clinical relevance: isotonic saline (0.9% NaCl, ~308 mOsm) for IV fluids; dialysis uses osmosis principles; kidney concentrates urine via osmotic gradients in the medulla.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes active transport?
    [ ] The spontaneous movement of molecules from high concentration to low concentration without energy input — also called diffusion
    [*] The movement of substances across the plasma membrane against their concentration gradient (from low to high concentration), requiring energy input — primary active transport uses ATP directly (e.g., Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase, Ca²⁺ ATPase) while secondary active transport uses an electrochemical gradient established by primary active transport to drive cotransport of another substance (e.g., Na⁺-glucose symporter SGLT1)
    [ ] The formation of vesicles around extracellular fluid for nonspecific cellular uptake
    [ ] The movement of water across a membrane from low solute concentration to high solute concentration
    Explanation: Active transport: movement of substances against their concentration (or electrochemical) gradient — requires energy. Primary active transport: ATP hydrolysis drives conformational changes in transport proteins. Examples: Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in — Skou, Nobel 1997), Ca²⁺ ATPase (removes Ca²⁺ from cytoplasm after muscle contraction), H⁺/K⁺ ATPase (secretes stomach acid), ABC transporters (MDR1/P-glycoprotein — drug efflux in cancer resistance). Secondary active transport: couples 'downhill' movement of one ion (usually Na⁺) to 'uphill' movement of another substance. Symport (same direction): SGLT1 (glucose), Na⁺-amino acid transporters. Antiport (opposite directions): Na⁺-Ca²⁺ exchanger (NCX), Na⁺-H⁺ exchanger (NHE). Cells spend ~25-40% of their ATP on active transport; neurons during signaling: ~70%.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes endocytosis?
    [ ] The process of fusing intracellular vesicles with the plasma membrane to release their contents to the outside of the cell
    [*] The process by which cells take in large molecules, particles, or fluids by engulfing them with the plasma membrane to form intracellular vesicles — types include phagocytosis (engulfing large particles like bacteria), pinocytosis (nonspecific uptake of extracellular fluid and solutes), and receptor-mediated endocytosis (highly selective uptake via clathrin-coated pits, e.g., LDL cholesterol uptake)
    [ ] The passive diffusion of small nonpolar molecules like oxygen and carbon dioxide through the phospholipid bilayer
    [ ] The movement of water across a selectively permeable membrane from low solute to high solute concentration
    Explanation: Endocytosis: bringing material into the cell via membrane-enclosed vesicles. Requires ATP. Three types: (1) Phagocytosis ('cell eating'): pseudopods extend around large particles (bacteria, dead cells) → phagosome → fuses with lysosome → digestion. Immune cells: macrophages (~200 bacteria/hour), neutrophils, dendritic cells. Also: amoebae use for feeding. (2) Pinocytosis ('cell drinking'): small invaginations capture extracellular fluid + dissolved solutes. Nonspecific, continuous. All cells perform pinocytosis. (3) Receptor-mediated endocytosis: ligands bind specific receptors → cluster in clathrin-coated pits → invagination → clathrin-coated vesicle → uncoating → early endosome → sorting. Most selective and efficient. Classic example: LDL receptor pathway (Goldstein & Brown, Nobel 1985). Also: transferrin (iron uptake), insulin, growth factors.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes exocytosis?
    [ ] The process of bringing materials into the cell by folding the plasma membrane inward to form vesicles
    [*] The process by which cells export or release large molecules by fusing intracellular vesicles with the plasma membrane — the vesicle membrane merges with the cell membrane (mediated by SNARE proteins) and the contents are released into the extracellular space — examples include secretion of neurotransmitters at synapses, hormone release (insulin from β cells), and mucus secretion by goblet cells
    [ ] The pumping of sodium ions out of the cell and potassium ions into the cell using ATP energy
    [ ] The breakdown of glucose in the cytoplasm during glycolysis to produce ATP
    Explanation: Exocytosis: releasing material from the cell via vesicle-membrane fusion. Requires ATP and SNARE proteins. Process: secretory vesicles (from Golgi apparatus or specialized granules) transported to plasma membrane via motor proteins (kinesins along microtubules) → vesicle SNARE (v-SNARE/synaptobrevin) binds target membrane SNARE (t-SNARE/syntaxin + SNAP-25) → SNARE complex pulls membranes together → membrane fusion → contents released extracellularly. Vesicle membrane incorporated into plasma membrane (adds lipid and protein to cell surface). Types: constitutive exocytosis (continuous, default pathway — membrane maintenance, ECM protein secretion) and regulated exocytosis (triggered by signal, usually Ca²⁺ — neurotransmitter release, hormone secretion). Examples: neurotransmitter release (~1 ms at synapses), insulin secretion by pancreatic β cells (glucose-stimulated), mucus secretion, antibody secretion by plasma cells. Balances endocytosis to maintain membrane surface area.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes a concentration gradient?
    [ ] A uniform distribution of molecules where no difference in concentration exists between two regions
    [*] A difference in the concentration of a substance between two areas (such as inside and outside a cell) — it represents stored potential energy because molecules at higher concentration have a thermodynamic tendency to move toward lower concentration — this gradient is the driving force for all forms of passive transport and is maintained by active transport pumps like the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase
    [ ] A type of membrane protein that transports glucose into cells via active transport
    [ ] The rate at which enzymes catalyze chemical reactions inside the cell, unrelated to molecular movement
    Explanation: Concentration gradient: the difference in concentration of a substance between two regions. Functions as stored potential energy because the second law of thermodynamics drives molecules from high to low concentration (increasing entropy). This gradient powers: simple diffusion, facilitated diffusion, osmosis (all passive — 'downhill'). For ions, the electrochemical gradient = concentration gradient + electrical gradient. The Nernst equation gives the equilibrium potential for each ion. Cells invest ATP to create and maintain gradients (Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase maintains high Na⁺ outside, high K⁺ inside) → these gradients then power secondary active transport, nerve impulses, muscle contraction, and other vital processes.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes facilitated diffusion?
    [ ] The movement of molecules against their concentration gradient using ATP energy, similar to active transport
    [*] A form of passive transport in which polar molecules, ions, or large molecules cross the plasma membrane with the help of specific membrane transport proteins (channel proteins or carrier proteins) — it requires no energy input, moves substances DOWN their concentration gradient, but is faster and more selective than simple diffusion and shows saturation kinetics due to the limited number of available transport proteins
    [ ] The direct passage of small nonpolar molecules like O₂ and CO₂ through the phospholipid bilayer without any protein assistance
    [ ] The process of engulfing large particles by wrapping the cell membrane around them to form intracellular vesicles
    Explanation: Facilitated diffusion: passive transport assisted by membrane proteins. Still moves molecules DOWN the gradient, no ATP required. Two protein types: (1) Channel proteins — form pores/tunnels. Ion channels: voltage-gated, ligand-gated, mechanically-gated. Aquaporins: water channels (AQP1-13). Fast (~10⁷-10⁹ ions/sec for ion channels). (2) Carrier proteins — bind solute, conformational change, release on other side. Slower (~10²-10⁴ molecules/sec). GLUT transporters (GLUT1-5 for glucose). Amino acid transporters. Key properties: specificity (each protein transports specific molecules), saturation (rate plateaus at Vmax when all proteins occupied — similar to Michaelis-Menten enzyme kinetics), regulation (can be opened/closed, inserted/removed from membrane). Example: GLUT4 insertion in response to insulin signaling.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes tonicity?
    [ ] The total concentration of all solutes in a solution, regardless of whether they can cross the membrane
    [*] The ability of a surrounding solution to cause a cell to gain or lose water — determined by the concentration of non-penetrating solutes (solutes that cannot cross the membrane) — a hypertonic solution causes water loss and cell shrinkage, a hypotonic solution causes water gain and cell swelling, and an isotonic solution results in no net water movement
    [ ] The speed at which molecules diffuse across a membrane, measured in molecules per second
    [ ] The electrical charge difference across the plasma membrane, also known as the membrane potential
    Explanation: Tonicity: a measure of a solution's ability to cause a cell to gain or lose water. Depends ONLY on non-penetrating solutes (molecules that cannot cross the membrane — e.g., NaCl, sucrose). Distinguished from osmolarity (total solute concentration including penetrating solutes). Hypertonic: cell shrinks (more non-penetrating solute outside). Animal cells → crenation. Plant cells → plasmolysis. Hypotonic: cell swells (less non-penetrating solute outside). Animal cells → may lyse. Plant cells → turgid (cell wall prevents bursting). Isotonic: no net water movement. Example: 0.9% NaCl, 5% dextrose (penetrating) = isotonic to blood. Clinical: IV fluids must be isotonic to prevent hemolysis or crenation. Key subtlety: a solution can be hyperosmotic but isotonic if the excess solute penetrates the membrane (e.g., urea).

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes plasmolysis?
    [ ] The bursting of a plant cell when placed in a hypotonic solution due to excessive water intake
    [*] The shrinkage of the cell membrane away from the cell wall in a plant cell placed in a hypertonic solution — as water leaves the cell by osmosis, the cytoplasm contracts and the plasma membrane detaches from the rigid cell wall — plasmolysis causes wilting in plants and can lead to cell death if prolonged — it is reversible in early stages if the cell is returned to a hypotonic environment
    [ ] The process of a cell dividing into two daughter cells during mitosis
    [ ] The active pumping of water out of a cell using ATP energy, found exclusively in animal cells
    Explanation: Plasmolysis: contraction of the protoplast (cell contents + membrane) away from the cell wall in a plant cell placed in a hypertonic environment. Mechanism: hypertonic solution (more solute outside) → water leaves cell by osmosis → cell volume decreases → plasma membrane detaches from cell wall (cell wall is rigid, does not shrink with the membrane) → space between membrane and wall fills with the hypertonic solution. Stages: incipient plasmolysis (membrane just beginning to detach) → evident plasmolysis → convex plasmolysis (severe, protoplast rounded). Reversible in early stages (deplasmolysis) if cells returned to hypotonic solution. Biological significance: explains wilting of plants in drought or salty soil. Lab demonstration: Elodea leaf in concentrated salt or sucrose solution — observe under microscope. Opposite: turgor/turgidity — water enters hypotonic solution → membrane pushes against cell wall → turgor pressure → cell rigid and upright.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes turgor pressure?
    [ ] The pressure exerted by the cell membrane on the extracellular fluid in an animal cell, causing it to swell and burst
    [*] The pressure exerted by the cell contents (water-filled vacuole and cytoplasm) pushing outward against the rigid cell wall in plant cells — generated when water enters the cell by osmosis in a hypotonic environment — turgor pressure is essential for maintaining plant structural rigidity, driving cell elongation during growth, and controlling stomatal opening and closing via guard cells
    [ ] The force generated by ATP hydrolysis during active transport of sodium and potassium ions
    [ ] The pressure created when gases diffuse across the alveolar membrane in the lungs during respiration
    Explanation: Turgor pressure (pressure potential, Ψp): the pressure of the cell contents pushing outward against the cell wall. Generated by osmotic water uptake: hypotonic environment → water enters by osmosis → cell swells → membrane and contents push against rigid cell wall → wall pushes back (wall pressure) → equilibrium. Functions: structural support (non-woody plants rely on turgor for upright posture — wilting = loss of turgor), cell growth (acid growth hypothesis: auxin activates H⁺ ATPase → cell wall loosens → turgor drives expansion), stomatal regulation (guard cells: K⁺ pumped in → water follows by osmosis → guard cells swell and curve apart → stoma opens; K⁺ leaves → water follows → guard cells deflate → stoma closes). Water potential equation: Ψ = Ψs + Ψp. At full turgor: Ψp is positive and large, offsetting negative Ψs → Ψ approaches 0 → no further net water entry.

  Q21. A hospital patient accidentally receives an IV drip of distilled water instead of isotonic saline (0.9% NaCl). The patient rapidly develops hemolysis and hemoglobinuria (hemoglobin in the urine). Using your knowledge of osmosis and tonicity, explain the sequence of events from IV infusion to kidney damage.
    [ ] Distilled water is hypertonic — it causes red blood cells to shrink and crenate, leading to clotting in the kidneys
    [*] Distilled water is extremely hypotonic (0 mOsm) relative to blood (~300 mOsm) — water enters red blood cells rapidly by osmosis through aquaporin-1 channels → RBCs swell beyond membrane capacity → hemolysis (membrane ruptures) → free hemoglobin released into plasma → hemoglobin is filtered by kidney glomeruli but is toxic to renal tubular epithelial cells → acute tubular necrosis → potential acute kidney injury — additionally, potassium released from lysed cells → hyperkalemia → risk of cardiac arrhythmia
    [ ] Distilled water kills bacteria in the blood, which then release toxins that attack the kidneys — this has nothing to do with osmosis
    [ ] Distilled water is isotonic to blood — the hemolysis must be caused by a contamination in the IV tubing rather than a tonicity effect
    Explanation: Hypotonic hemolysis — clinical scenario: (1) Distilled water (0 mOsm) vs. blood (~300 mOsm) → extremely hypotonic. (2) Water enters RBCs via AQP1 aquaporins and slowly through the lipid bilayer → osmotic swelling. RBCs have no cell wall, cannot resist over-expansion. (3) Hemolysis: membrane ruptures → hemoglobin (Hb) released into plasma. Free Hb: saturates haptoglobin binding capacity → excess free Hb filtered by kidneys → Hb precipitates in renal tubules (especially under acidic conditions) → mechanical obstruction + direct toxicity to tubular epithelial cells → acute tubular necrosis → acute kidney injury (AKI). (4) Hyperkalemia: lysed RBCs release intracellular K⁺ ([K⁺]ᵢ ≈ 140 mM) → plasma [K⁺] rises → disrupts cardiac membrane potential → risk of fatal arrhythmia. (5) Prevention: all IV fluids must be isotonic (0.9% NaCl = 308 mOsm, Ringer's lactate = 273 mOsm) or carefully formulated to avoid osmotic cell damage. (6) Plant cell comparison: would not burst due to cell wall providing turgor pressure.

  Q22. A student places three groups of de-shelled eggs (using vinegar to dissolve the shell, leaving only the semipermeable membrane) into: (A) distilled water, (B) corn syrup, and (C) isotonic saline. After 24 hours, she measures mass changes. Predict and explain the results using osmosis principles.
    [ ] All three eggs will remain unchanged because de-shelled eggs cannot undergo osmosis — the semipermeable membrane blocks all water movement
    [*] Egg A (distilled water): gains mass and swells — water enters by osmosis because distilled water is hypotonic relative to egg contents. Egg B (corn syrup): loses mass and shrinks — water exits by osmosis because corn syrup is hypertonic (high sugar concentration). Egg C (isotonic saline): mass stays approximately the same — no net water movement because solute concentration is roughly equal inside and outside
    [ ] Egg A shrinks, Egg B swells, and Egg C dissolves — distilled water pulls water out of cells by reverse osmosis while corn syrup pushes water in
    [ ] All three eggs dissolve because removing the shell eliminates all protection — the semipermeable membrane cannot maintain osmotic balance
    Explanation: De-shelled egg osmosis experiment: (1) Preparation: vinegar (acetic acid) dissolves CaCO₃ shell → exposes the semipermeable vitelline/inner membrane. (2) Egg A (distilled water — hypotonic): water enters egg by osmosis (high water potential outside → low water potential inside) → egg swells, mass increases (~20-30%). Membrane may feel taut. (3) Egg B (corn syrup — hypertonic, ~70% sugar): water exits egg by osmosis (low water potential in corn syrup due to high solute) → egg shrinks, mass decreases (~20-30%). Membrane wrinkled, egg feels soft and smaller. (4) Egg C (isotonic saline — ~equal osmolarity): no net water movement → mass ~unchanged. (5) This is a classic AP Biology lab demonstrating: osmosis, tonicity, selectively permeable membranes, and the relationship between solute concentration and water movement. Quantitative analysis: percent change in mass = (final mass − initial mass)/initial mass × 100.

  Q23. SGLT2 inhibitors (e.g., empagliflozin, dapagliflozin) are a class of diabetes drugs that block the sodium-glucose cotransporter 2 in the kidney proximal tubule. Explain how blocking this secondary active transporter helps lower blood glucose levels.
    [ ] SGLT2 inhibitors increase insulin production from pancreatic β cells — they have nothing to do with glucose transport in the kidney
    [*] Normally, SGLT2 (a Na⁺-glucose symporter in the kidney proximal tubule) reabsorbs ~90% of filtered glucose back into the blood using the Na⁺ gradient established by the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (secondary active transport) — SGLT2 inhibitors block this cotransporter → glucose remains in the tubular fluid → excreted in urine (glycosuria) → blood glucose levels decrease — this mechanism is independent of insulin, making these drugs effective even in advanced Type 2 diabetes with severe insulin resistance
    [ ] SGLT2 inhibitors work by increasing glucose diffusion across all cell membranes — they eliminate the need for both insulin and GLUT transporters entirely
    [ ] These drugs prevent glucose absorption in the intestines by blocking GLUT2 transporters — kidney function is not involved
    Explanation: SGLT2 inhibitors and secondary active transport: (1) Normal kidney glucose handling: ~180 g glucose filtered by glomeruli daily → nearly all reabsorbed in proximal tubule. SGLT2 (in early proximal tubule): low affinity, high capacity, reabsorbs ~90% (1 Na⁺: 1 glucose symport). SGLT1 (in late proximal tubule): high affinity, low capacity, reabsorbs remaining ~10% (2 Na⁺: 1 glucose). Both powered by Na⁺ gradient from basolateral Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase. Glucose exits tubular cells via GLUT2 (basolateral). (2) SGLT2 inhibitors (gliflozins): block SGLT2 → ~70-80 g glucose/day excreted in urine → lowers blood glucose by ~30-50 mg/dL. (3) Additional benefits: weight loss (caloric loss via glucosuria), blood pressure reduction (osmotic diuresis, natriuresis), cardiovascular protection (reduced heart failure hospitalization — landmark EMPA-REG OUTCOME trial for empagliflozin), kidney protection. (4) Side effects: urinary tract infections, genital yeast infections (glucose-rich urine = bacterial/fungal growth medium), diabetic ketoacidosis (rare). (5) This is a direct clinical application of secondary active transport principles.

  Q24. A farmer waters his crops with irrigation water that has become increasingly salty due to mineral buildup. Over several weeks, the crops wilt and die despite receiving adequate water volume. Using your knowledge of osmosis, plasmolysis, and water potential, explain why salty irrigation water kills the plants.
    [ ] Salt is toxic to chloroplasts and directly destroys the photosynthetic apparatus — osmosis and water potential play no role in salt damage
    [*] Salty irrigation water is hypertonic to plant root cells → water potential of soil solution becomes more negative than root cell water potential → water moves OUT of root cells by osmosis instead of in → root cells lose turgor pressure → plasmolysis → plant cannot absorb water → leaves wilt (loss of turgor in leaf cells) → prolonged water stress → stomata close (guard cells lose turgor) → photosynthesis stops → plant dies — this is called 'physiological drought' because water is physically present but osmotically unavailable
    [ ] Salt makes the water too heavy for roots to absorb — this is a physical weight problem, not an osmotic issue
    [ ] The salt fills in spaces between soil particles, physically blocking water from reaching the roots — osmosis plays no role in this process
    Explanation: Salt stress and osmotic water relations in plants: (1) Normal water uptake: soil water potential (Ψ) > root cell Ψ → water enters roots by osmosis → moves through plant via transpiration stream. (2) Salty soil: dissolved salts (NaCl, etc.) lower soil water potential (more negative Ψs) → if soil Ψ < root Ψ → water flows OUT of roots → cells lose turgor → plasmolysis. (3) 'Physiological drought': plants experience water stress even though soil is wet — water is osmotically unavailable. (4) Cascade of effects: root water loss → reduced turgor throughout plant → wilting → stomatal closure (guard cells lose turgor) → reduced CO₂ uptake → reduced photosynthesis → reduced growth → death. (5) Ion toxicity: Na⁺ and Cl⁻ accumulate in cells → disrupt enzyme function, protein structure, membrane integrity. (6) Soil salinization: major agricultural problem worldwide (affects ~20% of irrigated land). (7) Plant adaptations: halophytes (salt-tolerant plants) accumulate compatible solutes (proline, glycine betaine) to lower cellular Ψs without toxic effects → maintain water uptake even in salty soil. Mangroves: salt-secreting glands.

  Q25. A neuroscientist explains that the resting membrane potential of a neuron (~-70 mV) depends on the ion gradients established by the Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase and on K⁺ leak channels. How does the interplay between active transport and passive transport create this electrical potential?
    [ ] The resting membrane potential is created solely by passive diffusion of all ions through the membrane — no active transport is involved
    [*] The Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (active transport) establishes steep ion gradients: high K⁺ inside (~140 mM), low outside (~5 mM); high Na⁺ outside (~145 mM), low inside (~12 mM) — K⁺ leak channels allow K⁺ to passively diffuse out of the cell down its concentration gradient → positive charge leaves → inside becomes negative relative to outside → this creates the resting potential (~-70 mV) — the pump also directly contributes by being electrogenic (3 Na⁺ out vs. 2 K⁺ in = net +1 charge exported per cycle), accounting for about -5 to -10 mV of the resting potential
    [ ] The resting potential is created exclusively by the Na⁺/K⁺ pump exporting positive charges — K⁺ leak channels play no role whatsoever
    [ ] Membrane potential only exists during an action potential — neurons have zero electrical potential when at rest
    Explanation: Resting membrane potential — active + passive transport interplay: (1) Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (active) creates and maintains gradients: [K⁺]ᵢ ≈ 140 mM, [K⁺]ₒ ≈ 5 mM; [Na⁺]ₒ ≈ 145 mM, [Na⁺]ᵢ ≈ 12 mM. (2) Resting membrane has many open K⁺ leak channels but few Na⁺ channels → membrane is ~50-75× more permeable to K⁺ than Na⁺ at rest. (3) K⁺ diffuses OUT through leak channels (down concentration gradient) → positive charge leaves cell → interior becomes negative. This is facilitated diffusion (passive). (4) K⁺ efflux continues until the electrical gradient (inside negative, pulling K⁺ back in) balances the concentration gradient (pushing K⁺ out) → electrochemical equilibrium for K⁺ → Nernst potential for K⁺ ≈ -90 mV. (5) Small Na⁺ leak inward moves potential slightly more positive → actual resting potential ≈ -70 mV (Goldman-Hodgkin-Katz equation). (6) Electrogenic contribution of pump: 3 Na⁺ out, 2 K⁺ in → net +1 charge exported per cycle → contributes ~-5 to -10 mV directly. (7) Key concept: active transport creates the gradients; passive transport (K⁺ leak) creates the voltage.

  Q26. Cystic fibrosis patients have defective CFTR chloride channels, leading to thick mucus in the lungs. A research team is studying how blocking a chloride channel can lead to altered water balance. Explain the transport principles linking CFTR dysfunction to mucus dehydration.
    [ ] CFTR produces mucus directly — when it is defective, the mucus it produces is thicker because the protein structure is wrong
    [*] Normal CFTR channels secrete Cl⁻ ions into the airway lumen (active transport, ATP-gated channel) and simultaneously inhibit epithelial Na⁺ channels (ENaC) — when CFTR is defective (most commonly ΔF508 mutation → misfolded protein degraded before reaching membrane): Cl⁻ secretion stops AND Na⁺ absorption increases uncontrollably (ENaC no longer inhibited) → Na⁺ moves from lumen into cells → water follows Na⁺ by osmosis → airway surface liquid becomes dehydrated → mucus layer thickens → impaired mucociliary clearance → chronic bacterial infections (Pseudomonas aeruginosa, Staphylococcus aureus)
    [ ] CFTR deficiency causes the phospholipid bilayer to become impermeable to all molecules — cells cannot exchange any substance with the airway lumen
    [ ] Thick mucus is caused by excess cholesterol in lung cell membranes — CFTR and chloride transport are unrelated to mucus hydration
    Explanation: CFTR and osmotic water balance in airways: (1) Normal: CFTR (Cl⁻ channel + ENaC regulator) on apical membrane of airway epithelial cells. Cl⁻ secreted into lumen → Na⁺ follows paracellularly (electrical attraction) → water follows by osmosis → maintains thin, hydrated airway surface liquid (ASL, ~7 μm deep) → proper mucociliary clearance (~12-15 mm/min). (2) CF (ΔF508 = ~70% of mutations): CFTR protein misfolds → degraded by proteasome → never reaches plasma membrane → no Cl⁻ secretion + ENaC unregulated → excessive Na⁺ absorption from lumen → water follows Na⁺ out of lumen by osmosis → ASL dehydrated to ~3 μm → mucus becomes viscous and sticky → cilia cannot beat effectively → mucus stagnates → bacterial colonization → chronic inflammation → bronchiectasis → progressive lung destruction. (3) Treatment: CFTR modulators (Trikafta® = elexacaftor + tezacaftor + ivacaftor) — correct folding and enhance channel function of ΔF508-CFTR. (4) Key transport principles: channel protein function, ion transport driving osmotic water movement, coupling of different transport processes.

  Q27. Paramecium is a freshwater protist that lives in a hypotonic environment. It uses a contractile vacuole to continuously pump out water that enters by osmosis. Without this organelle, the cell would swell and burst. What transport principles explain why the contractile vacuole is essential, and why do marine protists lack one?
    [ ] Paramecium actively pumps water INTO its cell and the contractile vacuole stores it — marine protists don't need one because salt water inhibits cell growth
    [*] Freshwater is hypotonic to Paramecium's cytoplasm → water enters continuously by osmosis (through the membrane and aquaporin-like channels) → without a mechanism to expel excess water, the cell would swell and lyse (no cell wall to provide counter-pressure) → the contractile vacuole collects excess water and actively pumps it out (requires ATP) at rates of 1-2 vacuole cycles per minute — marine protists live in an approximately isotonic environment (seawater ~1000 mOsm), so little net water enters by osmosis and no contractile vacuole is needed
    [ ] The contractile vacuole is used for digestion, not water balance — marine protists have different digestive enzymes that replace it
    [ ] Freshwater protists use active transport of salt ions to prevent osmosis entirely — the contractile vacuole is vestigial and nonfunctional
    Explanation: Contractile vacuole and osmoregulation: (1) Problem: Paramecium cytoplasm (~80-100 mOsm) is hypertonic relative to freshwater (~0-5 mOsm) → water enters continuously by osmosis → without countermeasure, cell swells and lyses. (2) Solution: contractile vacuole — a specialized organelle that collects excess cytoplasmic water and actively expels it. Mechanism: collecting tubules (radiating canals) gather water → feed into central vacuole → vacuole fills (diastole) → contracts (systole) → water expelled through a pore. Requires ATP (V-type H⁺ ATPase + aquaporins). Rate: ~1-2 cycles/min; expels cell volume equivalent every 15-20 min. (3) Marine protists: seawater (~1000 mOsm) is approximately isotonic or hypertonic to cytoplasm → minimal net osmotic water entry → contractile vacuoles absent or vestigial. (4) Analogous to kidneys in animals: kidneys regulate body fluid osmolarity (countercurrent multiplier in loop of Henle, ADH-regulated aquaporin insertion in collecting duct). (5) Demonstrates: osmosis in hypotonic environments, cellular adaptations to osmotic stress, ATP-dependent water expulsion.

  Q28. During organ transplant surgery, donor organs are preserved in special solutions (e.g., University of Wisconsin solution, Custodiol) rather than in normal saline or plain water. These solutions are carefully formulated to match intracellular ionic composition and osmolarity. Using your knowledge of transport, explain why the preservation solution matters.
    [ ] Preservation solutions are simply antibiotics that kill bacteria on the organ surface — the ionic composition has no effect on organ viability
    [*] Without functioning Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase (ATP depleted in ischemic organ), ion gradients collapse → Na⁺ leaks into cells, K⁺ leaks out → water follows Na⁺ into cells by osmosis → cellular edema (swelling) → membrane damage. Preservation solutions contain: high K⁺ (matches intracellular [K⁺] to reduce K⁺ efflux), impermeant anions like lactobionate and raffinose (maintain osmotic balance, prevent water influx), and low Na⁺ and Ca²⁺ — this slows gradient dissipation and prevents osmotic swelling during the hours between harvesting and transplant
    [ ] Organs can be stored in any solution including tap water — the chemical composition of the preservation fluid has no effect on transplant success
    [ ] Preservation solutions work by freezing the organ solid, and their ionic composition is irrelevant — it's the low temperature alone that prevents deterioration
    Explanation: Organ preservation and transport principles: (1) Problem: harvested organ → ischemia (no blood flow) → ATP depletion → Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase stops → ion gradients collapse. Without the pump: Na⁺ (high outside) leaks in through Na⁺ channels → water follows by osmosis → cell swelling → membrane disruption → irreversible injury. Ca²⁺ (high outside, low inside cytoplasm) leaks in → activates destructive enzymes (phospholipases, proteases, endonucleases) → cell death. (2) UW solution (Belzer, 1987): high K⁺ (~125 mM, matches intracellular) → reduces K⁺ efflux through leak channels. Impermeant anions (lactobionate, raffinose, hydroxyethyl starch) → maintain extracellular osmolarity → prevent osmotic cell swelling. Low Na⁺ → reduces Na⁺ influx. Glutathione → antioxidant. Adenosine → ATP precursor for recovery. (3) Result: extends cold ischemia tolerance: kidney 24-36 hours, liver 12-18 hours, heart 4-6 hours. (4) Demonstrates: passive transport (ion leak when pumps fail), osmosis driving cell swelling, and how understanding transport principles directly saves lives in transplant medicine.

  Q29. A biology student observes that a wilted celery stalk placed in a glass of fresh water becomes crisp and rigid again within a few hours. However, a celery stalk placed in concentrated salt water becomes even more wilted. Explain both observations using the concepts of osmosis, turgor pressure, and plasmolysis.
    [ ] The fresh water chemically reacts with cellulose in the cell wall to make it rigid — the salt water dissolves the cell wall, causing additional wilting
    [*] Fresh water (hypotonic): water enters celery cells by osmosis → vacuoles fill → turgor pressure increases → cells push against rigid cell walls → stalk becomes crisp and rigid (recovery from incipient plasmolysis/wilting). Salt water (hypertonic): water leaves cells by osmosis → vacuoles shrink → turgor pressure drops → plasma membrane pulls away from cell wall (plasmolysis) → cells lose structural support → stalk becomes even more wilted and limp
    [ ] Celery absorbs all available salt through active transport, making its cells too heavy to remain rigid — this is a gravitational effect, not osmosis
    [ ] Fresh water kills bacteria on the celery surface, allowing it to heal — salt water promotes bacterial growth that destroys the cell structure
    Explanation: Celery and osmosis — everyday example: (1) Wilted celery: cells have lost water (dehydration, perhaps from storage) → reduced turgor pressure → cells are flaccid → stalk is limp. (2) In fresh water (hypotonic): pure water has higher water potential (Ψ) than celery cells (which contain dissolved salts, sugars, acids → negative Ψs). Water enters cells by osmosis → central vacuoles refill → volume increases → plasma membrane pushes against cell wall → turgor pressure restored → cell wall provides structural rigidity → stalk is crisp. (3) In salt water (hypertonic): salt solution has lower Ψ than celery cells → water exits cells by osmosis → vacuoles shrink → membrane pulls away from cell wall (plasmolysis) → turgor lost → cells flaccid → stalk even more wilted. (4) Everyday food science applications: salting vegetables (drawing out water — pickling, sauerkraut); marinating (osmotic exchange of flavors); crispy lettuce (store in cold water). (5) Demonstrates: osmosis, water potential, turgor pressure, plasmolysis/deplasmolysis — all fundamental cellular transport concepts with tangible real-world observations.

  Q30. Cholera toxin produced by Vibrio cholerae causes massive, life-threatening diarrhea by permanently activating CFTR chloride channels in intestinal epithelial cells. Oral rehydration therapy (ORT) — a simple solution of water, salt, and sugar — saves millions of lives by exploiting a specific membrane transport protein to rehydrate patients. What is the transport mechanism behind ORT?
    [ ] ORT works simply by replacing lost water through passive absorption in the stomach — the salt and sugar are only for taste
    [*] Cholera toxin locks CFTR Cl⁻ channels open → massive Cl⁻ secretion into intestinal lumen → Na⁺ follows paracellularly → water follows by osmosis → severe watery diarrhea (up to 20 L/day). ORT exploits the Na⁺-glucose cotransporter SGLT1 (secondary active transport, unaffected by cholera toxin): glucose + Na⁺ enter intestinal cells together via SGLT1 → water follows by osmosis → rehydration occurs even while the secretory diarrhea continues — this is why the specific ratio of salt to sugar is critical and why plain water alone is insufficient
    [ ] ORT works by killing Vibrio cholerae bacteria directly — the sugar provides energy to immune cells and the salt creates a hostile environment for the pathogen
    [ ] ORT rehydrates patients through active transport of water molecules using ATP — the salt and sugar provide the energy needed to pump water back into cells
    Explanation: Cholera and oral rehydration therapy — transport principles saving lives: (1) Cholera pathogenesis: V. cholerae colonizes small intestine → secretes cholera toxin → toxin enters epithelial cells → permanently activates Gsα → constitutive cAMP production → CFTR Cl⁻ channels locked open → massive Cl⁻ secretion into lumen → Na⁺ follows (electrochemical attraction) → water follows by osmosis → profuse rice-water diarrhea (up to 20 L/day) → severe dehydration → hypovolemic shock → death within hours without treatment. (2) ORT mechanism: the Na⁺-glucose cotransporter SGLT1 on the apical membrane of intestinal enterocytes is NOT affected by cholera toxin. SGLT1: secondary active transporter — 2 Na⁺ + 1 glucose enter cell together (symport, powered by Na⁺ gradient from basolateral Na⁺/K⁺ ATPase). When patient drinks ORT solution (specific ratio: ~3.5 g NaCl, 20 g glucose per liter water, WHO formula): glucose + Na⁺ absorbed via SGLT1 → increases intracellular osmolarity → water follows by osmosis → net fluid absorption even while secretory mechanism is still active. (3) Impact: ORT called 'potentially the most important medical advance of the 20th century' (The Lancet, 1978). Reduces cholera mortality from >50% to <1%. Saves ~1 million lives per year globally. (4) Key transport concepts demonstrated: secondary active transport (SGLT1), osmosis driven by solute absorption, coupling of Na⁺ and glucose transport, clinical application of basic membrane transport biology.
