--- Lesson 7.6:  (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Prokaryotic vs. Eukaryotic Cells
Key Concepts: Prokaryotic vs. Eukaryotic Cells

This lesson covers the fundamental concepts of prokaryotic vs. eukaryotic cells in biology.

  • Understanding the basic principles and terminology
  • Exploring real-world applications and examples
  • Connecting to other biological concepts

Learning Objectives

  • Define key terms related to prokaryotic vs. eukaryotic cells
  • Explain the importance of this topic in biology
  • Apply concepts to solve problems

Why This Matters

  • Builds foundation for advanced biology topics
  • Has practical applications in medicine and research
  • Helps explain natural phenomena

--- Lesson 7.6:  (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is the most fundamental structural difference between prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells?
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells are always larger than eukaryotic cells and contain more organelles
    [*] Prokaryotic cells (pro = 'before,' karyon = 'kernel/nucleus') lack a membrane-bound nucleus and membrane-bound organelles, while eukaryotic cells (eu = 'true') have a membrane-enclosed nucleus containing their DNA and possess compartmentalized organelles such as mitochondria, ER, and Golgi apparatus — this distinction, formalized by Carl Woese and George Fox in 1977 with their three-domain classification (Bacteria, Archaea, Eukarya), represents the most fundamental division in cellular life
    [ ] Eukaryotic cells lack ribosomes while prokaryotic cells have them — this is the only difference between the two cell types
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells have DNA while eukaryotic cells use RNA exclusively as their genetic material
    Explanation: Prokaryotic vs. eukaryotic: the most fundamental division in cellular life. Prokaryotes (Greek: pro = before, karyon = kernel/nucleus): no membrane-bound nucleus — DNA in nucleoid region; no membrane-bound organelles; typically 0.1-10 μm. Eukaryotes (eu = true): membrane-bound nucleus; compartmentalized organelles; typically 10-100 μm. Historical context: Édouard Chatton (1925) first proposed the prokaryote/eukaryote distinction. Carl Woese and George Fox (1977) used 16S/18S rRNA sequences to classify all life into three domains: Bacteria (prokaryotic), Archaea (prokaryotic), Eukarya (eukaryotic). Both cell types share: DNA as genetic material, ribosomes (different sizes), plasma membrane, cytoplasm. The distinction matters because: compartmentalization allows eukaryotes to be larger and more complex (organelles create specialized chemical environments).

  Q2. What are the major structural components of a typical prokaryotic cell, and how do they differ from eukaryotic organelles?
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells contain all the same organelles as eukaryotic cells — the only difference is their smaller size
    [*] A typical prokaryotic cell has: a single circular chromosome in the nucleoid region (no nuclear membrane), 70S ribosomes (vs. 80S in eukaryotes), a plasma membrane, a rigid cell wall (usually peptidoglycan in bacteria), and often additional structures such as a capsule (polysaccharide coat for protection), pili/fimbriae (attachment and DNA transfer), flagella (rotary motor for motility), and plasmids (small circular extrachromosomal DNA) — none of these structures are enclosed in membranes, unlike eukaryotic organelles
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells have a nucleus, mitochondria, and ER just like eukaryotes but they are simply smaller versions of each organelle
    [ ] Prokaryotic cells have no internal structures at all — they are simply bags of cytoplasm with no organization
    Explanation: Prokaryotic cell anatomy: (1) Nucleoid: irregularly shaped region with single circular chromosome (typically ~1-10 million bp). No nuclear envelope. DNA associated with HU proteins and other nucleoid-associated proteins (NAPs) — not true histones (though archaea have histone-like proteins). (2) Ribosomes: 70S (50S large + 30S small subunit) — target for antibiotics (streptomycin, tetracycline, chloramphenicol, erythromycin). (3) Cell wall: bacteria — peptidoglycan (NAG-NAM polymer cross-linked by peptide bridges). Provides shape (cocci, bacilli, spirilla) and protection against osmotic lysis. (4) Plasma membrane: phospholipid bilayer. In bacteria: ester-linked fatty acids. In archaea: ether-linked isoprenoid chains (more stable in extreme conditions). (5) Capsule: polysaccharide or protein coat. Virulence factor (Griffith's experiment 1928 — S vs. R pneumococcus). (6) Pili: short protein appendages. Type IV pili (twitching motility), sex pilus (F pilus — conjugation). (7) Flagella: rotary motor (proton motive force drives rotation at ~100-1000 rpm). Made of flagellin protein. (8) Plasmids: extrachromosomal circular DNA (1-200+ kb). Carry accessory genes: antibiotic resistance (R plasmids), virulence factors, metabolic capabilities.

  Q3. What advantage does compartmentalization (membrane-bound organelles) provide to eukaryotic cells that prokaryotic cells lack?
    [ ] Compartmentalization provides no advantage — it simply makes the cell more wasteful by having too many membranes
    [*] Membrane-bound organelles create specialized microenvironments with distinct pH, ion concentrations, and enzyme sets — this allows incompatible biochemical reactions to occur simultaneously (e.g., lysosomal degradation at pH 4.5 while the cytoplasm maintains pH 7.2; mitochondrial oxidative phosphorylation at the inner membrane's H⁺ gradient) — compartmentalization increases efficiency, enables larger cell size, and supports greater cellular complexity
    [ ] Having organelles makes eukaryotic cells divide faster than prokaryotic cells — bacteria divide slowly because they lack mitochondria
    [ ] Organelles prevent mutations from occurring — prokaryotic cells mutate constantly because they lack nuclear membranes
    Explanation: Compartmentalization advantage: (1) Specialized environments: lysosomes (pH 4.5 for hydrolysis), ER lumen (oxidizing for disulfide bonds), mitochondrial matrix (enzymes for citric acid cycle), peroxisomes (catalase for H₂O₂ detoxification). (2) Simultaneous incompatible reactions: protein synthesis (cytoplasm) AND protein degradation (lysosomes) can occur in the same cell without interference. (3) Increased surface area: cristae in mitochondria, thylakoids in chloroplasts — membranes increase area for membrane-bound reactions (ETC, photosystems). (4) Larger cell volume: prokaryotes are limited by surface area:volume ratio (all metabolic reactions occur at/near the plasma membrane). Eukaryotes overcome this with internal membranes that increase total reactive surface area. (5) Regulatory control: nuclear envelope separates transcription from translation — allows mRNA processing (splicing, capping, polyadenylation) before export — additional layer of gene regulation absent in prokaryotes. (6) Evolutionary consequence: eukaryotic compartmentalization (especially mitochondrial endosymbiosis) enabled the ~1,000-fold increase in genome size and cellular complexity that distinguishes eukaryotes from prokaryotes.

  Q4. How do cell walls differ among bacteria, archaea, plants, and fungi?
    [ ] All cell walls are made of the same material (peptidoglycan) regardless of the organism
    [*] Cell wall composition varies dramatically: bacteria use peptidoglycan (NAG-NAM polymer — target of penicillin and lysozyme); archaea use pseudopeptidoglycan, S-layer proteins, or polysaccharides (but never true peptidoglycan); plant cells use cellulose microfibrils embedded in a matrix of hemicellulose and pectin; fungi use chitin (N-acetylglucosamine polymer) — these differences reflect independent evolutionary origins of cell wall structures across the three domains of life
    [ ] Only bacteria have cell walls — archaea, plants, and fungi all lack cell walls entirely
    [ ] Plants use peptidoglycan and fungi use cellulose — the opposite of what most students assume
    Explanation: Cell wall diversity: (1) Bacteria — peptidoglycan (murein): polymer of N-acetylglucosamine (NAG) and N-acetylmuramic acid (NAM) cross-linked by short peptide bridges. Gram-positive: thick peptidoglycan (20-80 nm), teichoic acids. Gram-negative: thin peptidoglycan (2-7 nm), outer membrane with lipopolysaccharide (LPS/endotoxin). Target: β-lactam antibiotics (penicillin, cephalosporins) inhibit transpeptidase → prevent cross-linking → osmotic lysis. Lysozyme: cleaves NAG-NAM bond (found in tears, saliva, mucus — innate immunity). (2) Archaea — NO peptidoglycan. Pseudopeptidoglycan (pseudomurein — NAG + N-acetyltalosaminuronic acid, β-1,3 linkages → resistant to lysozyme and penicillin). S-layer (crystalline protein/glycoprotein coat). Some have polysaccharide walls. (3) Plants — cellulose: β-1,4-linked glucose polymer. ~30 cellulose chains → microfibril. Embedded in matrix of hemicellulose, pectin, and sometimes lignin (secondary wall). Primary wall: thin, flexible. Secondary wall: thick, rigid (after growth stops). (4) Fungi — chitin: β-1,4-linked N-acetylglucosamine polymer. Similar structure to cellulose but with nitrogen-containing groups. Also: β-glucans, mannoproteins.

  Q5. What is Gram staining, and why is it one of the most important procedures in clinical microbiology?
    [ ] Gram staining measures the DNA content of bacteria — bacteria with more DNA stain purple and those with less DNA stain pink
    [*] Gram staining (developed by Hans Christian Gram in 1884) is a differential staining technique that classifies bacteria based on cell wall structure: Gram-positive bacteria (thick peptidoglycan layer, 20-80 nm) retain crystal violet dye and appear purple; Gram-negative bacteria (thin peptidoglycan, 2-7 nm, plus an outer membrane containing LPS) lose crystal violet during alcohol decolorization and are counterstained pink by safranin — this distinction guides antibiotic selection (e.g., penicillin is more effective against Gram-positive) and helps predict pathogenic mechanisms
    [ ] Gram staining is only used in research labs and has no clinical significance — it was an outdated technique replaced by DNA sequencing
    [ ] Gram staining always stains all bacteria the same color — it cannot distinguish between different types of bacteria
    Explanation: Gram staining — cornerstone of clinical microbiology: (1) Procedure (Hans Christian Gram, 1884): apply crystal violet (primary stain) → iodine mordant (forms CV-I complex) → alcohol decolorization (critical step) → safranin counterstain. (2) Results: Gram-positive: thick peptidoglycan traps CV-I complex → alcohol cannot wash it out → purple. Examples: Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, Bacillus, Clostridium. Gram-negative: thin peptidoglycan + outer membrane dissolved by alcohol → CV-I washed out → safranin counterstain → pink/red. Examples: E. coli, Salmonella, Neisseria, Pseudomonas. (3) Clinical significance: guides empiric antibiotic therapy before culture results return (takes 48-72 hrs). Gram-positive infections: penicillins, cephalosporins, vancomycin. Gram-negative infections: aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, carbapenems. (4) Predicts pathogenic mechanisms: Gram-negative outer membrane contains LPS (endotoxin) → triggers severe inflammatory response (septic shock). Gram-positive: exotoxins (many), biofilm formation. (5) Limitations: Mycobacteria are acid-fast (waxy mycolic acid wall — need Ziehl-Neelsen stain). Mycoplasma: no cell wall → cannot be Gram-stained. Still, >130 years later, Gram staining remains the first diagnostic test performed on most clinical bacterial specimens.

  Q6. How do archaea differ from bacteria, even though both are prokaryotes?
    [ ] Archaea and bacteria are identical in every way — the distinction is arbitrary and based only on where they live
    [*] Although both lack nuclei, archaea differ from bacteria in several fundamental ways: archaea have ether-linked isoprenoid lipids in their membranes (vs. ester-linked fatty acids in bacteria), lack peptidoglycan in their cell walls (using pseudopeptidoglycan or S-layer instead), have more eukaryote-like transcription/translation machinery (TATA-binding proteins, multiple RNA polymerases), and are often (but not exclusively) found in extreme environments — Carl Woese's 16S rRNA analysis (1977) revealed archaea are a distinct domain, actually more closely related to eukaryotes than to bacteria
    [ ] Archaea are actually eukaryotes — they have nuclei and membrane-bound organelles but are smaller than typical eukaryotic cells
    [ ] The only difference is that archaea are always harmful pathogens while bacteria are always beneficial to humans
    Explanation: Archaea — the 'third domain': (1) Carl Woese's revolution (1977): 16S rRNA sequencing revealed prokaryotes are NOT monophyletic. Archaea form a separate domain, more closely related to Eukarya. Led to three-domain system replacing the five-kingdom classification. (2) Key differences from bacteria: Membranes: ether-linked isoprenoid chains (vs. ester-linked fatty acids in bacteria). Some have monolayer membranes (tetraether lipids) — extremely stable at high temperatures. Cell wall: NO peptidoglycan → resistant to penicillin and lysozyme. Pseudopeptidoglycan, S-layer proteins, or polysaccharide walls instead. Transcription/translation: more eukaryote-like. TATA-binding protein, multiple RNA polymerases (resembling eukaryotic RNA Pol II). Initiator amino acid: methionine (like eukaryotes) vs. formylmethionine (bacteria). (3) Extremophiles: thermophiles (>80°C, Thermus aquaticus), halophiles (high salt, Halobacterium), acidophiles (pH <2), methanogens (produce CH₄, strict anaerobes). But also found in moderate environments: ocean, soil, human gut. (4) No known archaeal pathogens (unlike bacteria). (5) Ecological importance: methanogens — major impact on carbon cycle and climate.

  Q7. How do prokaryotes reproduce, and how does binary fission differ from eukaryotic cell division (mitosis)?
    [ ] Prokaryotes reproduce by mitosis using a mitotic spindle, just like eukaryotic cells — there is no difference in the division mechanism
    [*] Prokaryotes reproduce by binary fission: the single circular chromosome replicates from a single origin of replication, the two copies attach to the plasma membrane and are separated as the cell elongates, then a septum forms at midcell and the cell divides into two identical daughter cells — this is simpler and faster than eukaryotic mitosis (which requires a nuclear envelope breakdown, mitotic spindle assembly, and chromosome condensation) — E. coli can divide every 20 minutes under optimal conditions
    [ ] Prokaryotes only reproduce sexually through meiosis — they cannot divide asexually
    [ ] Prokaryotes create new cells by budding off pieces of their membrane — binary fission does not involve DNA replication
    Explanation: Binary fission vs. mitosis: (1) Binary fission: (a) DNA replication: begins at single origin of replication (oriC in E. coli) → bidirectional replication → two copies. (b) Segregation: replicated chromosomes attached to membrane at different points → cell growth separates them (no mitotic spindle needed). FtsZ protein (bacterial tubulin homolog) forms Z-ring at midcell → recruits division machinery. (c) Septum formation: new cell wall and membrane grow inward → cell pinches in two. (d) Speed: E. coli generation time ~20 minutes (optimal) → 1 cell → 2⁷² (~4.7 × 10²¹ cells) in 24 hours (theoretically). (2) Eukaryotic mitosis: much more complex. Nuclear envelope breakdown, chromatin condensation into chromosomes, mitotic spindle assembly (microtubule-based), attachment at kinetochores, metaphase alignment, anaphase separation, cytokinesis. Duration: hours (human cells ~24-hour cell cycle, with M phase ~1 hour). (3) Genetic variation in prokaryotes: binary fission is clonal (no variation) → variation comes from: mutation, horizontal gene transfer (transformation, transduction, conjugation). (4) FtsZ connection: FtsZ is homologous to eukaryotic tubulin → shared ancestor → supports evolutionary relationship.

  Q8. What are plasmids, and why are they important in both natural bacterial biology and modern biotechnology?
    [ ] Plasmids are the main chromosomes of bacteria — without them, bacteria cannot survive or reproduce
    [*] Plasmids are small, circular, extrachromosomal DNA molecules (typically 1-200+ kb) that replicate independently of the bacterial chromosome — they carry accessory genes not essential for survival but conferring advantages such as antibiotic resistance (R plasmids), virulence factors, and metabolic capabilities — in biotechnology, plasmids are used as vectors in genetic engineering to clone and express foreign genes (e.g., producing human insulin in E. coli), and they can be transferred between bacteria via conjugation, contributing to the spread of antibiotic resistance
    [ ] Plasmids are membrane-bound organelles found only in eukaryotic cells — they store extra nutrients for the cell
    [ ] Plasmids are protein structures on the bacterial surface used exclusively for attachment to host tissues during infection
    Explanation: Plasmids — versatile genetic elements: (1) Natural biology: small circular DNA molecules (1 kb to >200 kb). Self-replicating (own origin of replication). NOT essential for survival under normal conditions — but carry advantageous genes. Types: R plasmids (antibiotic resistance — major clinical concern), F plasmids (fertility — encode conjugation machinery → sex pilus → DNA transfer), Col plasmids (colicins — kill competing bacteria), virulence plasmids (toxins, adhesins), degradative plasmids (enzymes to metabolize unusual compounds like petroleum). (2) Transfer: conjugation (cell-to-cell contact via sex pilus), transformation (uptake of free DNA), transduction (bacteriophage-mediated). Horizontal gene transfer → rapid spread of resistance genes between species. (3) Biotechnology revolution: plasmids as cloning vectors. Engineered plasmids contain: multiple cloning site (MCS), antibiotic resistance marker (selectable marker), origin of replication, promoter for gene expression. Applications: recombinant insulin (Humulin, 1982 — first recombinant DNA drug approved), growth hormone, clotting factors, vaccines. CRISPR plasmids for gene editing. (4) Copy number: some plasmids maintain 1-2 copies per cell (stringent control), others 100+ copies (relaxed control).

  Q9. How do prokaryotic flagella fundamentally differ from eukaryotic flagella in structure and mechanism of movement?
    [ ] There is no difference — both prokaryotic and eukaryotic flagella are made of the same protein (tubulin) and move in the same way
    [*] Prokaryotic flagella are made of the protein flagellin and function as rotary motors powered by the proton motive force (H⁺ gradient) — they rotate like propellers at speeds of 100-1,000+ rpm — eukaryotic flagella/cilia have a completely different 9+2 microtubule arrangement (axoneme), are made of tubulin, are enclosed in the plasma membrane, and produce a whip-like bending motion powered by dynein motor proteins using ATP — these structures evolved independently (convergent evolution for motility)
    [ ] Eukaryotic flagella rotate like propellers while prokaryotic flagella bend back and forth — the protein composition is the same in both cases
    [ ] Only prokaryotes have flagella — eukaryotic cells are completely immotile and cannot move
    Explanation: Flagella comparison — convergent evolution: (1) Prokaryotic flagellum: Structure: hollow tube of flagellin protein subunits (~20 nm diameter). Components: filament (flagellin), hook (flexible coupling), basal body (motor embedded in membrane/wall — stator and rotor rings). Mechanism: rotary motor powered by proton motive force (H⁺ flow through MotA/MotB stator). Speed: 100-1,000+ rpm. Direction: counterclockwise rotation → flagella bundle → 'run' (forward movement). Clockwise → flagella splay apart → 'tumble' (random reorientation). Chemotaxis: bacteria sense chemical gradients → adjust run/tumble frequency. (2) Eukaryotic flagellum/cilium: Structure: 9+2 axoneme — 9 outer doublet microtubules + 2 central singlets. Made of α/β-tubulin. ~250 nm diameter. Enclosed in plasma membrane extension. Mechanism: dynein motor proteins (molecular motors) cross-bridge between adjacent doublets → sliding → produces bending wave. Powered by ATP hydrolysis. Cilia: short, numerous, coordinated wavelike beating (e.g., respiratory epithelium). Flagella: long, few, undulating motion (e.g., sperm). (3) NOT homologous — completely different molecular composition, energy source, and mechanism. Same function (motility) arrived at independently = convergent evolution.

  Q10. What is the endosymbiotic theory, and how does it explain the evolutionary relationship between prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells?
    [*] The endosymbiotic theory states that eukaryotes evolved when a large prokaryotic cell engulfed smaller prokaryotic cells that were not digested — instead, they formed a mutually beneficial relationship: an engulfed aerobic α-proteobacterium became the mitochondrion, and an engulfed photosynthetic cyanobacterium became the chloroplast — proposed by Lynn Margulis in 1967 — evidence includes double membranes, own circular DNA, 70S ribosomes, binary fission reproduction, and phylogenetic analysis showing mitochondrial genes cluster with α-Proteobacteria and chloroplast genes cluster with cyanobacteria
    [ ] Eukaryotic cells evolved first and then simplified into prokaryotic cells by losing their organelles — mitochondria and chloroplasts were never free-living organisms
    [ ] The endosymbiotic theory states that all cells are prokaryotic — eukaryotic cells do not actually exist and are an artifact of microscopy
    [ ] Mitochondria and chloroplasts were created when eukaryotic cells spontaneously generated new membranes around their enzymes — no prokaryotic involvement occurred
    Explanation: Endosymbiotic theory — bridging prokaryotes and eukaryotes: (1) Proposed by Lynn Margulis (1967, initially rejected by 15 journals). Core idea: eukaryotic organelles arose from endosymbiosis (one cell living inside another). (2) Sequence: ancestral eukaryote (large anaerobic archaeon?) engulfed aerobic α-proteobacterium (~2 BYA) → not digested → mutualistic relationship → aerobe provided ATP from O₂ → host provided nutrients and protection → aerobe became mitochondrion. Later, some mitochondria-containing cells engulfed cyanobacteria → cyanobacterium became chloroplast (~1.5 BYA) → gave rise to plant/algae lineages. (3) Evidence: see L7.5 Q27 for comprehensive list (double membranes, circular DNA, 70S ribosomes, binary fission, phylogenetics, gene transfer to nucleus). (4) Ongoing evolution: most original endosymbiont genes transferred to host nucleus over billions of years (endosymbiotic gene transfer). Mitochondria retain only ~37 genes (humans). Chloroplasts retain ~120 genes. Must import ~1,000-1,500 nuclear-encoded proteins through TOM/TIM (mitochondria) or TOC/TIC (chloroplasts) complexes. (5) Present-day examples: Paulinella chromatophora (photosynthetic amoeba with 'chromatophore' acquired from cyanobacteria ~60 MYA — endosymbiosis in progress). (6) Significance: endosymbiosis was the key step that allowed eukaryotic cellular complexity — mitochondrial ATP production provided the energy budget for larger genomes and greater cellular complexity.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes a prokaryote?
    [ ] Any organism that is too small to see with the naked eye, including all bacteria, fungi, and viruses
    [*] A unicellular organism (belonging to domain Bacteria or domain Archaea) whose cell lacks a membrane-bound nucleus — DNA resides in a nucleoid region without a nuclear envelope — and which lacks membrane-bound organelles such as mitochondria, ER, or Golgi apparatus, though it possesses ribosomes (70S), a plasma membrane, and usually a cell wall
    [ ] An organism with a true nucleus enclosed by a double membrane, containing linear chromosomes wrapped around histone proteins
    [ ] A non-living infectious particle made only of protein and nucleic acid that requires a host cell to replicate
    Explanation: Prokaryote (Greek: pro = before, karyon = kernel/nucleus): cell type lacking membrane-bound nucleus and organelles. Two domains: Bacteria and Archaea. Characteristics: single circular chromosome in nucleoid (some exceptions — Vibrio cholerae has two chromosomes), 70S ribosomes, cell wall (peptidoglycan in bacteria, various in archaea), plasma membrane, often: capsule, pili, flagella, plasmids. Size: typically 0.1-10 μm (but exceptions exist — Thiomargarita namibiensis up to 750 μm). Metabolic diversity: photoautotrophs, chemoautotrophs, photoheterotrophs, chemoheterotrophs — prokaryotes collectively perform nearly every known metabolic reaction. Abundantly diverse: estimated 10³⁰ bacterial cells on Earth. First life on Earth: ~3.5-3.8 billion years ago (prokaryotic fossils — stromatolites). Note: viruses are NOT prokaryotes — they are not cells and lack independent metabolism.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes a eukaryote?
    [ ] Any organism that is multicellular — all eukaryotes must have multiple cells working together in tissues
    [*] An organism whose cells contain a true membrane-bound nucleus (enclosing linear chromosomes organized with histone proteins) and membrane-bound organelles (mitochondria, endoplasmic reticulum, Golgi apparatus, and others) — domain Eukarya includes four major kingdoms: Animalia, Plantae, Fungi, and Protista — eukaryotic cells are typically larger (10-100 μm) and more structurally complex than prokaryotic cells due to compartmentalization
    [ ] A unicellular organism with a single circular chromosome located in a nucleoid region and 70S ribosomes
    [ ] Any organism that performs photosynthesis — only photosynthetic organisms are classified as eukaryotes
    Explanation: Eukaryote (Greek: eu = true, karyon = nucleus): cell type with membrane-bound nucleus and organelles. Domain Eukarya — four kingdoms: Animalia (multicellular, heterotrophic, no cell wall), Plantae (multicellular, autotrophic, cellulose cell wall), Fungi (uni- or multicellular, heterotrophic/absorptive, chitin cell wall), Protista (diverse, mostly unicellular — 'catch-all' kingdom, paraphyletic). Key features: nuclear envelope (double membrane with NPCs), linear chromosomes (humans: 46), histones (nucleosome packaging → chromatin), 80S ribosomes (60S + 40S), membrane-bound organelles (mitochondria in nearly all eukaryotes, chloroplasts in plants/algae, ER, Golgi, lysosomes, etc.), cytoskeleton (microtubules, microfilaments, intermediate filaments). Size: typically 10-100 μm. Can be unicellular (yeast, many protists) or multicellular. Evolved ~1.5-2 BYA (endosymbiotic origin involved prokaryotic ancestors). Reproduction: mitosis (asexual) and meiosis (sexual — genetic recombination).

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes the nucleoid?
    [ ] A membrane-bound compartment within the cell that houses linear chromosomes and a nucleolus, found in all cell types
    [*] An irregularly shaped region within a prokaryotic cell where the circular chromosome is concentrated — the nucleoid is NOT enclosed by a membrane (distinguishing it from the eukaryotic nucleus) — the DNA is compacted by supercoiling and association with nucleoid-associated proteins (NAPs such as HU, H-NS, and IHF) and is attached to the plasma membrane, which assists in chromosome segregation during binary fission
    [ ] A dense, spherical organelle inside the nucleus where ribosomal RNA is produced and ribosome subunits are assembled
    [ ] The fluid-filled space between the inner and outer membranes of mitochondria and chloroplasts
    Explanation: Nucleoid: the DNA-containing region of prokaryotic cells — NOT membrane-bound. Contains: single circular chromosome (typically ~1-10 Mbp; E. coli: 4.6 Mbp, ~4,300 genes). Compaction: DNA supercoiling (topoisomerases regulate), nucleoid-associated proteins (NAPs): HU (most abundant, bends DNA), H-NS (silences genes, bridges DNA segments), IHF (integration host factor — bends DNA at specific sequences), Fis. Note: these are NOT true histones (archaea have histone homologs). Nucleoid organization: chromosome organized into ~400 topologically independent domains (supercoiled loops). Attached to plasma membrane (important for segregation during binary fission — as cell grows, attachment points move apart → chromosomes separate). Visible by: light microscopy (with Feulgen stain or DAPI fluorescent stain), electron microscopy. NOT a nucleus: no double membrane, no nuclear pores, no nucleolus. Key distinction: in prokaryotes, transcription and translation are coupled (ribosome begins translating mRNA while it's still being transcribed) — in eukaryotes, nuclear envelope separates these processes.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes a cell wall?
    [ ] The phospholipid bilayer that surrounds all cells, controlling what enters and exits through selective permeability
    [*] A rigid or semi-rigid structural layer located outside the plasma membrane that provides mechanical support, maintains cell shape, prevents osmotic lysis, and varies in composition across organisms — in bacteria it is made of peptidoglycan; in archaea, pseudopeptidoglycan or S-layer proteins; in plants, cellulose; and in fungi, chitin — animal cells lack cell walls entirely
    [ ] A network of protein filaments inside the cell (microtubules, microfilaments, intermediate filaments) that maintains cell shape
    [ ] A sticky polysaccharide layer found only in pathogenic bacteria that helps them avoid being consumed by immune cells
    Explanation: Cell wall — external structural layer: (1) Function: maintains cell shape, provides mechanical protection, prevents osmotic lysis (in hypotonic environments, cell wall prevents over-expansion), acts as barrier for some molecules, site of important enzymes. (2) Bacterial cell wall (peptidoglycan/murein): NAG-NAM polymer cross-linked by peptide bridges. Gram-positive: 20-80 nm thick, multiple peptidoglycan layers, teichoic acids (linked to peptidoglycan or membrane). Gram-negative: 2-7 nm thin, single peptidoglycan layer + outer membrane (LPS, porins, phospholipids) + periplasmic space between inner and outer membranes. (3) Archaeal cell wall: diverse — pseudopeptidoglycan (some methanogens), S-layer (crystalline protein/glycoprotein lattice — most common), polysaccharides. NO true peptidoglycan → resistant to penicillin and lysozyme. (4) Plant cell wall: primary wall (thin, flexible — cellulose + hemicellulose + pectin) → secondary wall (thick, rigid — cellulose + lignin, deposited after growth stops). Middle lamella (pectin — cements adjacent cells). Plasmodesmata: channels through the wall connecting adjacent cells. (5) Fungal cell wall: chitin (β-1,4-NAG polymer) + β-glucans + mannoproteins. Echinocandin antifungals target β-glucan synthesis. (6) Animal cells: NO cell wall → rely on cytoskeleton and ECM for structural support.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes a flagellum?
    [ ] A short, hair-like appendage found in large numbers on the cell surface, used for attachment to surfaces and DNA transfer between bacterial cells
    [*] A whip-like appendage extending from the cell surface that provides motility — in prokaryotes, it is made of the protein flagellin and functions as a rotary motor (powered by the proton motive force, rotating at 100-1,000+ rpm) — in eukaryotes, it has a 9+2 microtubule axoneme structure (made of tubulin, powered by dynein motor proteins using ATP) and produces a wave-like bending motion — despite similar function, prokaryotic and eukaryotic flagella are not homologous (independent evolutionary origins)
    [ ] A membrane-bound organelle that produces ATP through aerobic respiration, containing its own DNA and ribosomes
    [ ] A thick polysaccharide capsule surrounding the cell that protects against immune system attack and environmental desiccation
    Explanation: Flagellum — motility appendage with two distinct forms: (1) Prokaryotic flagellum: protein: flagellin. Structure: filament + hook + basal body (motor: rotor and stator rings embedded in membrane/wall). Energy: proton motive force (H⁺ gradient, or Na⁺ in marine bacteria). Mechanism: rotary motor — counterclockwise = 'run' (flagella bundle, forward swimming); clockwise = 'tumble' (flagella splay, random reorientation). Speed: up to 100+ body lengths/second. Chemotaxis: biased random walk toward attractants/away from repellents (mediated by MCPs → CheA/CheY signaling → flagellar motor switch). (2) Eukaryotic flagellum/cilium: protein: tubulin (α/β-tubulin heterodimers). Structure: 9+2 axoneme (9 outer doublets + 2 central singlets), nexin links, radial spokes, dynein arms. Covered by plasma membrane. Energy: ATP (dynein motor proteins). Mechanism: dynein walking causes doublet sliding → bending (restricted by nexin links). Cilia: short, numerous, coordinated beating (respiratory epithelium — mucociliary clearance). Flagella: long, few, undulating (sperm tail). (3) Key point: NOT homologous — completely independent origins. Same function arrived at by convergent evolution. Prokaryotic flagellar motor has been called 'the most efficient molecular machine known' — converts ~100% of input energy to output rotation.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes binary fission?
    [ ] A type of sexual reproduction in which two parent cells exchange genetic material before dividing, producing genetically diverse offspring
    [*] An asexual reproduction process used by prokaryotes in which the single circular chromosome replicates, the two copies are separated as the cell elongates (aided by FtsZ ring formation at midcell), and the cell divides into two genetically identical daughter cells — binary fission is simpler and faster than eukaryotic mitosis (E. coli can divide every ~20 minutes under optimal conditions) and does not involve a mitotic spindle, chromosome condensation, or nuclear envelope breakdown
    [ ] The process by which eukaryotic cells divide their chromosomes using a mitotic spindle during the M phase of the cell cycle
    [ ] A process where a cell produces multiple spores that each develop into new organisms, typical of fungi and plants
    Explanation: Binary fission: prokaryotic cell division. Steps: (1) DNA replication begins at origin of replication (oriC). Bidirectional replication by DNA polymerase III. (2) Chromosome segregation: replicated chromosomes anchored to plasma membrane at different points. As cell elongates, anchoring points move apart → chromosomes separate. No mitotic spindle needed. (3) FtsZ ring: tubulin homolog FtsZ forms a contractile ring at midcell (the divisome). Recruits peptidoglycan synthesis machinery. (4) Septum formation: new cell wall and membrane grow inward → cell pinches into two daughter cells. Speed: E. coli ~20 min generation time (optimal). Some bacteria even faster: Clostridium perfringens ~10 min. Theoretical: 1 bacterium → 2^72 cells in 24 hours (~4.7 × 10²¹), but nutrient limitation prevents this. Results: two genetically identical daughter cells (clonal). Genetic variation introduced by: mutation, horizontal gene transfer (transformation, conjugation, transduction). Medical relevance: rapid division = fast infection spread; also means bacteria evolve resistance quickly.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes a plasmid?
    [ ] The main chromosome of a bacterium that contains all essential genes required for survival and reproduction
    [*] A small, circular, extrachromosomal DNA molecule found in prokaryotes (typically 1 kb to 200+ kb) that replicates independently of the bacterial chromosome and carries accessory genes — such as antibiotic resistance (R plasmids), virulence factors, or metabolic capabilities — plasmids can be transferred between bacteria via conjugation and are widely used as vectors in genetic engineering and biotechnology (e.g., cloning human insulin gene into E. coli)
    [ ] A type of virus (bacteriophage) that infects bacteria by injecting its DNA into the host cell
    [ ] A membrane-bound vesicle used by eukaryotic cells to transport proteins between the ER and Golgi apparatus
    Explanation: Plasmid: extrachromosomal genetic element. Features: small circular DNA (1 kb to >200 kb), own origin of replication (replicates independently), not essential for growth under normal conditions. Types: R plasmids (resistance — carry antibiotic resistance genes: β-lactamases, aminoglycoside-modifying enzymes, efflux pumps), F plasmid (fertility factor — encodes sex pilus and conjugation machinery for DNA transfer), Col plasmids (produce colicins — bacteriocins killing competing bacteria), virulence plasmids (toxins, adhesins, invasion factors), degradative plasmids (enzymes for xenobiotic metabolism — oil spills, pesticide breakdown). Transfer mechanisms: conjugation (F pilus → DNA bridge → plasmid copy transferred), transformation (uptake of free environmental DNA), transduction (phage-mediated transfer). Biotechnology applications: cloning vectors (MCS, antibiotic resistance marker, promoter, ori). Landmarks: recombinant human insulin (1982 — Genentech/Eli Lilly — first FDA-approved recombinant DNA product), growth hormone, EPO, clotting factors. Copy number: stringent (1-2 copies/cell) vs. relaxed (10-100+ copies/cell). Clinical crisis: multi-drug resistance plasmids (carrying resistance to multiple antibiotics simultaneously) spreading globally via horizontal gene transfer.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes a bacterial capsule?
    [ ] The rigid peptidoglycan layer that maintains the shape of all bacterial cells and prevents osmotic lysis
    [*] A thick, organized polysaccharide (or sometimes protein) layer secreted outside the cell wall that serves as a major virulence factor — the capsule provides protection against phagocytosis by immune cells (slippery surface prevents engulfment), helps bacteria adhere to surfaces and form biofilms, prevents desiccation, and can shield against antibiotics — Frederick Griffith's 1928 transformation experiment used encapsulated (virulent S) vs. non-encapsulated (non-virulent R) Streptococcus pneumoniae to discover the 'transforming principle'
    [ ] A double membrane structure found only in Gram-negative bacteria containing lipopolysaccharide on its outer surface
    [ ] Short protein appendages on the bacterial surface used for attachment to host tissues and conjugation between cells
    Explanation: Bacterial capsule: extracellular protective layer. Composition: usually polysaccharide (e.g., Streptococcus pneumoniae — >90 serotypes; Klebsiella — thick mucoid capsule), sometimes protein (e.g., Bacillus anthracis — poly-D-glutamic acid capsule). Functions: (1) Anti-phagocytic: capsule is hydrophilic and slippery → prevents opsonization and engulfment by macrophages/neutrophils → major virulence factor. Encapsulated bacteria are generally MORE virulent. (2) Biofilm formation: capsular material helps bacteria adhere to surfaces (teeth, medical devices, tissues) → biofilm development. (3) Desiccation resistance: retains water. (4) Antibiotic resistance: can slow antibiotic penetration. (5) Immune evasion: some capsules mimic host sugars → molecular mimicry → avoid immune detection. Historical significance: Griffith's experiment (1928): heat-killed S strain (encapsulated, virulent) + live R strain (non-encapsulated, non-virulent) → live S strain recovered from dead mice → 'transforming principle' (later identified as DNA by Avery-MacLeod-McCarty, 1944). Vaccines: many bacterial vaccines target capsular polysaccharides (conjugate vaccines: Hib, pneumococcal — PCV13/PPSV23, meningococcal).

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes peptidoglycan?
    [ ] A cellulose-based polymer found in the cell walls of plants and algae that provides structural rigidity
    [*] A unique polymer found in bacterial cell walls (also called murein) composed of alternating N-acetylglucosamine (NAG) and N-acetylmuramic acid (NAM) sugar molecules cross-linked by short peptide bridges — this mesh-like structure provides rigidity and protects against osmotic lysis — peptidoglycan is the target of important antibiotics (penicillin inhibits transpeptidase cross-linking, lysozyme cleaves the NAG-NAM bond) and is a key basis for the Gram stain classification (thick in Gram-positive, thin in Gram-negative bacteria)
    [ ] A chitin-based polymer found in fungal cell walls that is targeted by echinocandin antifungal drugs
    [ ] A lipid-based molecule found in the outer membrane of all cells that forms the basic structure of biological membranes
    Explanation: Peptidoglycan (murein): unique to bacterial cell walls — not found in archaea, eukaryotes, or viruses. Structure: alternating NAG (N-acetylglucosamine) and NAM (N-acetylmuramic acid) linked by β-1,4 glycosidic bonds → linear polysaccharide chains. Short peptide chains extend from NAM → cross-linked to adjacent chains via transpeptidase (also called penicillin-binding protein, PBP). Result: mesh-like sacculus surrounding the entire bacterium. Gram-positive: thick peptidoglycan (20-80 nm, ~40 layers). Teichoic acids (wall teichoic acids + lipoteichoic acids) interspersed. Gram-negative: thin peptidoglycan (2-7 nm, 1-2 layers) in periplasmic space between inner and outer membranes. Antibiotic targets: β-lactams (penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems) → inhibit transpeptidase → prevent cross-linking → weakened wall → osmotic lysis. Vancomycin → binds D-Ala-D-Ala terminus of peptide chain → blocks cross-linking (Gram-positive only — too large to penetrate Gram-negative outer membrane). Lysozyme (innate immunity) → cleaves β-1,4 bond between NAG and NAM. Why important: peptidoglycan's absence in human cells makes it an ideal drug target → selective toxicity.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes Gram staining?
    [ ] A laboratory technique that determines the DNA sequence of bacterial chromosomes to identify species
    [*] A differential staining technique (developed by Hans Christian Gram, 1884) used to classify bacteria based on cell wall structure — the procedure uses crystal violet (primary stain), iodine mordant, alcohol decolorization, and safranin counterstain — Gram-positive bacteria (thick peptidoglycan) retain crystal violet and appear purple, while Gram-negative bacteria (thin peptidoglycan plus an outer membrane) lose crystal violet during decolorization and are counterstained pink — this classification guides antibiotic therapy and predicts pathogenic mechanisms
    [ ] A technique that measures the mass of bacterial cells in grams to determine how many bacteria are present in a clinical sample
    [ ] An electron microscopy method that allows visualization of individual atoms within bacterial cell walls
    Explanation: Gram staining: the most widely used differential staining technique in clinical microbiology. Developed by Hans Christian Gram (1884, Copenhagen). Procedure: (1) Crystal violet (primary stain) → all cells stain purple. (2) Gram's iodine (mordant) → forms crystal violet-iodine (CV-I) complex → larger molecule trapped in thick peptidoglycan. (3) Alcohol/acetone decolorization (critical step) → dissolves Gram-negative outer membrane → washes CV-I out of thin peptidoglycan layer. Thick Gram-positive peptidoglycan retains CV-I (alcohol dehydrates and tightens thick layer). (4) Safranin (counterstain) → Gram-negative cells (decolorized) now stain pink/red. Results: Gram-positive = purple. Gram-negative = pink. Clinical significance: first test on clinical specimens → guides empiric antibiotic selection before culture results (48-72 hrs). Gram-positive: often susceptible to penicillins, cephalosporins, vancomycin. Gram-negative: outer membrane excludes many antibiotics → need aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, carbapenems. Pathogenic implications: Gram-negative LPS (endotoxin) → TLR4 activation → severe inflammatory response (sepsis, septic shock). Limitations: acid-fast bacteria (mycobacteria — waxy mycolic acid), Mycoplasma (no cell wall), intracellular bacteria — not well classified by Gram stain.

  Q21. A hospital microbiology lab receives a blood culture from a patient with high fever and suspected sepsis. The Gram stain shows Gram-negative rods. The physician immediately changes the antibiotic regimen from vancomycin to a broad-spectrum Gram-negative agent. Why does the Gram stain result change the treatment strategy, and what specific threats does a Gram-negative infection pose?
    [ ] Gram staining has no clinical relevance — the physician was wasting time, and the same antibiotic would work equally well on both Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria
    [*] Vancomycin cannot effectively penetrate the Gram-negative outer membrane (it's too large to pass through porins) → it works on Gram-positive bacteria but not Gram-negative — switching to agents that can penetrate the outer membrane (aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, carbapenems) is essential — additionally, Gram-negative bacteria have lipopolysaccharide (LPS/endotoxin) in their outer membrane that can trigger a massive inflammatory response (cytokine storm → septic shock → organ failure → ~30-50% mortality) — the Gram stain, within minutes, provided the critical information to redirect life-saving therapy
    [ ] The physician switched antibiotics because Gram-negative bacteria are always more dangerous than Gram-positive — the staining result is irrelevant to antibiotic selection
    [ ] Gram-negative bacteria are resistant to all antibiotics — the physician was switching to supportive care only, not a different antibiotic
    Explanation: Gram stain in clinical decision-making: (1) Why vancomycin fails against Gram-negatives: vancomycin (MW ~1,449 Da) works by binding D-Ala-D-Ala at the end of peptidoglycan precursor peptides → blocks cross-linking → cell wall synthesis fails → lysis. For Gram-positives: thick peptidoglycan exposed → vancomycin accesses target easily. For Gram-negatives: outer membrane acts as permeability barrier → vancomycin too large to penetrate porins (typically allow molecules <600-700 Da) → cannot reach peptidoglycan target. (2) Appropriate Gram-negative antibiotics: aminoglycosides (gentamicin — enter through porins + self-promoted uptake), fluoroquinolones (ciprofloxacin — penetrate porins), β-lactams with outer membrane penetration (aztreonam — monobactam), carbapenems (meropenem — small enough + resistant to most β-lactamases). (3) LPS/endotoxin threat: Lipid A component of LPS → triggers TLR4 on macrophages → massive cytokine release (TNF-α, IL-1β, IL-6) → systemic inflammatory response (SIRS) → sepsis → septic shock (vasodilation, hypotension, organ failure) → mortality 30-50%. Even dead Gram-negative bacteria release LPS → antibiotic-mediated bacterial killing can initially worsen sepsis (Jarisch-Herxheimer reaction). (4) Time is critical in sepsis: every hour of delayed appropriate antibiotics increases mortality by ~7.6%. The Gram stain provides actionable information within 15-30 minutes — vs. culture (48-72 hrs).

  Q22. A patient with a MRSA (methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus) wound infection does not respond to standard penicillin or methicillin treatment. Investigation reveals the bacterium acquired the mecA gene (encoding an altered penicillin-binding protein PBP2a) on a mobile genetic element from another bacterial species. How does horizontal gene transfer enable the spread of antibiotic resistance among prokaryotes?
    [ ] Antibiotic resistance can only arise through random mutation during DNA replication — bacteria cannot acquire resistance genes from other organisms
    [*] Prokaryotes can acquire new genes through three mechanisms of horizontal gene transfer: (1) transformation — uptake of free DNA from the environment (dead bacterial cells); (2) conjugation — direct cell-to-cell transfer of plasmid DNA via a sex pilus (F pilus); (3) transduction — bacteriophage accidentally packages host DNA and delivers it to a new cell — MRSA acquired mecA via SCCmec (a mobile genetic element) from coagulase-negative staphylococci → PBP2a has low affinity for β-lactams → all β-lactam antibiotics are ineffective — this is uniquely prokaryotic: eukaryotes cannot transfer genes horizontally between species this readily
    [ ] MRSA resistance is caused by the capsule becoming thicker — horizontal gene transfer is not involved in antibiotic resistance
    [ ] Horizontal gene transfer only moves genes within the same species — resistance cannot cross species boundaries in prokaryotes
    Explanation: Horizontal gene transfer (HGT) and antibiotic resistance: (1) Three mechanisms (unique to prokaryotes — no eukaryotic equivalent of this scale): (a) Transformation: competent bacteria uptake naked DNA from environment (released by dead cells). Natural competence in some species (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, B. subtilis). Griffith's experiment (1928) demonstrated transformation. (b) Conjugation: requires cell-to-cell contact. F plasmid encodes sex pilus → bridges donor (F⁺) to recipient (F⁻) → plasmid copy transferred → recipient becomes F⁺. Can transfer chromosomal genes (Hfr = high frequency recombination, when F integrates into chromosome). R plasmids (resistance) spread via conjugation — major clinical concern. (c) Transduction: bacteriophage packages host DNA fragments during lysis → injects into new host. Generalized (random host DNA) or specialized (specific genes near phage integration site). (2) MRSA: mecA gene on SCCmec (Staphylococcal Cassette Chromosome mec — a mobile genetic element). PBP2a: altered penicillin-binding protein with low β-lactam affinity → transpeptidase activity continues despite β-lactam presence → cell wall synthesis not inhibited. Treatment: vancomycin (Gram-positive → no outer membrane barrier), daptomycin, linezolid. VRSA (vancomycin-resistant) now emerging. (3) Global crisis: WHO lists antibiotic resistance as 'one of the biggest threats to global health' — ~700,000 deaths/year from resistant infections (projected ~10 million by 2050). HGT enables resistance to spread between species — unlike eukaryotic vertical (parent-to-offspring) inheritance.

  Q23. In the 1960s, Thomas Brock discovered Thermus aquaticus, a bacterium thriving at 70-80°C in hot springs of Yellowstone National Park. Decades later, a heat-stable DNA polymerase (Taq polymerase) isolated from this organism revolutionized molecular biology. How did an extremophilic prokaryote enable the development of PCR (polymerase chain reaction)?
    [ ] Thermus aquaticus was injected directly into patients to diagnose diseases — its role in PCR is unrelated to the organism itself
    [*] PCR requires repeated cycles of heating to ~95°C (to denature DNA double strands), cooling to ~55°C (to anneal primers), and heating to ~72°C (for DNA synthesis) — Taq polymerase, evolved in T. aquaticus to function at high temperatures, survives the 95°C denaturation step without being destroyed (unlike mesophilic polymerases) → enables automation of multiple PCR cycles without adding fresh enzyme each time — this revolutionized genetics, forensics, diagnostics, and earned Kary Mullis the Nobel Prize in Chemistry (1993)
    [ ] Taq polymerase was artificially synthesized in a laboratory — no actual organism was involved in its discovery or production
    [ ] Thermus aquaticus is a eukaryotic organism with a nucleus — it was reclassified after Taq polymerase was discovered
    Explanation: Taq polymerase and PCR: (1) Thomas Brock (1966): discovered Thermus aquaticus in Yellowstone hot springs (Mushroom Spring, 70-80°C). Published in Science, 1969. (2) Kary Mullis (1983): conceived PCR — amplify specific DNA sequences through repeated denaturation → annealing → extension cycles. Original problem: DNA polymerase from E. coli (mesophile) denatured at 95°C → destroyed each cycle → required manual addition of fresh enzyme every cycle. (3) Solution: Taq polymerase (isolated from T. aquaticus) — optimum activity at 72°C, survives 95°C. Enabled automated thermal cycling (thermocycler). One enzyme addition → 30+ cycles → 2³⁰ = ~1 billion copies of target DNA. (4) Impact: forensic DNA fingerprinting (crime scenes — nanogram DNA samples), medical diagnostics (COVID-19 RT-PCR testing), gene cloning, genome sequencing, paternity testing, ancient DNA analysis, metagenomics. (5) Nobel Prize: Kary Mullis, Chemistry, 1993 ('for contributions to the developments of methods within DNA-based chemistry'). (6) Broader lesson: basic research on extremophilic prokaryotes (seemingly obscure) produced one of the most transformative tools in modern biology. Bioprospecting: searching extreme environments for useful enzymes from extremophilic bacteria and archaea. Other examples: restriction enzymes (from bacteria → genetic engineering), Taq rivals from archaea (Pfu polymerase from Pyrococcus furiosus — higher fidelity).

  Q24. A researcher discovers a new microorganism from a deep-sea hydrothermal vent at 110°C and pH 2. Analysis reveals: no nucleus, ether-linked membrane lipids, no peptidoglycan in the cell wall, and RNA polymerase resembling eukaryotic RNA Pol II. To which domain does this organism belong, and what evidence distinguishes it from bacteria?
    [ ] This organism is a bacterium — all prokaryotes belong to domain Bacteria, and the extreme environment is irrelevant to classification
    [*] This organism belongs to domain Archaea — key distinguishing features from Bacteria: (1) ether-linked isoprenoid lipids (vs. ester-linked fatty acids in bacteria — more thermostable); (2) no peptidoglycan (bacteria always have it); (3) eukaryote-like RNA polymerase (Archaea and Eukarya share more transcription machinery similarities than either shares with Bacteria); (4) extreme environment (110°C, pH 2) is typical of thermoacidophilic archaea — Carl Woese's 16S rRNA analysis demonstrated that Archaea are a separate domain, actually more closely related to Eukarya than to Bacteria despite both being prokaryotic
    [ ] This must be a eukaryote because it has eukaryote-like RNA polymerase — the lack of a nucleus is an error in the analysis
    [ ] It could be either Bacteria or Archaea — there is no reliable method to distinguish between the two domains
    Explanation: Archaeal identification: (1) Diagnostic features pointing to Archaea: no nucleus (prokaryotic) → Bacteria or Archaea. Ether-linked lipids: archaea have glycerol-1-phosphate backbone + isoprenoid chains via ether bonds (more heat-stable, resistant to hydrolysis). Bacteria: glycerol-3-phosphate + fatty acids via ester bonds. Different stereochemistry of glycerol backbone = independently evolved membrane chemistry. No peptidoglycan: eliminates Bacteria (all bacteria have peptidoglycan). Archaea use pseudopeptidoglycan, S-layers, or polysaccharide walls. Eukaryote-like transcription: archaeal RNA polymerase resembles eukaryotic Pol II (multiple subunits, TATA-binding protein, transcription factor B). Bacteria have simpler σ-factor-based RNA polymerase. Extreme environment: many (not all) archaea are extremophiles. Thermoacidophile examples: Sulfolobus (80°C, pH 2-3), Picrophilus (60°C, pH 0.06 — most acid-tolerant known organism). (2) Woese's revolution: before 1977, prokaryotes considered one group. 16S rRNA sequencing → revealed Archaea and Bacteria are as different from each other as either is from Eukarya. Three-domain system replaced Whittaker's five-kingdom classification. (3) Archaea are NOT 'just extreme bacteria': they are a fundamentally distinct lineage. Molecular phylogenetics consistently places Archaea closer to Eukarya (particularly in information-processing systems: DNA replication, transcription, translation).

  Q25. Scientists Jennifer Doudna and Emmanuelle Charpentier received the 2020 Nobel Prize in Chemistry for developing CRISPR-Cas9, a gene-editing tool derived from the bacterial immune system. How does the bacterial CRISPR system work as a defense mechanism, and how was it adapted for gene editing?
    [ ] CRISPR is a synthetic tool designed entirely by humans — it has no natural origin in bacteria or any biological function outside the laboratory
    [*] CRISPR (Clustered Regularly Interspaced Short Palindromic Repeats) is a prokaryotic adaptive immune system: when a bacteriophage infects a bacterium, the cell captures short fragments of viral DNA and inserts them as 'spacers' between CRISPR repeat sequences → these spacers serve as a genetic memory — upon reinfection, the spacer is transcribed into guide RNA (crRNA) that directs the Cas9 nuclease to the matching viral DNA → Cas9 makes a double-strand break → destroys the phage DNA — Doudna and Charpentier showed this system could be reprogrammed with synthetic guide RNAs to cut any DNA sequence at a desired location → revolutionary gene editing tool for medicine, agriculture, and research
    [ ] CRISPR was discovered in eukaryotic cells and later found in bacteria — it functions only as a DNA repair mechanism and has nothing to do with immune defense
    [ ] CRISPR only deletes genes — it cannot insert or modify DNA sequences, which severely limits its applications in medicine
    Explanation: CRISPR-Cas9: from bacterial immunity to gene editing: (1) Discovery: CRISPR arrays first noticed in E. coli (Ishino, 1987). Function as adaptive immune system discovered by Barrangou et al. (2007) in Streptococcus thermophilus (dairy bacterium). (2) Natural mechanism: Adaptation — bacterium captures short DNA fragment (~20 bp 'spacer') from invading phage → integrates into CRISPR array between repeat sequences → genetic memory. Expression — CRISPR array transcribed → processed into individual crRNAs (each containing one spacer). Interference — crRNA + tracrRNA (trans-activating) form dual-guide → direct Cas9 to complementary sequence on phage DNA → Cas9 makes double-strand break (DSB) → phage DNA destroyed. Requires PAM (protospacer adjacent motif, e.g., NGG for SpCas9) — prevents self-targeting. (3) Gene editing adaptation: Doudna & Charpentier (2012): fused crRNA + tracrRNA into single guide RNA (sgRNA). Any 20-nt guide sequence → directs Cas9 to cut any target DNA with matching PAM. After DSB: NHEJ (non-homologous end joining → indels → gene knockout) or HDR (homology-directed repair with template → precise gene editing). (4) Applications: sickle cell disease treatment (CASGEVY — first CRISPR therapy approved, 2023), cancer immunotherapy (CAR-T cell engineering), disease-resistant crops, malaria-resistant mosquitoes (gene drives), COVID-19 diagnostics, basic research. (5) Nobel Prize in Chemistry 2020: Doudna & Charpentier (first shared by two women).

  Q26. A patient develops a persistent catheter-associated urinary tract infection that resists multiple rounds of antibiotic treatment. Microscopy reveals bacteria embedded in a dense matrix of polysaccharides, proteins, and DNA on the catheter surface. What is this structure, and why does it make infections so difficult to treat?
    [ ] This is a large colony of free-floating (planktonic) bacteria — antibiotics should work normally if given at higher doses
    [*] This is a biofilm — a structured community of bacteria enclosed in a self-produced extracellular polymeric substance (EPS) matrix of polysaccharides, proteins, and extracellular DNA — biofilm bacteria are 100-1,000× more resistant to antibiotics than planktonic cells because: (1) the EPS matrix physically blocks antibiotic penetration, (2) bacteria deep in the biofilm have reduced metabolic activity (antibiotics target active processes), (3) persister cells (metabolically dormant) survive antibiotic exposure, and (4) proximity facilitates horizontal gene transfer of resistance genes via conjugation — biofilms cause ~80% of chronic bacterial infections
    [ ] The bacteria formed endospores — endospores are reproductive structures that allow bacteria to multiply faster on catheter surfaces
    [ ] This is a capsule surrounding a single large bacterium — only one cell is present, not a community
    Explanation: Biofilms — prokaryotic community living: (1) Structure: bacteria attach to surface → produce EPS (extracellular polymeric substance) matrix → form 3D architecture with water channels (nutrient delivery) → mature biofilm with microcolonies. EPS composition: polysaccharides, proteins, extracellular DNA (eDNA — structural scaffold). (2) Resistance mechanisms: physical barrier (EPS prevents antibiotic penetration — slow diffusion, adsorption, enzymatic degradation within matrix), metabolic heterogeneity (outer bacteria — active, susceptible; inner bacteria — nutrient-deprived, slow metabolism → antibiotics targeting DNA replication, protein synthesis, cell wall synthesis are less effective on non-growing cells), persister cells (stochastically dormant subpopulation — survive antibiotic exposure → regrow when antibiotic removed → relapsing infections), horizontal gene transfer (close cell proximity → conjugation rates up to 1,000× higher within biofilm → rapid spread of resistance genes). (3) Clinical significance: ~80% of chronic bacterial infections involve biofilms: catheter infections (UTI, central line), dental plaque (caries, periodontal disease), lung infections in cystic fibrosis (Pseudomonas aeruginosa), prosthetic joint/implant infections, chronic wounds, endocarditis (heart valve). Treatment: often requires physical removal (catheter removal/replacement) + high-dose combination antibiotic therapy. (4) Quorum sensing: bacteria in biofilms communicate via chemical signals (autoinducers) → coordinate gene expression as a community (quorum sensing — discovered by Bonnie Bassler et al.).

  Q27. Robert Koch (Nobel Prize 1905) established his famous postulates to prove that a specific microorganism causes a specific disease. He used these criteria to identify Mycobacterium tuberculosis as the causative agent of tuberculosis and Bacillus anthracis for anthrax. What are Koch's postulates, and what are their modern limitations?
    [ ] Koch's postulates are cooking guidelines for sterilizing laboratory equipment — they have no connection to disease causation
    [*] Koch's postulates (1884): (1) the microorganism must be found in all cases of the disease but absent in healthy individuals; (2) the microorganism must be isolated and grown in pure culture; (3) the pure culture must cause the disease when inoculated into a healthy susceptible host; (4) the same microorganism must be re-isolated from the experimentally infected host — limitations include: some pathogens cannot be cultured (M. leprae, T. pallidum), asymptomatic carriers exist (violates postulate 1), ethical constraints prevent inoculating humans, and some diseases require multiple microorganisms or host factors — modern 'molecular Koch's postulates' use gene knockout/complementation to link specific virulence genes to pathogenesis
    [ ] Koch's postulates state that all diseases are caused by viruses, not bacteria — Koch disproved the bacterial theory of disease
    [ ] Koch's postulates have been entirely replaced by modern genetics — no one uses them in any form today
    Explanation: Koch's postulates — foundation of medical microbiology: (1) Robert Koch (1843-1910): German physician. Identified causative agents of: anthrax (Bacillus anthracis, 1876), tuberculosis (Mycobacterium tuberculosis, 1882), cholera (Vibrio cholerae, 1883). Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine, 1905. (2) Postulates (formalized 1884): (a) Association: pathogen present in all disease cases, absent from healthy hosts. (b) Isolation: pathogen isolated from diseased host, grown in pure culture. (c) Causation: pure culture causes disease in new susceptible host. (d) Re-isolation: same pathogen re-isolated from experimentally infected host. (3) Limitations: unculturable pathogens (Mycobacterium leprae — causes leprosy — never grown in pure culture; Treponema pallidum — syphilis), asymptomatic carriers (Salmonella typhi — 'Typhoid Mary' — healthy carrier), ethical constraints (cannot inoculate humans with deadly pathogens), polymicrobial diseases (multiple organisms required), viral diseases (Koch worked before virology matured), host factors (genetic susceptibility, immune status). (4) Modern adaptations: molecular Koch's postulates (Stanley Falkow, 1988): (a) virulence gene present in pathogenic strains, absent from avirulent; (b) gene inactivation → loss of virulence; (c) restoration of gene → restores virulence. Used with: transposon mutagenesis, gene knockouts, complementation studies. (5) Legacy: Koch's work established germ theory on solid experimental ground and created the template for identifying causative agents of infectious diseases.

  Q28. Mycoplasma pneumoniae causes 'walking pneumonia' (mild respiratory infection). Unusually, this bacterium completely lacks a cell wall. A medical student prescribes penicillin, which inhibits peptidoglycan synthesis. Why will this treatment fail, and what alternative approach should be used?
    [ ] Penicillin will work fine because all bacteria have peptidoglycan — Mycoplasma is no exception despite claims to the contrary
    [*] Penicillin targets peptidoglycan synthesis (inhibits transpeptidase cross-linking) — since Mycoplasma completely lacks a cell wall and peptidoglycan, there is no target for penicillin to act on → the drug is inherently useless — Mycoplasma instead has a cholesterol-containing plasma membrane (unusual for bacteria) that provides osmotic stability — treatment requires antibiotics targeting other cellular processes: macrolides (azithromycin — inhibits 50S ribosomal subunit → blocks protein synthesis), tetracyclines (doxycycline — inhibits 30S ribosomal subunit), or fluoroquinolones (levofloxacin — inhibits DNA gyrase/topoisomerase IV)
    [ ] Mycoplasma has an extremely thick cell wall that penicillin cannot penetrate — a higher dose would eventually overcome this resistance
    [ ] Penicillin fails because Mycoplasma is actually a virus, not a bacterium — antibiotics don't work against viruses
    Explanation: Mycoplasma — the wall-less bacterium: (1) Class Mollicutes: smallest free-living organisms (~0.2-0.3 μm — near lower limit for independent life). Minimal genome (M. genitalium: ~580 kb, ~470 genes — used in synthetic biology, Craig Venter's minimal cell project JCVI-syn3.0). (2) No cell wall: completely lost peptidoglycan genes during reductive evolution. Consequently: resistant to ALL cell wall-targeting antibiotics (penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems, vancomycin). Cannot be classified by Gram staining (no peptidoglycan to retain crystal violet). Pleomorphic (variable shape — no rigid wall). (3) Membrane: unique among bacteria — contains cholesterol (incorporated from host environment) → stabilizes membrane without a wall. (4) Treatment: must target other bacterial processes: macrolides (azithromycin, clarithromycin — bind 50S ribosomal subunit → block translocation), tetracyclines (doxycycline — bind 30S → prevent aminoacyl-tRNA entry), fluoroquinolones (levofloxacin, moxifloxacin — inhibit DNA gyrase and topoisomerase IV → prevent DNA replication). (5) Clinical: M. pneumoniae — 'walking pneumonia' (mild, atypical — no lobar consolidation, gradual onset, dry cough). Most common in school-age children and young adults. M. genitalium — sexually transmitted infection. (6) Teaching point: drug target must exist in the pathogen for the antibiotic to work — fundamental principle of selective toxicity.

  Q29. Farmers have long observed that crops like soybeans, clover, and alfalfa (legumes) improve soil fertility when included in crop rotations. Scientists discovered that Rhizobium bacteria form root nodules on legume plants, converting atmospheric nitrogen (N₂) into ammonia (NH₃) through biological nitrogen fixation. How does this prokaryotic capability benefit agriculture and ecosystems?
    [ ] Rhizobium bacteria are parasites that damage legume roots — the observation that legumes improve soil fertility is incorrect and unrelated to bacteria
    [*] Nitrogen fixation by Rhizobium converts atmospheric N₂ (which plants cannot use) into ammonia (NH₃) / ammonium (NH₄⁺) — a biologically available nitrogen form — the bacteria reside in specialized root nodules where the plant provides carbon/energy sources and a low-oxygen environment (leghemoglobin, an oxygen-binding protein, protects the oxygen-sensitive nitrogenase enzyme) — in return, the bacteria supply usable nitrogen to the plant — this mutualistic symbiosis reduces the need for synthetic nitrogen fertilizers (Haber-Bosch process), and when legume crops are rotated with other crops, the nitrogen enriches the soil for subsequent plantings — globally, biological nitrogen fixation contributes ~100-140 million tons of fixed nitrogen per year to terrestrial ecosystems
    [ ] All bacteria fix nitrogen equally — Rhizobium is no more special than E. coli or any other bacterium in converting N₂ to ammonia
    [ ] Nitrogen fixation only occurs in eukaryotic plants — bacteria play no role in converting atmospheric nitrogen into biologically available forms
    Explanation: Biological nitrogen fixation — prokaryotic superpower: (1) The problem: N₂ makes up 78% of atmosphere but the N≡N triple bond is extremely stable (946 kJ/mol — one of the strongest bonds in nature) → plants and animals cannot break it → cannot use atmospheric N₂ directly. (2) Nitrogenase: exclusively prokaryotic enzyme (no eukaryote has it). Catalyzes: N₂ + 8H⁺ + 8e⁻ + 16 ATP → 2NH₃ + H₂ + 16 ADP + 16 Pi. Extremely oxygen-sensitive (Fe-S clusters and FeMo-cofactor destroyed by O₂). (3) Rhizobium-legume symbiosis: Rhizobium (and related genera: Bradyrhizobium, Sinorhizobium) colonize legume root hairs → induce root nodule formation (Nod factors → signal exchange between bacteria and plant). Inside nodules: bacteria differentiate into bacteroids. Plant produces leghemoglobin (analog of hemoglobin — binds O₂ → maintains low O₂ for nitrogenase while providing O₂ for bacterial aerobic respiration). Mutualism: plant provides carbon (photosynthate) → bacteria provide fixed nitrogen. (4) Agricultural significance: crop rotation with legumes adds ~50-200 kg N/hectare. Reduces dependency on synthetic fertilizers (Haber-Bosch process: Fritz Haber, Nobel Prize 1918 — industrial N₂ + 3H₂ → 2NH₃ at high temperature/pressure — consumes ~1-2% of world's energy supply). (5) Other nitrogen fixers: Azotobacter (free-living, aerobic), Clostridium (free-living, anaerobic), cyanobacteria (Anabaena in heterocysts — rice paddies). (6) Global nitrogen cycle: biological fixation contributes ~100-140 million tons fixed N/year — essential for all life (amino acids, nucleotides, ATP require nitrogen).

  Q30. Carl Woese's analysis of 16S ribosomal RNA sequences in the 1970s fundamentally changed biology by revealing that what were thought to be a single group of 'bacteria' actually comprised two profoundly different domains — Bacteria and Archaea. How did rRNA analysis reshape our understanding of the tree of life, and where does the Last Universal Common Ancestor (LUCA) fit in this picture?
    [ ] Woese's work was rejected by the scientific community and has no impact on modern classification — we still use Whittaker's five-kingdom system exclusively
    [*] Before Woese, all prokaryotes were grouped together as 'Monera' — 16S/18S rRNA analysis revealed three distinct domains: Bacteria, Archaea, and Eukarya — surprisingly, Archaea are MORE closely related to Eukarya than to Bacteria (sharing similar transcription/translation machinery) — all three domains likely diverged from a Last Universal Common Ancestor (LUCA), estimated to have lived ~3.5-4 billion years ago — LUCA was probably a prokaryote-like organism with DNA, RNA, ribosomes, and a plasma membrane — this three-domain tree fundamentally restructured our understanding of life's diversity and evolutionary history
    [ ] LUCA was a fully formed eukaryotic cell with a nucleus, mitochondria, and all organelles — prokaryotes evolved later when eukaryotes lost their organelles
    [ ] rRNA analysis showed that Bacteria and Archaea are identical — Woese's three-domain classification was disproven by later studies
    Explanation: Woese's revolution and LUCA: (1) Before Woese: Whittaker's five-kingdom system (1969): Monera (all prokaryotes), Protista, Fungi, Plantae, Animalia. All prokaryotes lumped as one group. (2) Carl Woese and George Fox (1977): used 16S rRNA (prokaryotes) and 18S rRNA (eukaryotes) as molecular chronometers (universal — present in all cells; functionally constrained — evolves slowly; contains both conserved and variable regions). Oligonucleotide cataloging → sequence comparison → phylogenetic trees revealed: prokaryotes are NOT monophyletic. Two distinct prokaryotic groups: Bacteria and 'Archaebacteria' (later renamed Archaea to emphasize their distinctness). (3) Three-domain system: Bacteria, Archaea, Eukarya. Key insight: Archaea and Eukarya share a more recent common ancestor than either shares with Bacteria. Evidence: archaeal RNA polymerase resembles eukaryotic Pol II (multi-subunit, TATA-binding protein), archaeal ribosomes respond to some eukaryotic inhibitors, histone-like proteins in archaea, similar DNA replication machinery. (4) LUCA (Last Universal Common Ancestor): lived ~3.5-4 BYA. Reconstruction from genes shared across all three domains: DNA-based genome, DNA replication machinery, RNA-based information transfer (transcription), ribosome-based translation (genetic code already established), plasma membrane. Likely lived near hydrothermal vents (thermophilic characteristics in deeply branching lineages). LUCA → divergence into Bacteria lineage and Archaea-Eukarya lineage. (5) Current debates: Eocyte hypothesis (eukaryotes arose FROM within Archaea, specifically Asgard archaea — Lokiarchaeota et al., discovered 2015 — rather than as a sister group). If confirmed, two-domain (Bacteria + Archaea-including-Eukarya) rather than three-domain. (6) Woese's legacy: his work was initially controversial/ignored (published in obscure journals). Now recognized as one of the most important biological discoveries of the 20th century.
